THE RED PAGE.
SPORT AND A SPECTACLE
A N exhibition of rough riding, an
Australian spectacle, an exhibition
of physical prowess and athletic art,
the spectacle we love. “ Muscle
and pluck for ever ! ” says Whitman. “ For
ever ! ” echoes Australia—a land devoted
to many aims and pursuits, but pre-
eminently to the pursuit of the Horse.
Little you know in the coastal cities,
little you know how hearts leap to horse-
flesh throughout N.S. Wales and Queens-
land at least, and through many a district
in the-other States. Can he ride? How
can he ride ? Can I outride him ? These
are the questions that decide many a man’s
fate on the stations and stock-routes where
still a substantial part of our country’s
wealth is won.
The horse can be used and raced in Sydney
and Melbourne ; but before he is ridden he
must be broken, and the breaker shines like a
star. Every station has its champion ; happy
the man accredited champion of a district,
able to tackle every outlaw that can be
drafted from the ropeable, rideable mobs.
At holiday gatherings, at pountry shows,
rough-riding is still “the sport of kings” of
the Bush. As you go into the Gulf country,
where wilder spirits and wilder horses abide,
breaking and riding become the regular Sun-
day pleasure ; and at every opportunity
horsemen will muster for scores of miles to
see a champion sit a champion.
Did you never hear of Jack Pendergast, a
Windsor native, thirty-nine years old, who
has done nothing fpr twenty-two years but
handle horses, breaking and bubkriduig < Yet
Pendergast is acclaimed champion buck-
rider of coastal N.S. Wales, and from
the Hawkesbury to the Tweed his
fame is greater than Napoleon’s Or have
you never heard of Willy Waite, of the Pro-
serpine, Q., exhibition rider with Martini, and
enthusiastically declared “the best
buckjump rider on the earth ! ”
“ That’s my opinion,” says Martini ; “ and
here’s my money to back it.” Have you
Beard of Harry Williams, •of Cloncurry ?
Jack Collins, of Harvest Home station, N.Q.?
Ralph Elliot, of Winton ? Have you never
heard of Jack MacGuire, of Cunnamulla ?
or Ken. McPherson, of the Richmond ?
or Ted Holery, of Toowoomba ? of Joe
Thomas or Jack Phipps ? It is these and
a thousand others who are the heroes of the
Bush, by virtue of “ muscle and pluck.”
J*
The spectacle is Martini’s buckjumping
show, which started from Sydney three years
ago with one small tent and one small waggon,
and, after touring N.S.W. ami Queensland,
has returned to Sydney with forty horses,
ponies, donkeys, mules, jennets, and asses,
and impedimenta to load a
of the appreciative Bush. In Sydney the
show has found, bounded by brick and
mortar, an arena which suits it admirably.
The spectacle is open to the sky. It is a
true Australian theatre, after the model of
Greece. When Summer’s with Australia,
to sit in stench in a suffocating
what folly in the name of pleasure ! Surely
in clean air under the stars, on a fine Aus-
tralian night, the dramatic and spectacular
entertainments of our civilised future will
be given.
To-night there is a crowded, eager audi-
ence ; for the city lads are sportsmen too,
and many a good horseman lias been bred in
a Sydney suburb'. The evening’s pro-
gramme, diversified with gymnastics and
circus acting, has reached its climax,
and under eight electric lamps is led forward
the heroic Bobs—with the exception of
Dargin’s Grey most celebrated of buck-
jumpers. To Dargin’s Grey belongs the
glory of the past, to Bobs the acclamations of
the future ; for youth will be served. Both
are natural buckers, and these prodigies of
the Australian animal creation grow every
year scarcer. All the outlaws went to
astonish British Yeomanry in the African
war; and even the brumbies are civilised
nowadays.
But Dargin's Grey ! The blood of a
bush generation tingles to his name.
He was bred nearly twenty years ago
by W. Tindall, of Capertee, near Lith-
gow, N.S.W.—a grey gelding branded TJon
near shoulder ; „and lie has been bucking
ever since. Hundreds of crack Australian
horsemen have bestridden him ; and though
some have tamed, none has ever broken his
rebellious spirit. His early history, like that
of all heroes, is shrouded in legend. The
Hawkesbury Herald of 3rd November last, in a
loving account of his exploits, brings him into
the dry light of journalism as the property
of W. Gardiner, of Wallerawang, in the early
nineties. Transferred to Arthur Dargin,' he
gained a master and a name. Exhibited at
Litligow show, he bucked across a carriage
and pair. In The Herald's account, the
names of riders thrown by the grey horse
are thick as thistles. Peter Molloy, of
.Wellington, is credited with sticking to his
seat; so is Jack Pendergast; so, too, is
Harry Morant, “The Eieaker ” par Emi-
nence ; and doubtless there were others.
“For,” says Martini, “Australian riders
will put any horse down ; I don’t care what
it is. If there's a mob of them and they
determine to get his scalp, they’ll come at
him one after the other. He may down
thirty of them, but the thirty-first will down
him. They ’re bound to have his scalp. ” The
old horse was slightly hurt at Toowoomba,
and is having a spoil. He twisted himself,
came down, and smashed his near hind
fetlock ; you could hear the crack. But still
he bucked away on three legs—a very Wid-
drington of horseflesh—bucked until he threw
Ted Hunt, who was riding him. “Why,”
says Pendergast, “I believe that if the grey
was lying down at his last gasp and anybody
got on him, he’d get up and buck himself to
death ! ”
&
Bobs was bred at Byrimine station, N.Q.
—a light bay geldi.tg nearly 17 hands high,
branded ST4 on off shoulder. Sawtell of
Byrimine sent him in 75 miles to Cloncurry
to see if anyone in Martini’s show.could ride
him. “I didn’t know r what we’d struck,”
says Mart. ; “so I said Yes.” But Willy
Waite talks of Bobs with serious respect.
“ He is n’t like any other horse I ever rode.
It is n’t the come-back or the rolling buck,
or the way he jerks one way with his
forequarters and another way with
his hindquarters, but everything together.
And he has such a lift—he rises like
a jumper; it’s like riding a spring-
board. And "he comes down with his
legs solid like bars of steel.” Waite adds :
“ It’s heart a rider wants: —and head as
well. If I ride it’s because I’m not fright-
ened. of a horse. I ’ll get on anything ; and
if it throws me I don’t care. Because I ’ve
been riding all my life.” Willy is a half-
blood, a tall, jolly-faced chap with a 44-inch
chest, splendid thigh and calf muscles, and
good jaw and chin. “ I like to know my
horse and keep a little ahead of him—like
to know what he’s going to do before he
does it. But Bobs is different from other
horses. There’s only one Bobs. People
sing out to you, ‘ Watch his head ! ’ —but
that’s no use at all.” And if you want a
testimonial to Willy Waite as a rider, ask
those A 1 judges, the Misses Armstrong of
Disputed Plains, N.S.W.
Martini makes his challenge to the crowd
under the stars. “ £lOO to anyone who will
sit Bobs for five minutes ; £2 to sit him for
two minutes ; same conditions as our own
men.” That means a hunting saddle and a
thin cord tied round the loins. “ The loin-
cord doesn’t hurt him,” says Martini. “ It’s
just to make sure we ’ll have a go for our
money. Because a rider for money will
mount without whip or spurs and sit as quiet
as the horse will let him.” And Jack Pen-
dergast, to whom Bobs has brought strong
conviction of his merits, adds: “I’ll bet
£5O to £1 that nobody can ride him—and
put down the sugar ! ” Up springs O’Neill,
a light-weight jockey from Maoriland : “I ’ll
take that! ” There is a slight difficulty about
“the sugar.” Pendergast explains that a
man does n’t carry £5O in his pockets, and
the crowd retorts that a man should n’t talk
without acting.
Presently a bet is arranged—£3o to 10s.
“Who’ll hold the stakes?” says Martini.
The crowd offers various persons fit for the
responsibility, and is silenced by a decided
voice: “ Here’s Paddy Crick ! ” Then that
jovial, hard-faced citizen, out on bail on
certain grave charges, comes forward to the
ropes and takes charge of the money. .Every-
body is content. Everybody feels that what-
ever becomes of the grave charges Paddy
Crick would die at the ropes in defence of
£3O 10s. entrusted to him in the way of
sport. ,
Presently Bobs, rearing away gaily, is re-
duced to submission by a twitch on his ear.
Saddle and cord are adjusted, and O’Neill
mounts with gay Bush gallantry : ‘ 1 Let him
go ! he’s mine for ever ! ” Bobs bucks twice,
wriggles once, and O’Neill comes o£F—quite
satisfied, thank you. Jack Pendergast is
also quite satisfied. Bobs threw Jack
Pendergast at Lismore, and at once won
Jack’s whole-souled admiration of his talent.
“ I held him for two forward bucks,” says
Jack; “then he bucked back, jerked his
shoulders—and threw me up and away 1
Something like a horse ! While I stuck on
him he landed so hard that it felt as if a
flock of scrub turkeys was running up my
backbone—right up my fleck ! ’’
Then Willy Waite mounts, and Bobs per-
forms. Willy sits him for half a minute and
is thrown heavily. But Willy has been
thrown so many times that he has acquired
the art of falling like an acrobat. “ Ladies
and gentlemen,” says Martini, “that con-
cludes this evening’s entertainment.”
J* J*
Martini says: “My name is Martin
Breheny. I was bom at Araluen, N.S.W.,
in 1868. Had a bit of gymnastic practice as
a nipper in Sydney; then picked up with
Harmston’s Circus and Wild West Show;
and was with them for years as an all-round
athlete. I struck Melbourne with Harms-
ton’s in Martini-Henry’s year, and the boys
thought I was so smart on the bars (my
speciality is bar-work) that they said, “Why,
he’s a regular Martini! ” And the name
has stuck to me ever since.
“ Look here, I’ll tell you a funny thing.
I could always get more money from an
American show than- from an Australian.
The Australian man would say “Oh, you
were born here. You can’t be much good.”
The American would say, I guess that act
is worth so much to me ; and I don’t care
where you come from,” —and pay on the
merits. When I left Sydney three years ago
with a small side show, we just struggled
along. At Parramatta the van-man thought
I was going to balance him—swindle him out
of his money; and when I paid up he said,
“ Look here I I didn’t expect this I’ll tell
you where there ’sa horse you ought to get-
up at Lithgow, owned by Jones, of the Zigzag
Brewery—that’s Dargin’s Grey.” Well, we
went up to Lithgow, and I arranged to get
the horse for one night for £5. If the chap
had asked for the money in advance
we were done, because I hadn’t got
it; but we took £lO, and after the show I
gave him his £5, and he was so pleased that
he said, “ Look here, you can have the horse
on Monday night for nothing.” A few days
after that I bought him for £B. Since then
I’ve walked him overland right up to Port
Douglas, then across to Normanton. Then
by sea to Thursday Island, and down again to
Sydney here—picking up buckers all the
way.
“ Look here, this last three years I’ve seen
the best bucking horses and the best riders
in the world, and I ’ll tell you. It’s prac-
tice makes perfect. A little man can
ride as . well as a big man : you watch
George Hoskins to-night. He isn’t a
little man, but he’s not long-legged,
and that’s what' you mean. A balance
rider does n’t use his legs to grip his
horse all the time: he’s just moving to
meet him—playing him as you might play a
fish; and gripping him with all his power
for the moment that the grip is needed. A
man ought to be able to ride a buck jumper
quite well at fifty if he’s a balance rider : it
doesn’t do him a bit of harm, because he’s
always going with the horse. Riding loose,
you ’re all over the horse, onetime on his neck,
another on his rump ; but you ’re always on
him. If you've got a stiff seat, once he
shifts you he shifts you for good. It’s just
like me handling my gymnastic bars : at one
time you hang on with all your power, at
others you ’re just touching lightly.
“ Make no mistake, Bobs iB a wonder.
That’s not my opinion only ; it’s the opinion
of real good judges—men like Dan O’Leary,
Of Hughenden ; and J. S. Love, of Towns-
ville, the Indian horse-buyer; and Mr.
Carter, of Richmond, W.Q. And he goes so
high that he pitches most riders a somer-
sault—throws them clean over. My chal-
lenge is open to anybody to try. But
the horse must have a fair show. Once I
made my challenge and a voice sang out,
‘ Fetch him up here, boss, and I ’ll ride
him ! ’ I could n’t make out where it came
from. Then I looked, and there was a black-
fellow perched right up a big tree. Well,
we didn’t take that on.”

Some Blake stories get themselves reprinted
It was at Felpham Blake once asked a lady if she had
ever seen a fairy’s funeral. The lady replied that she had
not. “ I have, but not before last night,” Blake con-
tinued. “ I was walking alone in my garden ; there was
a great stillness among the branches and flowers, and
more than common sweetness in the air; I heard a low
and pleasant sound, and I knew not whence it came. At
last I saw the broad leaf of a flower move, and underneath
I saw a procession of creatures, of the size and colour of
green and grey grasshoppers, bearing a body laid out on
a roseleaf, which they buried with songs and then dis-
appeared. It was a fairy funeral I ”
One day a drunken soldier, a muscular brute, broke
into the tiny slip of garden in front of Blake’s house.
The poet, although short in stature, was full of a frenzied
courage. Exasperated at the intrusion, he laid hands on
the King’s uniform and unceremoniously ejected the sur-
prised and frightened soldier, such was his passionate
wrath ! “ I do not know how I did it, but I did it,” he re-
marked afterwards, appearing to ascribe his feat tq
spiritual determination, which he firmly believed over-
came muscular limitations. In the course of the en-
counter the red-coat blustered about being the King’s
soldier. ‘ ‘ Damn the King and you too ! ” was the forcible,
if disloyal, rejoinder.
Perhaps the best-known story of the author of Songs
of Innocence and Experience is the alleged Return to
Nature. Mr. Butts, one of his best friends and a constant
patron, found Blake and his wife sitting in the summer
house “untramn fled by those troublesome devices”
which were the subjects of Carlyle’s satire. “ Come in,”
cried Blake, “it is only Adam and Eve, you know.” On
entering he found husband and wife reciting passages
from “Paradise Lost” in character—“l had almost
written ‘in costume,’” says Herbert Ives, who tells the
story anew.
RECENT BOOKS
A former friend of Oscar Wilde’s, J. M.
Stuart-Young, meditating the Muse (not, it
appeals, the ; UfAniari) ih West Africa, publishes
Osrac , the Self-Sufficient (Lond., Greening; 10s. 6d.)
—a handsomely printed book of verses and
recollections that twine round Wilde and Stuart-
Young, whose portraits have equal illustrative
prominence. The original verses are clever boy’s
rhymes and little more, though the eye .lights
upon one quatrain : '
God, for this world bright and gay,
I in Thy Mansion of Time
Lovingly, reverently pay
Eent with the run of a rhyme.
There are a few new fragments of Wilde:
I greatly admire Arthur Symons’s work. He feels what
he writes. But he is occasionally too sickly. He pants
uporl the breasts of his loves in a laboured effort to feel.
He urges his passion, instead of permitting his passion to
surge within him.
No marriage has been contracted yet where the man
was n’t the loser ; and if he be gifted, the heavier must
be the toll be pays to Eros. . . . And I love children too.
After all' the common drab necessity of propagation... .
To George Moore Wilde was sympathetic; but
to Moore’s realistic method he was antipathetio :
George Moore’s novels are unbearable to a sensitive
spirit. I always think of a grocer temporarily installed
in the place of an artist. . . . The grocer sits et the desk
of the artist and pens modern stories of modernjftve : all
impregnated with the suburban outlook and smeared with
the grease of “ our best shilling per pound."
This is rather hard on the denunciator of the
Brixton Empire.
Between Henry James and Edith Wharton
there is only sex to choose. James’s work is
just a trifle tenser and intenser, his phrasing just
a trifle brighter, his sense of the shades of an idea
just a little more acute. Mrs. Wharton is now in
her maturity of womanhood and literary power,
and The House of Mirth, her new book (Lond.,
Macmillan; 2s. 6d.) is a solid performance in
fiction that mirrors truth. The personages move
in to-day’s New York society. The story relates
the tragedy of a “society girl” whose hard polish
has*a fatal flaw of sentiment. Her duty to make
a “good,” unpalatable marriage is thwarted by
the romantic imp that now and again besets her ;
and presently her life becomes so difficult that
an accidental overdose of. chloral, cutting the
knot, is merciful maybe. The characters are
closely studied and well displayed, and the
book repays reading and suggests meditation.
The author is not without illuminating passages
that are almost epigrammatic. Some fragments:
The spot was charming, and Lily was not insensible to
the charm,,or to the fact that her presence enhanced it;
hut she was not accustomed to taste the joys of solitude
except in company, and the combination of a handsome
girl and a romantic scene struck her as too good to be
wasted. No one, however, appeared to profit by the
opportunity ; and after a half-hour of fruitless waiting
she rose and wandered on.
... the Stoic’s carelessness of material things, combined
with the Epicurean’s pleasure in them. Life shorn of
either feeling appeared to him a diminished thing ; and
nowhere was the blending of the two ingredients so
essential as in the character of a pretty woman.
It is surprising how little narrow walls and a low ceil-
ing matter, when the roof of the soul has suddenly been
raised.
Now that women have taken to tobacco, we live in a
bath of nicotine. It would be a curious thing to study
the effect of cigarettes on the relation of the sexes.
Smoke is almost as great a solvent as divorce : both tend
to obscure the moral issue.
Jt
One’s inability to read Ayesha, by H. R. Hag-
gard (Lond., Ward Lock ; 3s. 6<£.), snows that the
away of fashion in novels is no imaginary thing.
To be sure* Haggard writes in an elder manner :
be has lost elasticity, and wastes too many words
on his incidents ; yet this is not all.
The little light that always shone upon Ayesha’s brow ;
the wide-set, maddening eyes which were filled sometimes
with the fire of the stars, and sometimes with the dark-
ness of the heavens wherein they float ; the curved lips ‘
so wistful yet so bright; the tresses fine as glossy silk
that still spread and rippled as though with a separate
life...
These things might have interested us in She.
That they excite only mild distaste in Ayesha musfc,
be set down to the fact that crinolines and
mystic adventures have lost seduction. Doubt-
less new life will again breathe in the old
forms, but the years of oblivion are not yet
passed. If it be hinted ' that the reason
is subjective...but no ! last week the critic-
burned midnight gas in—guess l—The Arabian
Nights. Somehow one thinks that the publishers
have realised a shortcoming, or they would not
have given the book so many brilliant illustrations
by such an expensive man as Maurice Greiffen-
hagen. Now here is ah illustrator! a master!
whose work is a delight. If Ward, Lock & Co.
will employ him to visit Australia and illustrate
Ethel Turner’s books and others, we may forgive
some of their curious commercial idiosyncrasies.
♦
E. J. Forbes, Sydney, publishes The Art Reader
(4s. 6d.), “a series of readings on art, illustrated
by eighty reproductions of famous masterpieces
in painting, sculpture, and architecture, specially
prepared for use in schools as a supplementary
reading book.” This well-printed little book is
written and compiled by P. E. Quinn, who explains
that it is in no sense a text-book, and the technical
treatment of art and architecture has not been
attempted. The lessons are explanatory and histo-
rical, rather than critical. The object of the book is
to stimulate elder children’s interest in art; and the
plan takes Egyptian monuments, Greek buildings
and sculptures, Roman architecture, pictures by
the old masters, and a selection of famous works
by modern artists, sketching their history and
character, and giving some admirable illustrations
to point the lesson. Within its limitations the
work has been very carefully done, and Mr.
Quinn deserves credit for the manner in which be
has accomplished a difficult task.
A.G.S.

Indigestion & Rheumatism.
A THOROUGH CURE.
The Case of
Mr. J. TYE.
(By a Special .Reporter.)
The public will be interested to
learn how it came about that Mr.
Jacob Tye threw off a serious illness
that had overtaken him, and full de
tails of the fortunate event will be
found in the following - conversation,
which took place at his residence, Wo.
19 Victoria-street, Alexandria :
Mr. Tye : “Two years last month 1
was laid up with rheumatism and in-
digestion, and if over a man felt that
his term on this planet was just about
ended it was I. Such acute pains as
those which had entered my shoulders
and arms you could not imagine, and
1 felt so stiff and sore that it took
me all my time to get out of bed in
the morning, let alone work.”
Reporter: “Had you been suffering
from those complaints for any length
of time ?”
Mr. Tye : “Yes ; far too long for my
liking, and I earnestly hope that 1
shall never have such experiences
again. In one way I would rather be
dead than go through the same
agony, for it was really too dreadful
for words. I tried all" sorts of lini-
ments for the rheumatism, but even
temporary relief did not come from
them ; and as for taking medicine in-
ternally —well, it was not my fault
that 1 did not get better, for 1 took
plenty of it. lor months my diges-
tive organs wore on strike—wouldn’t
do their work at all, in fact—and
there is no doubt that the nossibilitv
of getting well again seemed very re-
mote. It was an extraordinary thing
if for half a day my head was not
aching and I do not think anybody
could be more troubled with giddiness
than I was. It was a peculiar feeling,
as if I was on the point of fainting,
and what also assisted in adding to
my physical troubles was a sensation
in my chest as if the breathing tubes
were clogged up. Of a morning the
taste I had in my mouth was enough
to turn one sick, but I had enough of
that as it was, for after every meal I
vomited the food up again almost di-
rectly, and getting as thin as 1 did
was only a natural consequonco.”
Reporter : “Perhaps you ate too
heartily, and took more food than the
weakened condition of your stomach
could withstand ?”
Mr. Tye : “There was no fear of that
for my appetite was too poor. hJo ; if
1 only took a couple of biscuits I
would vomit them up again, so you
can see what a critical state 1 was in.
One day I was seized with cramps all
over me, and I had to come home and
lie up for a couple of weeks. Oh, 1
thought I was done for then, and the
way my loins used to ache was some-
thing fearful. 1 was so restless that- I
could not get half as - much sleep as I
wanted—just dozing off in fits and
starts like—and I never slept but what
1 had dreams of a most repulsive char-
acter ; that my nerves were all broken
down was quite evident from the way
in which 1 tised to tremble if anything
out of the way occurred, and I became
the victim of an uncontrollable cranki-
ness that used to arise on the least
provocation. Truly, I got excited and
bad tempered sometimes for nothing,
although I tried hard enough not to,
and the longer I suffered the more dis-
mal in mind I became. I felt that 1
had no desire for anything—no amuse-
ment, no work, no company—nothing
at all, in fact, except to lie down and
remain as quiet as possiblo. Many a
day 1 came home, with a sinking feel-
ing, which induced me to say tliat I
wanted some nourishment, but directly
it was _ placed before me I’d turn
against it. But there was something
elsie that worried me a great deal.”
Reporter: “Yes; what was that’”
Mr. Tyo : “My heart, for the way it
palpitated, especially if I had been ex-
erting myself in the least, was some-
thing- terrible, and when I retired at
night, after lying down a little while
it would start fluttering in a most dis-
tressing manner. Naturally enough L
thought l had some form of heart dis-
ease. but soon after accepting a
friend's- advice, and starting on- a
course of Clements Tonic, all doubts
on that question were settled, for the
heart’s action became as regular as it
could be. That was a great, relief to
my mind, 1 can tell you, and didn t 1
feel delighted to know that at last I
had got a remedy that was really able
to do me some good ? Don't for a mo-
ment think that 1 am overdrawing my
story, as 1 have no reason lor doing
so, and it is nothing but the plain
truth when 1 tell you that Clements
ionic actually saved my life, for when
l started on it 1 was almost too weak
to move, and I bad certainly nothing
to expect from any other medicine.
Nothing I had hitherto tried could
lessen in the slightest degree my rheu-
matic pains, but. I can honestly say
that Clements Tonic gave me relief in
a few weeks, and by continuing with
the same remedy 1 was freed from
those torments altogether. Oh, how
thankful 1 was for all that, yet other
benctits still awaited me."
Reporter : ‘‘ln respect to your di-
1 suppose V"
Mr. Tye : "In every possible way l
benefited by the use of Clements Tonic,
and I reckon there is nothing in. crea-
tion to equal it for giving an appetite
to a sick person. Why, Clements Tonic
made me eat so that 1 never felt satis-
fied, and it was a healthy appetite,
too, for J. never felt uncomfortable af-
ter eating. And weren’t my people sur-
prised at the way i was coming round
again—right back from the grave. I
might say—and what a treat it was
tp feel some vigour and strength run-
ning through my frame once more. My
nerves were settled and invigorated to
a remarkable degree, and it was not
long before 1 ceased turning about at.
nights instead of sleeping soundly, and
my sleep was not marked by frightful
dreams as was previously tho case. No;
in every way 1 had improved beyond’
recognition, my sunken cheeks having
filled out., and there was an air of ac-
tivity about mo that had been lacking
for months past. Constipation had
troubled me considerably, but by tak-
ing Di\ Fletcher’s pills with the Cle-
ments Tonic I soon got over that dif-
ficulty permanently, and from the day
1 was cured over two years ago my
health has been as sound' as a boll. J.
ought to make special mention of tho
promptitude with which Clements To-
nic stopped the vomiting after meals,
and when that was done 1 made
wholesale progress, and soon lost
every pain and ache.”
Reporter: "Well, if you are agree-
able your narrative will appear in the
papers
. Mr. Tye : "And I trust every sufferer
in the land may read it. Certainly,
publish tho facts I’ve given you how
and when you please.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dreamland.
A golden river’s flowing to the realms of sleep and
dreams,
A myriad stars are showing in its tributary
streams;
Through all the night unwaking, light fairy spells
unbreaking,
Are holding me in triumph, and a blissful essence
seems
To be lifting and upholding, and my burning
brain enfolding.
In a calm embrace and soothing, whilst soft fairy
hands are smoothing
From out my wearied forehead all the wrinkles
that are there.
In enchanted woods around floats a melody of
sound,
And the echoing air is ringing with a sweet and
subtle singing,
Till the waves of song are bringing quiv’ring
leaflets to the ground.
From branch to treetop fluttering, chirping birds
are ever uttering
Notes that swell the harmony ;
Singing birds all golden-breasted, golden-winged
and golden-crested ;
Flowers blooming flood the air with a perfume
sweet as rare.
Here I wander in my dreams, through this land of
limpid streams,
Mystic land where peace holds sway, where is
neither night nor day,
Disappointment nor distress, care nor other
bitterness;
Passion has no dwelling here, love nor hatred,
hope nor fear;
Jar of contest is not heard, strife of deed nor war
of word,
But—as round the senseless dead —all-pervading
peace is spread.
Joy were mine could I remain, free from turmoil,
fret, and strain.
Here, where only visions reign. Woe is me ! I
may not stay.
In the east the dawn is breaking, and the stars
the sky forsaking,
Fly before the face of day.
N.s.w.
EDGAR HEANE HARRIS.
The Artist.
I do not care how slowly I proceed,
Or even whether I proceed at all,
So long as youth gains not the veteran’s mead,
Nor dreams of brilliant pasts hold age in thrall.
What hell ’twould be to spur a flagging brain
Whence old ideas trickle faint and slow!
What hell to sweat like stokers to attain
The height we soared to, birdlike, years ago!
I dread that moment when my magic flies,
And when —my work grown placid, frail as air—•
I see contemptuous thoughts in people’s eyes,
That cursed compassion bids them not declare.
Be triumph my reward, I hope it waits
Till my last years ; I dread that brilliant past 1
Joyance will light the rudimentary states
If God will let me do my best things last.
VIO.
FURNLEY MAURICE.
Along the Huon.
All along the Huon River fields lie fair and wide,
Sweeping curves of orchard lands spread white on
either side,
Dreamy blue of distant hills, plain and valley
fair,
And the red-gold wattle blossom haunts the air.
Roses by the gateways and poplars by the wall,
Grey lights merging into green ’mid shimmering
gum-trees tall.
Sure, the seed is on the grass where the reed-beds
bow
Down along the Huon River now.
Flashing sunlight on the stream, and glamor of
old ways,
Fringed dogwoods by the road, and tangled,
musky maze,
Where, in gullies deep with fern, magpies carol
iong
Vesper hymn and joyous matin song.
Paddocks cream with clover bloom are fresh from
flying rain,
Best balm for a wounded heart, heal for hurt and
pain,
Breezes cool for aching head, breath of Heaven
sweet,
Soft green ways for weary, aching feet.
All along the Huon River dandelions blaze,
Painting all the earth with gold in sweet Sep-
tember days;
Where the grasses flutter, for the bees are droning
low
Fragments of lost day-dreams long ago.
All along the Huon when the purple shadows
fall
Spreads the moonlight’s silver veil faintly over
all,
Glitters back the drowsy stream, drifting white
and wide,
A million flashing jewels on its tide.
All the world wore tints of rose, Time swept
swiftly past,
In those merry, merry days, far too sweet to last.
We, the exiled weary ones, fain their joy would
know
Down along the Huon, long ago.
vio.
KATHLEEN WALKER.

Correspondents to Tiib Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should, in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 4, 1906.
THE GAINS OF N.S.W. UNDER
FEDERATION.
A noisy section of the old anti-Federal party has
lately raised its objectionable head in and around
Sydney?’and is talking feeble, bumptious nonsense
about the alleged calamitous position of N.S.W.
under Fedferation. Not daring to label itself the
anti-Federal party—the old, roaring Cabbage
Tree Mob —this gathering professes to be strongly
Federal at heart but driven to desperation and
righteous anger by the “ wrongs ” heaped on the
stomach of its prostrate State. Then, as a matter
of course, it has to invent the wrongs, and in this
respect, though it reveals very moderate in-
genuity, it shows an amazing power of lying and
a vast 'unscrupulousness which almost calls for
admiration, and which certainly calls for six
months* hard labor. Among the various per-
versions which it has published at various
times are • the statements that N.S.W. re-
venue goes to support the other States;
that the N.S.W. patent records have been
taken to Melbourne ; that N.S.W. has been robbed
of its postal revenue, its defences, and its property
generally ; that N.S.W. was a very rich State
before it became part of the Federation in 1901,
and that it is now a very poor one ; and the rest
is mainly vague whirling charges of highway
robbery, burglary, house-breaking, arson, bigamy,
infidelity, and piracy on the high seas. One wild
Sydney Telegraph correspondent actually put
his name, the other day, to a letter in which he
accused the Commonwealth of robbing N.S.W. of
its water ; but his case can only, in charity for an
afflicted brother, be set down to madness. Still,
in view of the fact that even the most hopeless
and incredible lie may find believers if repeated
often enough, and that Dowieism, Mormonism,
the Tichborne claimant, and the “ wrongs of
N.S.W.” all have their believers, it is not amiss to
consider exactly what has happened in the way of
gain or loss to N.S.W. during the first years of
Federation.
• • « •
In the last years of the separate State system
N.S.W. was a desperately poor country. All
through the last ten years of provincialism immi-
gration was so utterly dead, and N.S.W. was so
good a place to get away from, that, according to
the latest published figures of its own State Statis-
tician, the departures exceeded the arrivals by
7904—an average loss of 790 people per annum.
That was up to the end of 1901, when the Federal
tariff came into effect. In 1902-3-4 (again accord-
ing to its own Statistician’s figures) N.S.W. under
Federation gained 18,037 people by excess of arri-
vals over departures—a gain of over 6000 immi-
grants a year. The State’s total increase of popu-
lation (by immigration and by excess of births over
deaths) varied in the last years of the old separate
tariffs from 23,020 in 1895 to 14,941 in 1901—the last
year of the old tariff. In 1902 the increase rose to
28,090; in 1903 it was 24,008 ;in 1904 it was 29,904.
Roughly speaking the growth of population Was
at once accelerated by about 40 per cent. But
while N.S.W. boomed, some of the other States
struck disaster. The Federal tariff was an in-
creased tariff as regards N.S.W., and N.S.W.’s
population began to expand by leaps and bounds.
It was a diminished tariff as regards the other
States and they suffered. Here are a few in-
stances :
Increases of Population.
1901. 1904.
N.S. Wales 14,941 29,904
Victoria 13,763 1,445
Queensland .. .. 12,830 6,125
Tasmania 1,400 716
New South Wales profited, while most of the
other States lost—though anyone who listens to
the wails of the Cabbage Tree Mob of Sydney
might think the facts were the other way about.
* * * »
The Federal tariff brought to Victoria, South
Australia, Tasmania and Queensland a grievous
diminution of revenue and great temporary
financial embarrassment, in add.tion to checking
the growth of population in three of those States.
To N.S.W., however, the tariff brought a glorious
increase of revenue which N.S.W. has spent
royally—on itself. It was Federation, with its
tariff, its increase of revenue and its boom, which
made the payment of old age pensions possible in
N.S.W. That State passed its Old Age Pension
law a few days before the Commonwealth started
business, and trusted wholly to the Federal tariff
which was to come to provide the money (about
£500,000 a year). It made not the slightest
effort to find the money itself, and it only
began to pay the old age pensions when the
new Federal tariff revenue commenced to
roll in. The N.S.W. State Government took
vast credit to itself for paying these pensions,
while the courage of the Federal Parliament in
finding the money was denounced by the Cabbage-
Tree Mob of Sydney as tyranny, extortion, and a
callous “ heaping of burdens on the backs of the
people.” Yet the N.S.W. State Parliament
plainly reckoned and expected and hoped for this
same extortion, and this same heaping of burdens
on the people, for it had arranged to spend
the proceeds of the alleged “iniquity” even
before the “iniquity” arrived. The mob that
demands secession and a return to the old State
tariff proposes, of course, to abolish the Old Age
Pension system as well as to abolish the system
which finds the money for the pensions.
* * * *
Under Federation and the Federal tariff and
the Federal policy the population of N.S.W. has
increased in the last four years, by 96,943,
though these years included the bitterest
period of the drought. The six years before
that showed just about the same increase. Under
Federation the shipping going into and out of
N.S.W. ports has grown by a little over 1,000,000
tons, which does not seem wholly like ruin. The
total trade of N.S.W. has grown in the same
brief period by rather over £4,500,000—in other
words, there was as great an increase in the last four
years under Federation as there was in the pre-
vious 10 under Separatism, and 1904 was the best
year for commerce that N.S.W. has ever seen.
The N.S.W. postal and telegraph department,
which hardly paid for its own axle-grease until
1897, and which, in the very best year under
Separatism,. only yielded £07,i13 over and above
bare working expenses (£67,113, that is, towards
interest on the money borrowed to build post-
offices and telegraph lines) has improved steadily
under Federal management until in 1904 it paid
N.S.W. £85,546 —this despite the fact that
the department now pays everything out of
revenue instead of charging many of its expenses
to loans as it did under State control. N.S.W.
manufactures have not grown as they should have
done, and as they would have done under a better
tariff ; but inefficient as the tariff is, it is better
than anything N.S.W. ever had before, and
under it N.S.W. had, at the end of 1904, 68,036
“hands” employed in its factories —an increase
of 7251 employees since the last year of Separatism.
This may not be much of an increase, but at least
it represents the high water mark of N.S.W.
manufactures up till now.
*■* '**
The value of property in N.S.W. municipalities
lias increased by almost exactly £16,000,000 during
the brief period of Federation; the last few
years of Separatism had seen it decrease
by something like £30,000,000. There is more
coin in the N.S.W. banks now than there ever
was before; the increase since the beginning of
Federation has been not far short of £2,000,000.
Almost every kind and variety of stocks and
shares have gone up during the Federal period,
as they had never previously done since the begin-
ning of the ’9o’s, and there has been a memorable
expansion in banking and company profits. Even
the long unsaleable Sydney suburban allotment,
after being dead for a dozen years or so, has come
into demand again, and the boss of the Sydney
Labor Bureau had the joyous effrontery to issue a
Christmas report whose tenor was that the work-
less class had been, for the moment, practically
provided for. Of course, despite all these things
and many other things, progress has been lament-
ably slow. The increase of population and the
growth of industries should be three times as
rapid as they are, and with the tariff and the
land laws that Australia should have, the growth
in both lines would be three times—or six times—
as rapid. But still N.S.W., under Federation,
has moved ahead as it hadn’t done previously for
half a generation or so, and the great bulk of the
good fortune which has attended on Federation
has fallen to N.S.W.’s share.
* * * •
The little Sydney crowd of importers, and the
remnant of the old anti-Bill push, reply, of course,
that these blessings and advantages are not owing
to Federation. They are things that would have
happened in any case. To that it can only be
said that at least they never happened previous to
Federation, and they had much more time to
happen in before the founding of the Common-
wealth than they have had since; that some of
them—the growth of manufactures and the
Old Age Pensions, for example—were plainly
the direct results of Federation; and that the
fact that Federation at least didn’t prevent
the other things happening— the increase of trade
and shipping, the sudden growth of population,
the rise in the values of stocks, shares and 1 .nd,
the increase in banking and company profits and
all the rest —gives the lie direct to the whole
pathetic tale of the anti-Federalists about the ruin
which Commonwealth legislation has brought on
the State. They brazenly assert that there is
actual visible disaster and decadence, and every
line in the share lists and the State statistics and
the balance-sheets of public institutions calls them
Ananias, individually and collectively. N.S.W.
is not prospering as it should do, but it is prosper-
ing as the old incapable purelv-State management
never made it prosper. It is at the top of the
pinnacle of its prosperity, and if the pinnacle isn’t
so high as it should be, the responsibility lies with
the Sydney contingent in the Federal Parliament,
which succeeded in getting Kingston’s draft
tariff cut down so heavily. If George P.kid and
about ten other N.S.W. members had been
temporarily or permanently dead in 1901 and 1902
N.S.W. might easily have gained 80,000 im-
migrants in the past three years instead of 18,000,
and have now 120,000 people employed in
its factories instead of 68,000. Unfortunately
in addition to its Foreign-traders and im-
porters, N.S.W.—or rather Sydney and a small
district attached thereto—is cursed with the
bumptious “Mother State ”, push—the old vci-
Billites, who are more inflated over the fact chat
N.S.W. has a slightly larger population than its
next neighbor, and that it is the State that was
lagged first, than Napoleon was when he had
conquered most of Europe. The pomp and pride
and circumstance and bursting dignity of this
mob make it impossible for the Commonwealth
to do anything that isn’t a “wrong” or an
“ insult ”or an “ injury.” A few members of this
crowd of deafening boss roosters are now talking
secession, and seeking to persuade the people of
N.S.W. that it is almost worth treason and
civil war to get away from the union
which has lifted the State out of the mud-
hole of poverty and depression to an affluence it
never knew before, and that it is almost worth
insurrection and bloodshed to get back to the old
rut, the old poverty, the old chronic departure of
the State’s best manhood to other countries, the
old lack of work, and the old dead days when
there were no old age pensions, because there was
no money to pay them with, and when the chief pro-
vision for the aged poor was what they could find
in the garbage box after dark. If N.S.W. is in
its senses it isn’t “ taking any more ” of its vener-
able Cabbage Tree Mob and its old Anti-Bill
acquaintances. They are to-day a humorless
assemblage of very unconvincing frauds, just asthey
were in the Federal referendum days and on
many previous occasions.
N.S.W. LABOR IDEAS.
The Political Labor League of N.S.W. opens its
annual conference in Sydney on January 26, and
it will have then to consider 144 suggestions for
the alteration and repair of the party’s political
platform. A number of the proposals are trivial
or merely formal; a few are foolish or crude or
unfinished-looking; a considerable number show
an amount of plain horse sense which is a missing
quantity at the gatherings of the “National”
Ass, and the “Liberal” League, and the people
who weep bitterly about the “wrongs” of N.S.W.
The Executive of the League proposes the
appointment of a committee to draft a compre-
hensive land and financial policy, and the need
for a definite financial policy is so obvious that the
proposal should be carried without any difficulty.
It also proposes that the Labor party should at
last go the Whole Hog—or something fairly near
to the Entire Animal—by declaring against all
borrowing except for the renewal of existing loans.
This is the noblest proposition in the list of 144 ideas,
and if the Executive would only add that every
time an old loan is renewed it should be renewed
with a sinking fund attached, the idea would be
complete, and the party would at last have gone
the Total Financial Swine in a most creditable
fashion. Nothing is definitely stated in the pro-
posal about setting aside a' certain amount of
revenue annually for new public works, but as
new public works are essential that is presumably
understood. It is at least a great step gained
when the Executive realises that the old idea of
permitting borrowing for new works, which will
pay interest on cost and something towards
sinking fund, is utterly impracticable. And it is
utterly and pathetically and obviously imprac-
ticable. Almost every new work, before it is
built, is going to pay full interest and something
over, but hardly any work does anything like that
after it is built, so only a party of prophets,
whose gift of prophecy was always in working
order, could make the present principle manageable.
The Leichhardt Labor League also submits a
proposal for the absolute cessation of borrowing
and the supply of money for public works
by means of a graduated land-tax. If any
proposal of this kind is carried, and if the men in
Parliament live up to it a good deal more con-
scientiously than they have lived up to the anti-
borrowing plank, in the present platform, the
N.S.W. Labor Party will become, distinctly and
pre-eminently, the Honest Party in finance, and
no body of members could ask for a better adver-
tisement than that.
* * * *
There are three separate proposals on the busi-
ness paper of the Labor Conference for a graduated
land-tax, one for an absentee-tax, one for the
compulsory cultivation of at least 10 per cent, of
each holding in agricultural districts, and several
dealing with the compulsory purchase of large
estates for closer settlement; but there is no
proposal—and there certainly should be one—
giving every tenant the right to purchase from his
private landlord, at an independent valuation,
the property he occupies. Three propositions
are made for the nationalisation of the
banking industry and one for the establish-
ment of a Commonwealth Bank, and two
propositions for replacing the present hopeless
and unworkable system of Party Government by
elective Ministries. Then there are proposals for
the restriction of usury, and the abolition of State
Governors; a State Life Assurance suggestion ;
proposals for the nationalisation of the liquor and
tobacco industries; for the taking over of “con-
sultations” by the Australian Government
per cent, of the profits—which isn’t much—to go
to a sinking fund and the balance towards old age
pensions); and a demand for the establishment of
an Australian navy. Also two country leagues
demand that the Labor party, in its Federal
capacity, should give up the foolishness of sinking
the unsinkable fiscal issue, and go solidly for
Protection to Australian industry. If these
and a few other valuable suggestions are
adopted into the Labor programme, and if
the party in Parliament doesn’t go to sleep
as it sometimes does, and if it doesn’t forget what
its principles are as it often does, Labor in poli-
tics should have a better outlook ahead of it than
it has ever yet had.
• • • •
There arc also, unfortunately, one or two loony
and reckless ideas to be submitted to the Confer-
ence, and it can only be hoped that they will be
treated with the disrespect they deserve. One
that strikes the eye conspicuously is a bold sug-
gestion that the accident pay from the Govern-
ment Accident Relief Fund should be raised to £1
a week—nothing being said as to whether the
Fund is expected to be thereby made bankruptor
to keep on its feet. It isn’t suggested that the
allowance should be increased if the fund can
stand it ; just that it should be increased.
Then there is an amazing proposal from
some bush league for cutting down the pay
of every public servant who receives £3OO a year
or more, whether he is worth his money or not
or even if he is worth twice his money. The scheme
is built on a sliding scale which works out so
amazingly that the man with £3OO is to be reduced
to £292 10s. ; the man with £3OOO to £2100; the
one with £3500 to £2275 ; the one with £4OOO to
£2190; and the one who now receives £4lOO is to
be cut down —heaven only knows why—to £2050,
which would put him below the £3OOO man.
Likewise there is a proposition from Broken Hill
that the Labor party in Parliament shall enter
into no alliance or coalition with any other bcdy—
a theory which seems designed to incite all other
parties to coalesce against it in self-defence. And
there is a scheme, which looks blankly illegal, pro-
viding that “ no candidate shall be eligible to run
for Parliament who has not been in a league
or Union for five years and a financial member of
one or the other.’’ The Constitution says exactly
who is and who is not eligible to run for Parlia-
ment, and any body which starts over-ruling the
Constitution in this fashion is liable to collide
disastrously and expensively with the law. More-
over, the idea suggests a desire to keep the Par-
liamentary billets for the battered old veterans of
the party, and however estimable the veteran may
be, he is apt to be stiff in the joints, and slow in
the mind, and gradual in the proceedings. But
despite the utterly foolish amendments, which are
not very many, and the trivial amendments,
which are pretty numerous, there are enough
bright ideas in the 144 subjects that the Confer-
ence will have to tackle to furnish material for
the best political programme in Australia. It is
all a question of selecting the really bright ideas.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Capital.
The failure of the Australian Parliament to come
to a vote on the Bill appropriating territory for
the Federal capital at Dalgety serves to emphasise
again the fact that the choice is between Mel-
bourne and Dalgety. The capital is at present—
more or less legally—located at Melbourne and it
will not be moved to any other spot than Dalgety.
The N.S. Walers who profess to see such dire
results flowing from the Melbourne location
should recognise this fact and accept the
inevitable. There was at the close of the 1905
session a clear majority in both Houses to ap-
propriate land at Dalgety for the capital. But
the opponents of that site (mostly N S.W. mem-
bers) “stonewalled” the measure (G. H. Reid
and J. 0. Watson for once finding themselves in
the same boat), and so it was postponed. The
same tactics may succeed again next session, and
again next Parliament, if the Administration is not
firmer; but still not a step will be taken towards
the Sydney ideal of having the capital at Balmain,
or to J. O. Watson s wild notion of building by
the site of a lake, which isn’t a lake, but a mud
flat, and appropriating one of Joseph Oarruthers’
rivers—as if Joseph Oarruthers looked like the
man to give away a river! —to make the mud liquid
enough to float a punt or two. The question as to
thesitehas been fixed, and fixed rightly, and—unless
the next elections bring a very material change to
the pers uiuel of the Parliament—fixed irrevocably.
The capital question now resolves itself into a
question of area ; and on that point a valuable and
timely report comes from Surveyor Scrivener.
He illustrates with maps proposed areas of 100,
200, 630, 900, and 1550 square miles, and the con-
viction forced home is that the largest area is the
only one at all satisfactory. The 100 miles
area is a mere suburban block, making no
provision for water supply, for power, or even for
proper parks and drainage. Within five miles of
the centre of the city, and within ultimately a few
yards of its outskirts, Federal territory would
cease, and there, in the State territory, might
cluster (and be encouraged to cluster by some
characteristic Oarruthers sort of person) noxious
trades, piggeries and such, making the city a den
of typhoid, and unendurable as a habitation.
Its water supply would be always at the
mercy of either State hostility or State care-
lessness—the hostility or carelessness of a State
which keeps in office a Premier who is a bitter
and unscrupulous enemy of the Commonwealth,
and loses no chance to throw an insult at it
or do it an injury. The possibility of the city
embracing Federal arsenals and other manufac-
tures would be nil. Even a reasonable growth of
suburbs would be out of the question. A 200-
miles area could be dosigned to embrace the
catchment of the Mowamba River and give a
decent water supply as far as quantity is con-
cerned, or to take in a stretch of the Snowy River
for electric power generation. Of course the
water supply in both these cases would still be
liable to be diverted above the Federal territory
by the State authority, and the Federal city’s
supply cut off; and the risk of pollution arising
out of carelessness, or out of the direct desire to
do the Federal city the worst kind of injury,
would still remain. But a 630 square miles area
is described by Mr. Scrivener as the irreducible
minimum
if a territory is to be acquired that has within it an
ample water supply from uncontaminated streams, and
sources of power sufficient for the needs of an important
city where coal cannot be obtained.
But in his opinion
it still leaves much to be desired because extensive
mining operations on the Snowy River and its tributaries
outside the area (where the country has been proved to
be auriferous and is now being worked) would con-
taminate the waters of the river, while the inclusion of
only a relatively small part of the Mowamba catchment
area involves the risk of pollution of the water from that
stream.
Surveyor Scrivener comes ultimately to the con-
clusion that the most desirable area is 1550 square
miles, giving the whole of the catchment area of
the Snowy and its tributaries. That area, as
shown on the map, is composed of 600 square
miles of Grown lands, valued at £224,000, and 950
square miles of alienated lands, valued at
£1,520,000. The latter the Australian Govern-
ment would, of course, pay for, and it might,
without grave wrong, pay also for the Crown lands,
as a concession to the spoilt brat attitude of New
South Wales. Making concessions to a spoilt
brat is not often good policy. " But The Bulletin
lives in Sydney, and feels selfishly inclined to
suggest something to soothe the brat a little. The
brat’s squeals are near to its«own ear.

The Fall of Mr. Fell.
David Fell, M.L.A., of Sydney, commonly
supposed to be a fair and sober-minded politician
and something above the average in intelligence,
has also lent himself to the purposes of the anti-
Federal push. In an interview to the Sydney Tele-
graph Mr. Fell helped to give a little extra oircula-


tion to the tired old lie that "the nearest com-
mercial centre” of the proposed h ederal capital at
Dalgety “would be Melbourn . Any reliable
map would burst up this story ; is some
miles nearer to Sydney than to Melbourne m a
straight line, and by the only practicable railway
route it is so many miles nearer Sydney t a o
Melbourne is quite out of the case ; but apparently
hone of the anti-Federal push ever looks at a map.
The feat about Dalgety in a commercial sense is,
hot lest the ttade Of the capital should go to Mel-
bourne, but lest it should find a new outlet from a
jjort ill southern BT;S;W. and thus do some damage
to the importers’ policy which seeks to bloat out
Sydney to absutd dimensions by dragging to it the
tvlidle commerce of the State. It ■vvouldn t look
ttell td say this, however ; so the old Melbourne
prejudice is dragged ih to disguise the situation.
Evhh now sonle df the trade of the ridrtherri P ar t
of Oroajingolortg, ih Victoria, which is much
further away frdhi Sydney thart Dalgety, comes
tb Sydney, and it did SO also m the days of the
Statfe system ana tlie border tariff ; irt fact, oii the
v ictbrian border SOuth of Dalgety add Delegate
the tariff was aljowfed to be almiost a dead letter
so that the people could get their supplies from
N.S.W.—the shpplies which came down froih
Sydney instead of from inaccessible Melbourne.
This fact is rough on the Dalgety-MelbOurnh
lie, but the lie keeps up a healthy, hus-
tling existence none the less. In some of his
other aspects Mr. Fell also showed a curious
conception of his duties. He says he has a definite
recollection that Premier Carrutheks brought
forth a proposal offering the Commonwealth four
sites—one of them being Dalgety. Nevertheless,
he reckons it the duty of every one “to fall in
line as citizens of New South Wales” and back
up Oarrothers in repudiating his own offer,
simply because a majority of the State Parliament
has endorsed Carruthers’ action. The idea that
he has any duty to the Commonwealth as a citizen
of Australia has apparently not occurred to Mr.
Fell ; there is no sign in his remarks that he ever
heard of Australia. Nor is there any sign of
an idea that his first duty as a citizen of
N.S.W. is to try to make N.S.W. an honest
State, which keeps faith with its neighbors.
David Fell was elected on the no-borrowing
ticket, but when he holds that it is his duty to
fall in line with the majority of the Assembly in
repudiating the offer, or the tacit offer, of Dalgety
as a Federal capital site, he will presumably also
feel it his duty to fall in with the majority in its
borrowing tendencies. With his curious notions
of his duty as a citizen of N.S.W., it is hard to
see what else Mr. Fell can do—in fact if the
majority went in for forgery or house-breaking he
would apparently feel called upon to “fall into
line.”
L for Leather—and Lunacy.
The tanning business is one of the tragedies of
N.S.W. industry. Ten years ago, it employed
1130 hands. Then, with diminished flocks and
herds and a keener foreign demand for the fewer
skins available, and with nothing in the shape of
an export duty on raw skins or an import duty on
leather, employment slackened, until, a few
months ago, the whole of the seven pelt tanneries
at Botany were practically closed so far as their
tanning departments were concerned. As late as
1902 there were 521 hands employed ; according
to figures just issued by the acting-Government
Statistician, the total at the end of 1904 was only
452—little more than a third of the number re-
corded ten years ago. Meantime, as N.S. W. has
grown more feet to shoe and produced less
leather to shoe them with, the imports of
foreign leather have increased enormously.
In 1900, Australia received from Britain
alone £77,690 worth ; last year, the Australian
tanneries having been almost knocked out by
foreign competition, it received £172,052 worth —
an increase of nearly 125 per cent, in four years.
Even larger imports come from the United States,
a total of £604,969 in the past five years. But the
U.S. imports are erratic, and there is a reason for
that. U.S. buyers mop up enormous quantities
of skins and pelts both in the Australian and
English markets, ship them at low freights, pass
them in free of duty, tan them, supply the pro-
tected American leather market, and dump the
surplus wherever the chance offers. If they can
make a profit out of the surplus, well and good ;
if they can’t use all up, and it seems good business
to knock out a foreign factory, they have their
surplus to do it with, and until Lyne’s Anti-
Dumping Bill passes there is nothing to stop them
doing it —they have already made their profit
from supplying their home market. Now it is
alleged that the Jap is also scenting out the Aus-
tralian hide and skin market. Six months ago a
Botany tanner, J. W. Birdsall, wrote to the
S.M. Herald that—
The Japanese are in our market for the raw hide ; and
the conditions they work under, and low wages they pay,
will be sure to stop our leather trade with them alto-
gether. They will have the latest machinery, no doubt,
and very likely at some future time will supply us with
leather. I have already seen samples of leather tanned in
Japan, and they are very good.
The same writer declared that there were enough
tanneries (nearly all idle) in the State to tan every
hide and skin produced here; yet the value of
exported sheep-skins alone runs—even in these
days of reduced flocks —to an average for all
Australia of about £1,000,000 a year (£2,991,535
for the . past three years). The summit
of absurdity is about reached when Australia,
not satisfied with exporting raw skins and
hides, and importing leather, and shutting
up its tanneries and turning its tanners idle,
last year actually also exported 251,986 cwt.
of bark for the foreigner to use in his tan-
neries. Australia has the crude, ill-paid job of
skinning its beasts and its trees; it gives the
foreigner the well-paid and more elaborate job of
using the two products to make its shoe-leather ;
and "its Railway Commissioners even send to the
foreigner for the leather to cover their carriage-
seats. And when the people who have been
thrown out of a job haven’t the money to pay a
fare to use the seats when they are covered with
foreign leather made out of Australian skins tanned
with Australian bark—well, there is always Cohen
to fall back upon for a loan. A worm can turn to
Cohen ; it takes some pluck, it seems, to turn to
an export duty on these raw products and a suf-
ficient import duty on leather.

. The Difference.
When the news of the Maoriland General Elec-
tion came to hand almost every Tory paper in the
Commonwealth had an article about the remark-
able prosperity of the country, and added that, of
course, Seddon's Democratic policy and his Pro-
tectionist tariff had nothing at all to do with it.
It was wholly owing to the soil and the rainfall.
In almost every case it could be seen that the
artiole was written for the purpose of dragging in
the assurance that it was all owing to the soil and
the rainfall; even the wooden old Launceston
(Tas.) Examiner made the same remark as the'
rest. And this was the worst mistake of all, for
Tasmania is all the time a horrible evidence that
it is legislation, and that is is not soil or rainfall
which makes all the difference. Tasmania has a
better rainfall than Maoriland ; two-thirds of
Seddon’ s State get less than 30in. of rain per
annum, while only one-fourth of Tasmania does.
Tasmania is the more fertile State of the two ; in
1904 the value of the output on its cultivated area
--the little bit it wasn't too tired to cultivate—was
£4 17s. sd. per acre, while Maoriland, on its more
barren soil, could only show a value of £4 3s. lOd. per
acre. Tasmania is the less crowded State of the
two ; it has less than seven persons to the square
mi!\ while Maoriland has over eight. And
Tasmania had 40 years start. But the blight of
Toryism and stagnation is on the island, and it
never raised a Ballance or a Seddon. While
Maoriland cut up its large estates to make room
for settlers, and broke up its bloated properties
by a graduated land-tax with the same object in
View, and helped its farmers through its State
Land Bank. Tasmania sat still and admired and
encouraged its big landlords, and these are the
results : J n IBfil Tasmania had 180 acres under crop
for every 100 inhabitants ; in 1903 only 150 acres.
Maoriland, in 1861, had only 70 acres under crop
for every iou inhabitants, but in 1903 it had
210. Maoriland had grown threefold while Tas-
mania sagged back. Tasmania in P raised
crops equal irt value to £7 Is. 9d. per
inhabitant—it is the most fertile part of the Com-
monwealth, yet the yalue of the crop it raises per
head is less even than that of barren South Aus-
tralia. Maoriland, with a less fertile soil, drags
out crops worth £8 17s. 2d. per inhabitant. The
area under cultivation in Tasmania is decreasing*
and its population is just about stationary ;
Maoriland, although already more crowded
than Tasmania, not only keeps its natural increase,
but attracts more immigrants than all the Com-
monwealth put together. Maoriland is only four
times the size of Tasmania, but it has ten times
as many sheep and cattle. Maoriland has 44 times
Tasmania’s population, but, under a live tariff, it
has seven times as many persons employed ir
manufactures. Maoriland, when it had a Govern-
ment and a tariff like Tasmania’s, was just about
as dead. The rain and the soil and the climate
were there just as they are now, but the rain fell
On a corpse, just as it does in Tasmania to-day.
But Maoriland awoke, while Tasmania, with
greater advantages, remained a corpse. _ The
difference between the two States now is all
Seddon, or, rather, the policy which Seddon
stands for—High Protection, the breaking up of
large estates, vigorous land settlement, the dis-
couragement of the absentee, and the fostering < t
a local manufacturing population to provide the
farmer with a market. And the painful anxiety
of the Australian Tory press to explain that the
difference isn’t Seddon is the best proof that it is
Seddon.
Let Us Have the Freehold.
When the Maoriland Parliament meets, the great
Freehold v. Leasehold question will come up for
settlement, and then anything may happen.
Neither the Freehold party nor the Leasehold
party, so far as is known, has a majority ; the rest
of the House is doubtful, and Premier Seddon
himself is doubtful. A few years ago Maoriland
decided that, so far as at least the bulk of its
public lands was concerned, it would only grant
leaseholds, and the sale of land was almost
entirely suspended. The State gave the first
leaseholders an absurdly easy bargain in the shape
of the 999 years tenure, but, all the same, the
loudest part of the present clamor arises out of
the demand of the 999-years lease people for the
right to actually purchase their lands. They claim
that Providence has implanted in man an incur-
able instinct that makes him desire to have for
his very own the bit of dirt on which he
lives and toils, and that no matter how
easy lus landlord may be the instinct is always
there. If this is so, it is, of course, no use at all
for Seddon, big as he is, to struggle with Provi-
dence. An ineradicable instinct is a hard thing
for any politician to argue with, while that and
Providence combined would make a majority at
any election. The Bulletin’s advice to Richard
Seddon under these trying conditions is, if he finds
the present situation untenable, to climb down
and cave in—to climb down thoroughly and to
cave in completely, lie ought to give even more
than is demanded. The argument against him
is that the leasehold system is bad, and if it is bad
for a man to be a tenant of the State, it is quite as
bad for him to be anybody’s tenant. The theory
of the Freehold party is that man naturally and
instinctively wants to own the bit of land he
occupies, and he must feel that way just as
instinctively whether his landlord is the State
or some affluent Tory person in the Upper
House. Providence, according to the Free-
hold push, has made every man long to be
the absolute possessor of his own home, and
if that is so Providence must have given him
that feeling no matter whether he rents the home
from the Government or from old Captain
Russell, one-time leader of the stupid party in
Maoriland politics. The present leaseholders
propose to break their bargain with the State by
means of a legal enactment, and to acquire the
power, contrary to the terms of their agreement, to
compulsorily buy their landlords put at a very
moderate price. If one bargain can be set aside
by legal enactment another can, and if it is right
for the State to be compulsorily bought out by
its tenant at a fixed price the same thing is cer-
tainly right for the private landlord. The
simplest way out of a very great difficulty is for
Premier Seddon to fully recognise the alleged
yearning—said to be implanted by Provi-
dence under every human waistcoat—to
have a home of one’s very own, and to
give every tenant, or every group of joint tenants,
the right to compulsorily buy out the proprietor
at the tax-valuation of the property. That will
be a generous recognition of all the arguments of
the people who demand the freehold; it will
carry their theories to their logical conclusion ;
and it will give them not only all they ask, but a
great deal more. And very many of the Lease-
hold crowd would accept such a compromise. So
long as landlordism is rigidly restricted, and the
land is divided with reasonable fairness among
the people, and the aggregation of great estates
is rendered impossible, it doesn’t matter so very
much whether the State collects rent or land-tax,
or whether the people it collects from are called
tenants or tax-payers. As regards the personal
aspect of the case, if Premier Seddon remains
with the Leasehold crowd he may easily
go down, for that crowd seems to be
weakening. If he goes with the present Free-
hold crowd—the baldly s fifish combination
which holds that the State tenant who gets his
land for very little a year should have a right of
compulsory purchase, while he should give no
right of compulsory purchase to his own sub-
tenant—he will almost certainly go down. The
glaring, insolent selfishness of that gang would
wreck any Minister who lent himself to its
schemes of plunder. And Seddon can’t sit on a
rail for ever ; even the strongest rail gets worn
out in lime with lGst. or ISst. of undecided
Premier upon it. It is a time for compromise—
judicious compromise. And this compromise
would rally up many old friends, and put many
old and new enemies in a most interesting
dilemma, and start a third issue which would
draw a lot of public attention away from the
other two.
B. Smith Again.
That gigantic political fraud, Bruce Smith, after
staying away from the late lamented session of
Parliament all the while there was any work to be
done, and especially while there were chances of
all-night sittings, put in an appearance during the
last week of the session. As usual, he got rid of
a lot of twaddlesome pro-Jap talk. Such talk in
the House is harmless. Even the Foreign-trade
press doesn’t think it worth reporting, and no one
who hears it considers it seriously. There would
be no cause of complaint and no reason for con-
tradiction if B. Smith made his remarks only to
those who meet him on equal or better intellectual
terms, for the man with a narrower intellect than
B. Smith hasn’t got a vote as a rule. But the
craving to be listened to, and to, be believed, has
taken such a hold of B. Smith that he has fallen a
victim to the talking-to-women habit. In almost
every town that he visits he addresses
the Women’s National Ass, or some such
body, on politics. He did this in Mel-
bourne, the other day, and Janet Mrs.
Clarke supplied the hall and the afternoon tea
and got together an audience. Then Bruce Mr.
Smith was in his glory. He talked for nearly two
hours. One hour and fifty minutes of it was silly
drawing-room “guff” that meant nothing and
sounded like brass falling on wood. But for two
minutes B. Smith said things that deserve to be
flatly contradicted. He told his women listeners
that the United States was at the zenith among
the nations, and the people of the United States
are an amalgam of races. Consequently he argued
“all the clatter we hear about the danger of
mixing races—-abuut shutting out Italians, Gcr"
mans, Spaniards, and other races—was nonsense.’
Consequently, also, it was humiliating to him and
to us as British people that the Japanese should
be shut out. B. Smith’s surface-skimming mind
has found that the United States are on top of
the heap. It has also found that Americans
are a mixed race. It at once connects the two as
cause and effect. The big Protc ctive tariff counts
for nothing to Smith. The highly developed
trades-unionism, the stringent State labor legisla-
tion, are all things that don t matter to Smiih.
The restrictions put on immigration are unknown
to Smith. No colored immigrant is admitted.
No white immigrant, no matter of what race, is
admitted unless he can table 200 dollars in cash,
unless he satisfies the authorities that he has never
been in gaol, unless he is free from disease, and
of good character, and not too old, and seems to
have a prospect of making a livelihood. A
strange passenger to the United States is turned
inside out, so that the authorities may see if he
has any germs concealed about him. Then take
the American regime in the Philippines. The
same restrictions as to entrance apply there, and
when once the immigrant has got through and
lands in Manila, he finds that he is not wanted.
The “amalgam of races” has no use for him.
Every application for a job is met with the same
question. “Yes, sir, an Australian. Are you a.
naturalised citizen of the Yew-nited States?” If
you are not, you don’t get the job no matter how
well qualified you may be. And naturalisation can
be obtained only by six months’ domicile in one
of the States of the Union. America says
that she fought for Manila and won it, and now'
means to use it for the benefit of her own people.
And the wonderful Smith contrives to be ignorant
of all these things—of the U.S. tariff, of the fact
that U.S. manufactures only began with the
beginning of Protection, of the U.S. anti-Chinese,
and anti-nigger laws, and of many other things.
The Smith capacity for ignorance is so vast that
an Alp could be dropped into it and not be
noticed.

The Gentle Chinese Butcher.
The following extracts from a letter received from
South Africa may prove of interest to those
individuals who think on “ Imperial ” lines, and
to colored labor advocates; also to the many
Australians who lost their heads during the war
fever, and helped to make a fair and well-governed
land the black hole of brutality and horror it now
is :
Murder and outrage by Chinese deserters is of daily
occurrence. As much as possible of their doings is kept
out of the papers, but some of it they cannot keep
quiet. The South African constabulary are con-
stantly being called out, and it simply means
shooting them down. A Dutch farmer was murdered!
the other night, and his wife and children nearly
done to death; there was another house within
. 00 yards, and yet these wretched Chinese broke into the
place in search of food, and when the farmer, hearing
l Pern, came out of his room, they attacked him with
knives and a pick. The farmer received 50 stabs before
they finally finished him. A little boy of 10 years of age,
win fought manfully by his father’s side, was badly
stabbed, but managed to make a dart for the door, and
ran to his grandfather’s house, pursued by a Chinaman.
The child reached the house and gave the alarm. Two
little girls, 13 and 15 years old, who were in the room,
escaped through a window. Meanwhile, the farmer had
dropped, and his wife, a strong, powerful woman, came
from the bedroom with a baby in her arms, snatched a
knife from a Chinamen, and kept the brutes at bay ; but
by the time help arrived she had received a ghastly cut
through the skull and a stab in the groin. Her baby was
also badly injured. m __ , .
Everybody is arming now. The Dutch are terror-
stricken and afraid to leave their families and homes. It
is all very well for people like Lord Selrorne and others,
who sit safely at home, to say there is no danger—they
would be the first to cry out, were they in the same
position that some of these people are in. Only those who
hear and see as much as we do can realise the horror of
lfc At the mines the Chinese are constantly declining
to work, and then charging the officials, driving them
before them. The S.A. constabulary are then called
in, and after some shooting the mob is cowed.
The trouble with the Chinese is getting very serious, and
letters in the papers are fast and furious. If this murder-
ing and outrage continues, I believe the people will rise
in a body and demand that the Chinese be sent back. On
Monday night a white storekeeper was murdered ; another
seriously injured, and others hurt. The Chinese attacked
them with “ jumpers,” which are easily procured, as they
are thrown all about the mines. This murder was prac-
tically on the mine at Klipfontein. A woman and ber
baby have come here to stay with friends. Her husband
works on one of the mines, and when her husband was on
night shift she walked about all night with the baby,
watching, and slept during the day. Twenty deserters
were brought to the gaol yesterday. There are about 600
at large. “ God Save the King ! ” Whaft'or?

Food Advertisements.
One material failing in all the Health Acts of
Australia is the absence of a provision to regulate
public advertisements dealing with food stuffs.
With the exception of the embargo on obscenity
there is practically no check on advertisements.
That class of notification which tells mankind that
to be healed of anything from scurvy to loco-
motor ataxia it is Only necessary to take some-
body’s pills or draughts, or to wear some
weird mechanical device, is harmful enough, but
it is at least humorous. It is so wildly humorous
that the only people who are gulled by it are those
who have, in all ages, been the natural prey of the
man with a cure-all, a gold brick, or a Fiji uncle.
Even these deserve to be protected in a con-
temptuous sort of way, though an Act for the
Complete Protection of Fools is a large order.
But something serious and definite should be
done to safeguard the public against a class of
advertisement which is put forward reasonably,
which claims to have sound authority for its
statements, and yet which, by its mis-
representations, may cause serious loss ot
life. This is not a humorous matter. In re-
gard to infants’ foods and preparations, it is
especially desirable to prevent the wastage of
population. There, may be noticed, in all the
Australian papers, advertisements of infants
foods and biscuits and medicinal preparations
which, if the instructions are followed
to the letter, must cause many thousands
of deaths yearly. Such and such ,an article
is advertised as a “perfect food ’ for in-
fants, though competent analysis would show
that only an exceptional child could, live on it as
a diet. Such and such a preparation is advertised
as the best to tide an infant over “the troubles
of childhood.” It is used and tides the unfortunate
over the Styx. The recent N.S. W. Birth-rate Com-
mission is understood to have made certain recom-
mendations on this point. It certainly went to the
trouble of analysing various foods, biscuits and
preparations for the young, and found its con-
clusions promptly suppressed by the ‘ ‘ business
interests ” which were set to work. The Public
Health Act in each State should provide that any
article advertised as a healthy food for children
should be subjected to analysis, and, failing proof
of its claims, the owners should be prohibited
from advertising it. Having effected that as a
start, another good move would be analytical
supervision of all medical preparations and any
liquors advertised as medicinal, whether intended
for infant or adult use.
