A Much-Suspended Act.
Parliament seldom' acts wisely in leaving the
enforcement of a law to the will of a Minister or
a Government; it never acts wisely if the law be
one passed in the interests of the working classes.
The N.S.W. Shearers’ Accommodation Act was
demanded for years. Those who knew the back
country, and the conditions of station life when
large bodies of men were brought together, pressed
on Government after Government the duty of
seeing that proper provision was made for health,
The Curse of the Council.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The anti-Australian cant cry, which represents every-
thing- “ colonial ” as being necessarily inferior, has not for
many years been louder or more audacious than it is at
present. At a time when members of an influential
ladies’ association take pride in announcing that they
never wear anything made in Australia, it is not sur-
prising that, in other directions, a vigorous attempt
should be made to discount the value of Australian talent.
—Melbourne Age.
Once, when the gods in Council,
Wary and old and wise,
Fixed the fate of the Nations
In their council hall of the skies —
From the hour of our first forefathers
Till the day when the dead shall rise.—
They made a compact among them
To lay on one land a curse !
And gave to the men of the Northland
The spell of the gods to nurse;
That they might hide it, and hold it,
As a miser holdeth his purse.
Until, ir. a timely season
(Thus ran the gods’ decree),
They laid the curse of the Council
On a young land, fearlev-, and free
From schism and Cld World sorrow—
And they answered : “ So it shall be ! ”
And the restless men of the Northl.nd,
Hardy and strong of limb,
Looked from their wave-worn bulwarks
■ Over the seaways grim,
And dreamed of new lands to conquer
O’er the verge of the far sea-rim.
And the train of the white years found them,
Steadfast of heart and hand,
Threading the sea-paths lonely
To hasten the gods’ command —
Bearing the Curse of the Council
Forth as a burning brand.
' a young land sprang from the distance,
A::d gathered them to her breast,
Weary of toil and tempest,
Hungered for land and rest;
And they laid the Curse of the Council
On her—at the gods’ behest.
And straightway her dusky people
I dwindled, as some strong light
Flickers in gusty spasms
And dies in the windy night—
And the curse clave to the young land
As a plant grows to the light !
They crowned her a free white nation,
But the Curse of the Council grew ;
Strength of her strength and purpose,
Breath of the breath she drew—
Ever its blight was on her
As a malignant dew.
And lo ! in the world of nations,
In the council halls of Time,
’Mong the sculptors and the artists,
’Mong the singers of song sublime,
She is a naked bankrupt
Whose nakedness is a crime !
Strangers sit in her councils,
Strangers possess her fame,
Strangers divide her treasure,
Strangers belie her name ;
Pulpit and Crown have witnessed
The document of her shame.
Thus ! for the gods in Council—
Wary and old and wise—
Fixed the fate of the nations
In their council hall of the skies,
From the hour of our first forefathers
Till the hour when the dead shall rise.
VIC.
M. E. J. PITT.

Tobacco
decency, and moderate comfort during the period
of employment. “Congested abominations ” was
a term that fitly described many of the
places designated as shearers’ quarters.
But it was hard to find a Government willing to
give the necessary attention. At last, after in-
cessant hammering, the Accommodation Bill was
brought in and passed, but passed with a condition
which enables the Government to hang it up out
of the reach of operation when and where so dis-
posed, It can be hung up because of a drought
and because of a flood ; because of a bad season,
and because of a very busy one. It was hung up
in various districts in 1902, 1903 and 1904. It was
hung up in 1905, and will continue to be hung up
wherever vested interests seem to be too powerful
to be attacked, l’he result is that the shearing
area of the country is a motley ; a Joseph’s coat
of patches varying in color, texture and quality.
Upright pastoralists give their men good quarters
and would do so without any compulsion; the
other kind for whom the law of compulsion was
devised, escape under the condition that allows
the Minister to use his own indiscretion. In
some parts men are worse off than before the Act
was passed, and in most as badly off. Reports
from one district show that the section of
the Act which separates the sleeping-room
from the dining-room is disregarded. In
another district, where shearing begins in
July or August, the huts are built of gal-
vanised iron, and are described as, at that time
of the year, refrigerating chambers. Bronchitis
and lung trouble are consequently always on hand.
Ihe floors being boarded, oil-drums or other im-
provised fireplaces are prohibited by the manage-
ment. Huts in other districts consist of iron bark
slab walls, with plenty of openings all round, but
with no proper doors or windows and no flooring.
Here and there, all over, sanitary arrangements
are primitive. Of course, many pastoralists provide
reasonable accommodation, and some go farther. On
i me station the squatter provides books and papers,
char png the men a few pence per week—just as
much as clears the expenses. At another a regular
reading-room has been erected, with draughts,
cards, and other amusements. Instead of the old
sheet-iron structure, with bunks in tiers for
50 or 100 men, the sleeping apartments are
fitted up with four or six bunks in each, and
proper protection from wind and rain is pro-
vided. There always were employers like
these, with whom legal compulsion is not needed.
But men of the other class not only need
law to compel them to give employees decent
conditions, but a law which they cannot get round
by climbing up a back stair and breathing surrep-
titiously on the back of the Ministerial head.
They may not (or again they may) bribe or
corrupt a Minister, but they are pertinacious
enough to weary-him into compliance, or clever
enough to bamboozle him. If the Shearers’
Accommodtition Act had imposed duties on
employees instead of on employers, it would not
have been practically hung up from 1902 to 1906.
And there is only a Ministerial say-so that
employers will be made to comply with the Act
in 1907.

The Sydney “Telegraph’s” New Year Lie.
Sydney Telegraph opened the New Year
by lying m a crude, shameless, brazen fashion,
‘ in a way calculated to sow dissension among
the people of Australia and undermine the
spirit of friendship and brotherhood. This is an
extract from the paper’s misleading article of
January 1
Australia begins the new year under conditions that
cannot be regarded as entirely satisfactory, though it
may be said that none of the difficulties in the path are
of a kind that will not yield to ordinarv common seme
and good feeling. In the first place, it cannot be ignored
that unpleasant friction has been created between the
Government of this State and the Government of the
Commonwealth, owing mainly to the manner in which
the plain meaning of the Constitution has been miscon-
strued in respect to the Federal capital. The interpre-
tation that places the capital, which w'as to be “not less
than 100 miles from Sydney”, at a distance of 296
miles from that city, and as near to the Victorian
border as possible, with a seaport which, as far as
access is concerned, makes it independent of this
btate altogether, may be very creditable to the
ingenuity of a lawyer, but it doesn’t represent what was
confessedly in the minds of the framers of the clause.
Ihe people of this State have ample justification for the r
irritation. To be manoeuvred, by means of ingenious
word-twisting, out of the very concession which induced
them to enter the Federation is surely sufficient reason
!u r ? T etermine(l Protest. It is earnestly to be hoped
that the Commonwealth Government will yet see the
absolute necessity of preserving good faith in this matter,
and that the Federal Parliament will divest itself of petty
provincialism and carry out the spirit, not the contorted
letter, of the Constitution.
N.S.W. wasn t “induced to enter the Federa-
tion 5 it invited the other States to enter it,
though that, perhaps, is an issue that is hardly
definite enough to allow falsehood to be legally
proved, therefore the point may pass. Nobody
proposes to “ manoeuvre ” N.S.W. by “ingenious
word-twisting ” or otherwise, out of one iota of its
bargain ; the bargain was that the capital was to
be in N.S.W. (and the Dalgety site is in N.S.W.),
not less than 100 miles from Sydney (the Dalgety
site is not less than 100 miles from Sydney), and
OI ) a federal territory of not less than 100 square
miles (the proposed area is not less than 100 square
miles). But this also may pass; it may be
possible, by the Telegraph’s amazing power of
word-twisting, to set up some apparent defence
here against a charge of direct lying. But a
third statement of the anti-Federal paper is that it
was only the “concession” of the capital which
led the people of N.S.W. to decide to enter the
Federation, and this is unvarnished lying. The
people of N.S.W. gave the first Constitution Bill
(in which the locality of the capital was left an
open question) a majority of over 5000 votes, and
the majority contained 21,000 more votes than the
minimum which N.S.W., in the original arrange-
ment with the other States, agreed should be suffi-
®nsm'° acceptance. The fact that the
iN.S. Y\. State Parliament, like any other Judas
or spieler, broke its agreement, raised the mini-
mum to 80,000, and thus nullified the people’s
acceptance of the original Bill, makes no difference
m the fact that the majority was there and that the
stipulated 50,000 votes were more than there.
Ihe people of N.S.W. didn’t require the
concession’ of the capital to “induce”
them to enter the Commonwealth. The
l elegraph s case against the Australian
Government is almost wholly based on
lies. Partly out of this special lie and partly out
of other lies the Telegraph has built up a long
and bitter agitation against the Commonwealth
Administration. It has been a slanderer and a
bearer of false witness, and if it cared to
swear to its statements it would be a perjurer.
It has pandered with its lie to the worst
and meanest grudges of provincialism, and
it has told its lie so often that the lie
has gained wide credence, and has done a
great deal to promote ill-feeling and to retard
n^ e BT° w th of a national Australian spirit.
Ihe New Year finds the Sydney Telegraph’s
liar writing up the paper’s old lie with all the old
energy and the old effrontery, yet when the lying
paper states that conditions “cannot be regarded
as entirely satisfactory” it certainly records
a fact. While the Sydney Telegraph is
zealously promoting an anti - Federal feel-
ing by the circulation of its malicious and
circumstantial lie, conditions are not satisfac-
tory. The mayor of Sydney and sundry dreary
and respectable gentlemen who are to hold forth
at Sydney Town. Hall some time this month on
the alleged “grievances” of N.S.W. are cordially
backed up by the Telegraph. The Telegraph is a
living evidence of the fact that the most outrageous
lies, if told often enough, can get a hearing and a
following, and that there is no lie so remarkable
that some highly-respectable-looking person
belonging to an allegedly honorable profession
won’t tell it for a consideration. The Telegraph
has possibly very few other uses, but in that
matter it fills the bill magnificently.

John Wonders lately wrote to Sydney Tele-
graph, signing his name, and affixing thereto the
legend “Miners’ Secretary, Helensburgh,” and
stating that the time had arrived for the workers
of N.S.W. to emulate those of Russia. Possibly,
as John’s foolish letter bore the date, December
24, it was only a Santa Claus joke, but the
Telegraph wrote a leader about it, and pointed
out to the community in general what dangerous
scoundrels these unionists are. In a pale, shocked
tone it expressed the pious hope that the views of
John Wonders were not those of unionists gener-
ally, but it carefully explained that John wrote
as the representative of the Helensburgh miners,
so his views were presumably, &c., &c., &c. When
the dreary Trickett, of the N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Council, advocated rebellion and secession
in order that N.S. Wales might dictate to the rest
of Australia in defiance of the Constitution Act,
the Telegraph wasn’t seized by this injured frame
of mind. It did not write in a pained tone of
pen, so to speak, to express a hope that the rest of
the M’s.L.O. were not possessed with the wild,
unreasonable, and dangerous spirit of Trickett.
Rather it proceeded, by downright lies, half-truths
and general misrepresentation, to fan the passions
of a misled populace in order that just the very
sort of thing that is happening in Russia to-day
might be brought about in the streets of Sydney,
and Trickett and the Gaud Mayor, and old
tottery Dr. M‘Laurin, and other antiquated poli-
tical humbugs make their last stand behind a
barricade of plug hats in front of the Telegraph.
office.
Amongst the needs of the Australian citizen army
when the several State forces came under Common-
wealth control was a cheap, serviceable, distinctive
uniform—a dress to be fought in as well as to be
worn in time of peace. Major-General Hutton
recognised this to some extent, and went as far
as an English officer was likely to. The Common-
wealth uniform approved by him was serviceable
in color and fit to fight in. The general pattern
was universal. Small gorget patches of different
colors distinguished each of the several arms,
while different regiments had their own badges
and other marks. So far so good. The fact that
uniforms are largely required in Australia for
Government House wear, and the British
gold-lace tradition generally, was recognised
by the allowance of certain gold fal-lals for officers
and colored shoulder-cords and breast-lines for
men. But apparently the all-British full-
plumage variety of the tinsel and feather tradition
has been only scotched, and not killed. The Aus-
tralian Defence Department is about to issue new
dress regulations for the forces, and lately it cir-
cularised commanding officers of regiments to
ask whether they desire to adopt the Common-
wealth uniform for full dress, or retain the old
uniform of their corps for this purpose. Such as
desire the latter may have their full dress
prescribed in, and authorised by, the new regula-
tions. There will probably be many corps which
will stick to the old order of things. The result
will be that such corps will have to adopt the
Commonwealth uniform for work, but will have
their special Joseph’s coats, after the style of the
British army, for Government House wear and
for purposes of celebration and pomp. These
extra clothes will serve no purposes except'
those of pomp. The cost of the special rig
varies according to gorgeousness, but a typical
variety would probably run to £5 per man, and
£3O per officer. Has the Australian Council of
Defence nothing to do but evolve such absurdities ?
In Europe, perhaps, the army which does not wear
a red, blue, green, or yellow coat would be outside
the comity of nations, and no self-respecting officer
would be expected to be seen in public without
either a rooster’s tail or some gold lace. But Aus-
tralia is just starting, in the Federal military
sense, and no long-standing and ineradicable
rooster-tail has been handed down to it by a long
series of Commonwealth traditions. Why go
looking for rooster-tail ?
Vkjtoma. now possesses, in its Amended Factories
and Shops Act, what most of the other States
already boast, an effective Early Closing Law.
Practically all shops in the city and suburbs will
henceforth close at 6 p.m. and at 1 p.m. on
Wednesdays or Saturdays, with a late night on
1 rid ay if Saturday be the half-holiday chosen.
Restaurants, pie-shops, fruit-shops, etc., may keep
open later, but must not work their employees
more than 00 hours a week ; while bicycle boot-
repairers’, dairy produce, flower, hairdressers’ and
pawnbrokers’ shops need not put up the shutters
till 8 p.m. on week-nights. The morning after the
.ill passed the House of -Rich Landlords, a deputa-
tion of grocers and their assistants called and
thanked . Chief Sec. Gillott, and asked him
to do his best to get both Houses to pass
a resolution constituting a Minimum Wage Board
tor the trade, “on which both employers and
employees were agreed,” excepting a few sweating
cutters who paid their managers 255. a week or
Jess if they could get them for less. Gillott was
sympathetic, but admitted plainly that, owing to
the provision requiring the consent of both Houses
to the establishment of any new Wages Board, the
clauses bearing thereon were practically dead
letters. In fact, the only board now in existence
is the Dressmakers’ Board, the others having
lapsed, though their wage-decisions still stand
good.

'J- D. Campbell, M.L.A., of Bananaland, came
back from England, the other day, much wiser in
his Tory generation
I was greatly impressed with the intense culture prac-
tised on the Continent. It is evident on every hand.
How different in England and Scotland! There, vast
areas are locked up under grass or as sport-grounds for
the aristocracy. On the Continent, rapid strides are
being made in soil-tillage, forest conservation, and
in the constructive industries. England seems stag-
nant. The people are not on the land, but huddled in
the cities. The Conservative classes are too self-satisfied,
or too selfish, to bother with the problem of race-dete-
rioration which is going on as a result of the unequal
distribution of wealth. I had a striking- example one
Sunday of thedisparit.v which exists between theconditions
surrounding the two classes—the wealthy and the poor.
I went down Petticoat Lane first, and saw the cheap rub-
bish sold there to the poor—stuff one would not offer to a
dog—and then I went to a tony church.parade, where the
darlings of society and fashion gathered up their skirts.
And what a contrast, ! It must be seen to be understood.
In the Petticoat Lane throng there were hundreds of
thousands steeped in the direst poverty ; and, on the
other hand, the moneyed classes, at whose mercy these
hordes of poor wretches are. This cannot, surely, last
always? It is a question to some extent bound up with
the idle lands of the country'.
♦ ’
Westrajja’s new Minister of Lands is making
enough discoveries to drive a stiff land-tax
through a stone-wall. Out of 11,000.000 acres of
allegedly good agricultural land, practically given
away by the. State, only 350,000 acres have been
brought under cultivation. Less than 200,000
acres have been sown with wheat this year, and
Westralia will have to import half-a-million
bushels. And yet more than ten years ago
Bigjohn said Westralia would overtake its own
requirements within a couple of seasons, and
would soon beat 8. Australia as an exporter of
grain. There is plenty of good land—millions of
acres of it; but it is mostly locked up in big estates
—many owned by Bigjohn’s friends and other
ancient ’Gropers, Government land agents and in-
spectors. Westralia has waited 10 years, instead of
Bigjohn’s two years, to see if the people who own
the country would use it without being driven by
a land-tax. As they evidently won’t use it, it’s
clearly only a fair thing to give the land-tax a
chance.
The Victor Daley Memorial.
A movement which should appeal to all lovers of
good poetry was inaugurated in Sydney during
the week, the object being to raise funds to place
a suitable memorial upon the grave of the great
poet, Victor J. Daley. Those desirous of contri-
buting can forward donations to this office. Any
sums which come to hand will be duly acknow-
ledged and forwarded to the treasurers.
“0.K.” : Postal Department, ever anxious
to study the inconvenience of the public, suspended
the business of letter-carrying for more than two
consecutive days this Christmas. Nobody could
receive a letter between the afternoon of Saturday,
23rd, and the morning of Tuesday, 26th, and on
Tuesday one delivery had to serve for the day.
For two days and a-half all the letters posted in
Melbourne for Sydney, or vice versa, lay untouched
in the boxes. The mail closed at, say. Melbourne
G.P.0., at 2.45 p.m. on Saturday, and the next
mail was despatched at 6 a.m. or thereabouts on
Tuesday. And this for the inconvenience of the
public rather than for the benefit of the Postal
employees ! This long period of closure happens
only when Christmas Day falls on a Monday, or
is gazetted for Monday (as in last year’s case)
when it falls on the Sunday. There is no logical
reason why the public should thus be victimised
on account of a certain date over which the
P.M.-G. has no control. The mailmen would be
glad to forego the occasional Monday off at Christ-
mas if they were compensated, in batches, at
other times of the year. And there is nothing,
save a little thoughtful management, needed for
the achievement of the desired alteration. But
the official brains of the Post-office have
no liking for any new effort in managennnt. As
long as the community suffers itself to be ill-
served the Postal service will stick to its present
you-be-damned policy.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Reclining in the long dry grass at Waitara, he
reminded me of a lion with a hurt lung ; from his
eyes came a nimbus that seemed to radiate from
an internal crater.
The cottage stood in the shadow of giant gums.
Hills of blue stretched in front and behind. I
saw him the day after he arrived at Waitara. He
was wrapped in an overcoat and muffler. He
told me solemnly that he was training for a fight.
He was to meet his opponent about Christmas, he
said.
“ What’s his weight ? ” I asked.
Tie looked at me steadily. “If you put him in
one scale and the Himalayas in the other, he’d
tip the beam. He has a terrible left.’’
“All very well,” I said, sorrowfully; “but
what about”-
“Eileen ! ’’ he roared, “bring the gentleman a
hymn-book ! ”
Eileen brought me a glass of wine.
“ Apart from the man with the dirty left,” he
said, “ I have been dreaming about the Corridor
of Volcanoes. I lay last night with the weight of
the Atlantic pressing on my chest. I had ceased
to breathe, and out of the black Chaos there
appeared a small live cinder. It was in the centre
of the earth, and I moved towards it leisurely
through a country of sandstone and lava.. Then I
began to descend in strides of a thousand feet
until the cinder grew wonderfully large and
luminous. I came upon an endless lane flanked
with burning metal shields. I was sorry to see the
word Hell printed in Hebrew letters over the rock-
bound entrance.
Australia’s sweet singer , VICTOR DALEY.
Died in the evening of 1205.
“Then I saw the volcanoes—endless vistas of
burning mountains stretching like mole hills
through the earth's interior. A black smoke-
tortured face gaped at me suddenly from the
entrance. ‘James,’ it said, ‘your volcano isn t
quite ready.”’
On Dec. 18th he passed a bad night. It was the
beginning of the end.
“I had one or two good moments, though,” he
said, genially. “I was driving a chariot and a
team of black horses that stretched for leagues
across the desert. My body was in Thebes while
my leaders were galloping into Borne. I rolled in
my glory across the Tiber, and swung into the
Coliseum without a break. Then I heard the
voice of a multitude and the clashing of iron
gates. Csesar stood up. and turned down his
thumb.
“‘James,’ he shouted, ‘who is paying your
vast horse-feed bill ? ’ ”
Throughout his long illness Daley preserved a
cynical good humor ; his philosophy tuned his
patience. Nights of sharp agony burned him
white; his face, usually alive with ferocious
geniality and warmth, became soft as a little
child’s. To be sure, he sometimes revoltedagainst
the inevitable, and it is a p.oof that the gods are
dead since they heeded not his prayer.
On Dec. 29th I walked through the still bush ;
I had cut a track from iny bungalow to his cot-
tage. The hills were shrouded with rain, the big
red gums stood like dripping giants at the head
of the gully. From the swaying tip of a red-
leafed sapling a tiny, green-throated bird was
singing madly ; its joy seemed strangely out of
tune, its shrill delight pierced the nerves.
At Daley’s cottage I paused a moment before
walking up the path. Then the shadow of a
woman’s hand fell before me.
Eileen said, “ This morning at seven.”
I turned, coward-like, to escape the little pitiful
sobbing and the cry of a woman that hurts. And
. . . There had been no violent assault-at-arms—
only peace, white and ineffable, where. Death had
kissed him between the eyes.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.

Florence Young is the brightest feature of
“ The Girl from Kay’s,” who began to snare her
Hoggenheimer for the second time in Sydney at
Her Majesty’s on Boxing Night. Twelve months
or so back Geo. Edwardes’ imported Gaiety Go.
introduced the Girl, Madge Crichton being the
Girl. J.O.W. now presents the Girl surrounded
by a co. wtiich can sing better, but generally acts
worse. In particular, so far as the acting goes,
Hoggenheimer is a failure. Besides pervading the
piece, “ Piggy ” is the one distinctly novel note in
it; with some reservations in favor of The Girl
herself, the people of the opera consist of the
garden variety of musical comedy maiden, crusty
bachelor, and sniggering dude. Hoggenheimer, as
Huntley did him, was quite a possible modern
freak ; Lauri’s creation is quite impossible. He is
impossible even before he opens his mouth. His
walk—when did man walk like that? It is not
the strut of one who is beastly rich and knows it,
and thinks that all the world is looking on in
admiration ; and that is the sort of man “Piggy
is. So what ought to be a clever and acute
character study is turned into a mere travesty and
nothing-in-particular. Of course Lauri knocks
some fun out of it, though no great amount;
but, in any case, “Piggy” is capable of
raising more than the occasional laugh—he
is too good a character to be used merely for
that purpose. Florence Young, however, makes
amends for that. She is a rather more sober Girl
than Sydney has seen before, yet a convincing
one ; and her songs—differing widely in character
—are conspicuous successes even in a performance
in which the singing all through is excellent.
Miss Margaret Thomas lias made no marked pro-
gress as an actress; but in “ Love was Meant to
Make us Glad” she has, and takes, a fine oppor-
tunity to show how well she can use her voice.
Miss Rose Musgrove seemed to be spurred
on to extra vivacity on the first night
by the warmth of the welcome she got (it was her
first appearance in Sydney since her illness).
Haigh Jackson is most successful in a duet
with Miss Young, though his “ I don’t Care ” solo
is a very clever piece of difficult work; Ban took
turns out a poor crusty bachelor uncle, and so
much repetition of the “ Maxixe ” dance was
called for on the opening night that Fred Leslie
and Miss Tilly Woodlock have a good case against
the audience for cruelty to animals.
1 * * * *
It is-a characteristic of the British race and
its descendants that they like to be harrowed at
an alleged time of whole-souled and universal joy,
and judging by the fact that he presented “Two
Little vagabonds” for a Christmas treat, Edwin
Geach knows his public to a nicety. This story
of how a boy named Dick lived a vagabond life for
seven years, and stole a purse in the interval, was
put on at Sydney Criterion the night The Bul-
letin went to press, and by the time the public
holds this issue in its trembling hand, and gazes
at it with its dissipated Christmas eye, the
great bulk of the people will have seen
Beatrice Holloway in the part of Dick, and have
been much gratified thereby. As a delineator of
small boys who have struck a hard world old man
Holloway’s gifted daughter is hard to beat; her
only drawback is that she never looks starved,
emaciated, and utterly crushed. Her cheeks have
a roundness that all the arts of the stage cannot
destroy, and in her voice there is a robustness that
breathes of the good times that always come at
the end of melodrama, no matter how high-class
it may be. To those who like a smattering of
human feeling in their stage woe “ Two Little
Vagabonds” should appeal, as it contains rather
more of that much desired quality than is usual.
* * *- *
“ Sinbad the Sailor ” proved a good holi-
day attraction at Sydney Royal, and on nights
when the atmosphere was like that of Hades
with the safety valve tied down the theatre was
crowded with folk who perspired cheerfully and
had everything damp about them but their spirits.
The gay irresponsibility of “Sinbad” is its chief
claim to consideration. You can turn in to see it
at any time and pick up the thread of the argu-
ment, and follow it in the sure hope of striking
a joke, and a ballet, and a bit of tumbling,
and a bright chorus, and a bevy of bright legs.
And if the dog that one goes out to see between
the acts proves to be a sort of dog that Strays far,
and so you stretch out the interval to half an hour,
it doesn’t matter. You’ve missed some good
things; but there are many other good things.
This hot weather dogs have a habit of straying.
* * * *
The last days of the Great Thurston at
Sydney Palace were brightened by the near
proximity of another magician, who with Asiatic
magnificence of effect concealed his identity.
Thurston used to cause fowls, pigeons, and a pig
or two to appear suddenly from next to nowhere.
The new magician caused them just as suddenly
to disappear and vanish right off the premises.
Thurston’s audience knew that the creatures were
always in existence, but no one can say the same
since the other fellow has caused them to dis-
appear. Australia is a wonderful country, and a
place where a Napoleon in any line is liable to
meet his Waterloo.

Eight English Primroses continue to lie on
their shoulders and wave their sixteen stems
towards the ceiling of Sydney Tivoli amid great
applause. Australia hasn’t hitherto seen eight
sprightly girls upended in this way ; the most it
has seen at a time is one, and she has generally
been letting out with her toes at a large person in
blue named O’Kelly and defying him to take her
to the station. The Bulletin doesn’t know
whether “Dansedesjambesenl’air” wassuggested
by the rush which always follows when even one
Primrose throws herself into this attitude ; but it
has been a powerful attraction, anyhow. The
rest of the Tivoli bill remains the same bill that it
was last week, with A. G. Spry, the two Bells,
and Boy Glen in the largest type.
* # * *
Mary Ann, who polished up Lancelot’s
boots so c irefully in Sydney, a while ago, is just
now putting the same shine on them at Her
Majesty’s, Melbourne. Mary Ann, as was stated
aforetime, is the incredibly guileless maid of all
work at Mrs. Leadbatter’s boarding-house, and
Lancelot is her best boarder. From the landlady 8
point of view he is the worst boarder, because he
doesn’t pay up. Lancelot. is a musical genius
with a future before him, but his soul shrinks
from the composition of popular songs and sale-
able waltzes. His friend Peter, a musical tea-
dealer, has been guilty of a song which yielded a
profit of £4OOO, but Lancelot’s talent is cast in a
Wagnerian mould, and every time he hears the
tawdry strains of “Kiss Me Good Night, Dear
Love,” proceeding from the bowels of a barrel
organ, he rolls his eyes in fine frenzy, and feels
his position keenly. For he is in the position of
not being able to give the organist a penny to go
away. Genius, as everybody knows, must get
inspiration from somewhere, and Lancelot finds it
in Mary Ann, his humble idolatress. He acquires
the habit of kissing her on account of her inno-
cence, and a sort of pastoral symphonic affection
grows up between them. The greenest fields are
not more verdant than the boarding-house slavey.
Lancelot, having consented to take £4oon account
for a promise to write a barrel-organ tune, is about
to retire into the country with Mary Ann (which
would entail the loss of her simplicity), when she
is suddenly converted into a millionairess, where-
upon Lancelot runs away and leaves the young
woman without a stain upon her character.
* * * •
The last act reveals Mary Ann as a mil-
lionairess of six years’ standing, bright and
polished, like the boots brushed in happier years.
She spends her time seemingly in watching from
afar the brilliant career of her long-lost Lancelot,
who has shaken the musical world with a grand
opera. He knows nothing of her career, neither
does Peter, his friend. The two men happen to be
invited to a manor hall where Marion (once Mary
Ann) is staying, and as the result of a hasty offer
of marriage from the amazed Lancelot she quits
the drawing-room in her stylish Marion costume,
and returns five minutes later disguised as the
original Mary Ann. The story doesn’t, state
whether she always carried the housemaid’s cos-
tume with her luggage or borrowed it on the
premises for the special occasion. Anyhow, this
quick change gives a distinctly vaudeville character
to the romantic farcical comedy of “ Merely Mary
Ann.”

The present commentator finds it im-
possible to take Miss Tittell Brune seriously in
the name-part. She seems to be acting it under
natural disadvantages. Her attempts at simple
pathos in speech and manner are intelligent but
unconvincing, and the most unconvincing thing
about her was Lancelot, as played by Douglas
Gerrard. The Peter in the present production is
Boy Redgrave. In all other important respects
the cast is the same as it was in Sydney, and with
the valuable assistance of Miss Vaughan, Miss
Irby Marshall, Oily Deering, Atholwood and
others the comedy goes very well.

At the Princess’s the Gilbert and Sullivan
Opera Co. was to revive its cheerful perform-
ance of “The Gondoliers” on Wednesday for a
season of three nights only. Up to time of
writing “Pinafore” has been thankfully received
by fairly large audiences. Miss Dolly Castles last
week took her place as Josephine, but that winsome
little woman is not singing so well as she did, and
her voice seems in need of a rest from active
service. “ Princess Ida ” is billed for Saturday
next. There are eight more of the G. and S.
operas to be produced this month, including
“ Utopia Limited ”.

At the Bijou a respectable amateur pro-
duction of “A Gaiety Girl” passed quietly away
last Saturday, and was to be followed on Monday
by a revival of “In Town,” the best thing The
Players have put upon the market thus far. But
the market for amateur musical comedy is not
brisk, and the corridors of the Bijou are stained
with managerial tear-drops.
*X X X
Bland Holt’s latest and loosest melodrama
is proceeding until further notice at Melbourne
Royal. It strikes one as being an awful reflection
on the moral character of Sydney, and it is actu-
ally deplorable for the reason that one of the
murderers is named “Jim.” Up to now the Aus-
tralian Jim has worn a halo of honesty. The bap-
tismal name of James is always open to suspicion,
like Matthew, Mark and Luke, but the hand of
Jim was never raised against a fellow 7 creature ex-
cept on behalf of a heroine, until Bland Holt
broke the rule. It is comforting to remember that
the other murderer in this case is known as Jonas
about the deadliest name in the annals of melo-
drama. Jim should be changed to Jabez. Jonas
and Jabez would be two splendid villains.

Best and Baker’s Living Pictures are
accountable for a good deal of business at the
Athenaeum Hall. A conjuror is thrown in with
the Biograph, and the quickness of the wizard’s
hand easily deceives the eye that has been study-
ing the question as to whether Best and Baker’s
pictures are free from all suspicion of flicker.
*X * *
The only trouble with Bostock and
Wombwell’s Circus of Varieties is to make Mel-
bourne fully aware of the fact that the circus
doesn’t cirque. There are no piebald steeds or
feats in the arena at B. and W.’s canvas establish-
ment. The show 7 is of the Rickards order, and it
transpires on a weather-beaten “fit-up” which
looks old enough to have been the theatre of the
original Wombwell, or the first Bostock, at the be-
ginning of last century. A well-stocked menagerie
diffuses powerful odors in another tent, and a
vast elephant assists the management by pushing
things into position with his nose. The entertain-
ment has the extraordinary virtue of modesty.
It puts no frills on its merits. A troupe of
Japanese performers, who change their programme
twice a week, are advertised as the “star” turn,
but some of the others are more amusing, and
none of them stay too long in evidence. On the
whole a very likeable little show, presented in an
unfamiliar way.

A strong, level programme at the Opera
House was strengthened on Saturday by two
more “turns.” Barnard’s Piccaninny Marionettes
came to hand, also a pair of song and dance
sisters whose dancing is quaintly acrobatic. The
Jackson Family is a popular success as usual, and
the large assortment of other people is so gener-
ally satisfactory that the cyclists are confronted
by a full house when they take the floor at
10.45 p.m. or later.

AT POVERTY POINT.
The chairman of Melbourne Board of Health mentioned
lately that the Board had at last come to ah “agree-
ment ” with the proprietor of the Bijou Theatre concern-
ing the alterations necessary for putting it on fair terms
with other Melbourne theatres as regards the risk of fire.
The alterations are already overdue, it seems, but lessee
Wilson is to have another month’s grace, and in the
meantime the house is open for business. Presumably
the Board arranged with Providence that no fire should
take place during the Xmas season.
“Lil” : The advertisements wrongly stated that Ken-
ningham played Ralph Rackstraw, in the latest produc-
tion of “ Pinafore,” for the first time in Melbourne. He
was Rackstraw to Carrie Moore’s Josephine, when the
old Alexandra re-opened as Her Majesty’s, in May, 1900,
just about the time the verb “to maffick” came into
existence. The occasion is impressed upon my memory
by the fact that mafficking hooligans ran the Bourke-street
tram-car, on which 1 was proceeding’ to Her Majesty’s,
off the rails, and when I reached the theatre I was
regarded as a pro-Boer because I laughed, instead of yell-
ing earnest approval, every time Rackstraw uttered one
of the “patriotic” sentiments that Gilbert wrote with
his tongue in his cheek. The humorless theatre-goer,
ready to sing “ Gor’ Save ” on every occasion or none, did
his best to make all plays a weariness of the flesh at that
trying period.
Just before sailing for Australia, one English vocalist,
now touring these parts, afforded a fairly cheeky instance
of the professional spleen that exists in England against
the imported voice. The lady was introduced to a newly-
arrived singer, and informed that she was from Sydney.
“ Oh, indeed,” she snorted, “then you’re another Aus-
tralian, come over to take the bread out of our mouths. ’
And this English chanteuse had already signed a contract
to go to Australia and take as much bread as possible out
of the mouths of the hungry congregation to be seen
daily outside Sydney and Melbourne music emporiums.
“Kodak” at a literary society One night I found
four men gathered round a bottle of water. They were
talking about Literature, and on the other side of the
bottle 50 women sat on hard chairs and glared at the
men and one another’s back hair. They also whispered
like a conference of serpents. One of the men rose to
address the 50 women. He leant on the bottle of water,
and spoke in hollow tones that suggested the clatter of a
tom-tom in a jungle of dead subjects. He assured the
50 women on the hard chairs that he couldn’t make up
his mind about Hamlet. All the women rustled sym-
pathetically. Evidently they too had tried to make
up their minds. Then he told them he had been
worrying about Hamlet for forty years—and the
women were so silent I could hear Shakspeare s
ghost trying to prevent the shade of Hamlet from hurling
Yorick’s skull at him. None of the women wanted to
appear too enthusiastic over anything that dated back
over 40 years, so the speaker sat down in the midst of a
silence so chill that it made him sneeze twice. Then,
after a young person had read a long paper entitled
“ Thoughts on Thoreau,” the secretary put one hand on
the bottle of water and told us that he never felt lonely
in a forest. He also told us that all animals were glad in
the spring, and called our attention to the fact that
the branches of the gum were arranged like umbrellas
—several umbrellas, arranged in a sort of tier
that was to sav, one above the other, and so forth.
He said that if we looked at the next gum-tree we came
to we would see for ourselves. Then he suddenly and
unaccountably subsided, and the four drew closer
together round the sacred bottle of water, and told each
other secrets in hoarse whispers that could be heard by
all. The fifty women tittered and whispered, until one
of the four rose wearing an expression of fearful gloom.
He said he was sorry to inform the meeting that several
members who had promised papers on various subjects
had disappointed the bottle of water, and that he would be
compelled to close the meeting—unless some of the ladies
present cared to discuss what had been said. The 50
women all tittered in chorus, collected their gamps, and
got up from the hard chairs. I fell down stairs with the
dreadful conviction that I’d dropped yet another precious
night into the abyss of Lost Time.
Citizens Samuel Williams and Charles Hartmann, of
America, the hypnotised and hypnotiser in the living burial
at Princes Court, Melbourne, are two cheery youths who
make no claim to scientific knowledge. Doctors on the
committee for the show say that the hypnotic cohdition in
which Williams apparently lay was genuine. Williams’
awakening took place six days after his plantation, and
he could not be stirred until offers of beer were yelled at
him. Then he sat up all at once.
“Melb.”: Best and Baker’s Christmas Day Biograph
whipped all for piety. A real live Christian was crucified
—back to audience—before a real Nero. With a sudden
flash a joint of meat was substituted for the Christian.
A real lion came and tore it in pieces, running away
with a large fragment. Horrid!
“ 0.K.” : An amusingly audacious item in the Bostock
and Wombwell’s Circus show is “The Human Ostrich.”
This downy old bird is of the British breed, and you find
him, or many another like him, giving his gastronomical
performance on the sands at popular sea-side resorts. He
makes a frugal meal of tallow candles and coal, washes it
down with draughts of kerosene (?), and then goes through
a form of setting himself on fire, internally. Having
produced some whiffs of flame by blowing through a tube
the “ Ostrich ” goes around the tent selling the story of
his life for a penny. It is a brief but humorous narrative,
stating that his father—“ a surgeon in the French Army ”
—trained him in his childhood to eat small quantities of
“coal nails, glass, sawdust, paraffin oil, poison, &c.”
There is sadness in the thought that the genial-looking
showman will probably cause a revival of this ancient
“ show ” industry in Australia, but on his personal merits,
as a relic of English barbarism, the “ Ostrich ” is an
interesting creature.
“D.”: As a playgoer who pays his " way, I have a
substantial grievance against the Melbourne papers which,
by saying kind things about the performance of “The
Gaiety Girl,” led me into buying two circle seats in
the belief that I would be somewhat entertained for three
hours or so. I am as much the victim of false pretences
as the man who buys sanded sugar or ham made of
hickorv. This pretence of dealing generously with
amateurs is all very well, but surely audiences have some
rights 1 The amateurs—many of them more or less pro-
fessionals, by the way—charge professional fees, and are
out on the make as well as to minister to a diseased
vanity, and it seems to me that if the newspapers won’t
warn amusement-seekers of the terrors that await them,
they might at least abstain from giving countenance and
assistance to a conspiracy of deceit. At a time when
legislation is being devised with great care and caution to
protect the public from turnips and tomatoes in its jam,
why should an entrepreneur be permitted to impose a
banal show upon the people with the assistance of
inaccurate advertisements and a gaudy label that is a mis-
representation in a dozen colors ?
“TD.”: London’s a ticklish place to be in if you’re
running for public favor. At the Green Room Club, the
other day, I heard the reason for George Anson’s failure
to secure the firm hold in London to which his excellence
as a comedian would certainly entitle him. “ Nobody could
play Eccles half as well,” affirmed a manager with whom I
was talkino- “ but he once did the unpardonable thing for
an actor who seeks the suffrages of the public here. Some
trouble was on in one of the theatres one night, and
Anson got annoyed with the audience and rebuked them
from the stage. Even after his years, of absence in Aus-
tralia this was remembered against him by the majority
of managers, and it was lucky for him that he got any
show in London at all.”
“Alba”: Wombwell’s menagerie, at present in Aus-
tralia, was noted throughout England for its man-eating
variety of lions. It was common enough in the old days
to see three or four keepers with arms and heads bandaged
poking about the cages. As a travelling menagerie it was
only equalled by Myers’ and Sanger’s. Thirty years ago
the entry of Wombwell’s into one of the big provincial
towns was a kind of Roman triumph not permitted in
later times. Old Dick Wombwell, the father of the show,
met his end in a manner worthy of a gladiator. One night
he was awakened by a series of roars coming from
a cage occupied by “Wallace,” a big forest-bred
lion, "slipping from bed, he discovered that a full-grown
Bengal tiger had effected an entrance into the lion s
quarters, and in two seconds India and Scotland were at
death-grips. The old man had no sporting interest in
such contests ; he only saw about £3OO worth of stripes
and mane rending itself to pieces. Releasing an elephant
he put his trust in a forlorn hope, and swung back the
gate of the lion house. In a flash the two
monsters rolled out to the ground. Seizing a red-hot bar
from the fire-cage, old Dick planted himself between
the elephant’s forelegs and rammed his bar between
the tiger’s jaws. Freed from the Bengal s gnp, hon
Wallace sprang at the elephant, and was promptly
slogged to the ground by a lump of trunk. me
elephant is more in dread of a tiger than of a lion, ana as
it swung round to avoid the disengaged Bengal, Dick
Wombw'ell received about seven inches of tusk througn
his spine. It was a night of disaster. Wombwell died
instantly. The camels and zebras enclosed within tne
menagerie stampeded round and round trampling to
death the Hainan gibbons and other sundries chained in
the centre. A family of monkeys came to a baa ena
under the feet of the vicious oonts. A brawny ourang-
outang raced up the bars of a panther house and got ms
forearm torn away. The keepers had to shoot the tiger
to prevent it mauling the horses and camels. All tins
happened 40 years ago. The proprietors could never
induce the elephants to camp on the old site again.
“T.D.”: More will be heard of Amy Castles this
winter season in the Great Fog than of any other singer,
“arrived” or to arrive. Usually, London is regarded as
a very fine place in which to get an occasional neap ot
press notices with which to sustain a reputation, but tne
big singers get their fees in the provinces, and are con-
tent to accept smaller terms when they sing in the
hub.” But the Australienne was so besieged for London
concerts at full fees immediately after her initial success
that she should have very little time to spare for P ro y in :
cial engagements for months to come. She has booked
10 dates in the next two months for the Queen s Hall
alone, whereas the usual artist is mighty glad to get an
appearance there once or twice in the year.
The costume of the Modern Milo grows less, and
audiences roll up in inverse ratio to the drapery, mere
will doubtless be an addition to the chiffons when she
shows in the country where the Lord Chamberlain looks
after these things, but when Gay Paree is reached it. will
probably be gladdened by the sight of the fair Milo,
almost entirely without what old Parkes used to term
Hadventitious Haids. Milo, by the way, is said to spend
three hours after each show scraping the white paint
from her fair form, but this doesn’t seem a probable lie.
“ T.D.” : When Amy Castles appeared in London three
or four years ago, she was advertised to a prodigious
extent, and many spiteful things were said about her in
consequence. This time Amy appeared merely as the
support of another artist, and Van Dyck, the great
tenor, had been brought over from the Continent to make
his only London appearance for the winter season. Amy
was only advertised in the small, ordinary way of the
average concert artist. There cannot be any doubt now
as to the genuine kind of success she has achieved, the
most authoritative writer at the concert was Baughan,
of the Daily News, and his signed notice, which
shelved the unfortunate recital-giver, devoted itself
almost entirely to Australia’s Amy. It was Baughan who
said that the Castles’voice “reminds one in timbre of
Melba’s, but has more power of expression, and its quality
is more varied.” He also called her “ the soprano of the
future.” The evening dailies hail Amy as “ the new
Jenny Lind.” In fact, in all the years which have passed
since Melba first came to town, no reputable critic has
ventured to acclaim any newcoming singer as Amy
Castles has been acclaimed.
Melbourne Age doesn’t seem to be able to make up its
mind about Little Buttercup. Some time ago it objected
savagely to Dorothy Vane’s presentment (afterwards
changed) of an old woman lavishly adorned with wrinkles,
and now it slates Celia Ghiloni for leaving the wrinkles
out. Studying the plot of the play, it is hard to see how
Little Buttercup could be much less than 60, but a com-
promise of views as to her appearance might easily be
arranged. In the first act, Buttercup would appear with
all her wrinkles on ; and, after removing them in the in-
terval, she could soliloquise, on a cash basis, thus : “ Such
is the effect of using Madame ’s marvellous slan-
food ; small boxes, 2s. 9d. ; reductions on taking a quan-
tity.”
After so favorably impressing London audiences and
critics by her fine voice and engaging platform manner,
Sydney contralto Maud Dairyrnple struck a patch of bad
luck, in the form of an attack of bronchitis, just when
engagements were at their thickest and the guineas were
beginning to roll in with great fury. Miss Dalrymple
was to have appeared at one ot the Chappell ballad
concerts, but this had to go by the board, together with
several other excellent engagements. At latest, however,
she had pulled round again, and was getting ready to
conquer fresh audiences in the Heavy Fog.
Some jewellery that formerly belonged to Wagner’s
patron, Louis ll.’ of Bavaria, recently found its way into
a London auction room. The lot included bracelets
adorned with scenes from Wagner’s music-dramas. Louis
frequently ordered performances of these plays, at which
he was the sole auditor, and even he was curtained off
from the view of the actors. Eventually, he went mad,
allowing anti-Wagnerites to draw cruel inferences. Louis
had a fancy for acting the swan-drawn Lohengrin on a
lake in the palace grounds, and in the same, lake he
drowned himself and his medical attendant. His foolish
grandfather, Louis 1., let the country get into a lovely
muddle while he was engaged in making love to Lola
Montez, who for a time was practically ruler of Bavaria.
The present King, Otho (Louis ll.’s brother), is also mad.
In fact, this particular group of royalties may be described
as “The Whole Mad Familv.”
“ O’Fipp ”: I fancy that either the Anglo-Jap alliance
or recent piebald musical comedies must have something
to do with a phenomenon to be seen lately in various Aus-
tralian theatres—to wit, a craze that has seized on some
female playgoers for raising themselves by sitting with one
leg tucked under them. First the big-hat nuisance spoilt
a man’s view, then padding and puffing of hair replaced it,
and now there is this added infliction. I tremble at tbe
thought of a full audience of white Australian women
going the whole Japanese monkey, and taking their
dramatic nourishment while seated geisha-wise.
“ T.D.”: Have lately, at London concerts, had many
encounters with the compositions of one Richard Strauss,
and have seen stars and come away afflicted with much
headache. Strauss is an incomprehensible person with
more discord to the square inch than a battalion of Irish
Tommies on a Patrick’s night jamboree, but when he likes
he can coo as gently as any sucking dove. His “ Death
and Transfiguration ” has already been performed once
or twice Australianwards. But anyone who wants to
escape lunacy had better hold off his “Domestic Sym-
phony.” The opening and the close are both rather grace-
ful music, but the attempts to describe the bathing of an
infant and other domestic crudities are the wildest farrago
of arrantly unmusical bosh. But I suppose some 10 years
hence would be about time enough to give advice to Aus-
tralian musical societies about how to avoid the produc-
tion of the “Domestic Symphony” with its struggles to
set the soap to music, and its wild outbreaks significant
of clothes hanging on the back fence.
« Lii ” • How fond the mummer is of posing as a painter
of pictures ! Hardly a month goes by without some
imported magazine reproducing a photograph of an
eminent actor devoting a few leisure moments to the
production of a painting that the magazine-writer deems
fit to place beside one of Rossetti’s. The work of painting
seems to take place in a neat drawing-room, where every-
thing is beautifully arranged, and the actor thinks too
much of his palette to soil it. As far as one can judge,
the photographer supplies room, material, and picture,
and the actor is responsible only for the prettily-parted
hair and the supposedly artistic necktie, which any real
painter would shudder at. Even if the mummer does
paint anything more than his face there is no necessity
for proclaiming the fact per camera. As his masterpiece
usually appears to be of the variety that the time-
payment shops vainly try to sell at 12s. 6d., he ought
rather to hide his guilty secret.
“ T.D.” : For the first time in my life I have heard Patti,
who nowadays keeps her end up with just one burst of
song in the vear. Her voice is sufficiently sound in
quality yet to enable her to sing things of the big
“ Ernani Involami” order; it is when she gets down to
trifles like “Home, Sweet Home,” and the old order of
British ballad, that she fails to carry matters through
quite smoothly ; also she tires so easily that four songs
are really as good as a feast. But the quality is still to be
heard in the voice ; not fluty like Melba’s, but with the
mao-ical sweetness of a nightingale and the something
more that arises from the fact that she is a human being
rather chan an animated vocal machine. Crowds still
flock to hear the Patti ; but for that matter the super-
fluous Albani is still making good fees at big concerts,
although everybody admits that it is long since anything
like ecstacy could be derived from listening to her.
Albani nowadays benefits from the fact that she was
always hail-fellow-well-met with “-professionals,” and
willing to help in time of need. So when a soprano
happens to be wanted. somebody is sure to suggest
“ What about Albani V She’s a good sort And she books
the job forthwith.

The -303 service rifle is a splendid example
of Australia’s military effectiveness. The am
has, during the last few months, earned something
of the reputation of the old trade guns with which
the nigger has been helped to destroy himself.
The constant recurrence of bursting rifles still
continues, the latest case being reported from
Castlemaine (Vic.), where several competitors in
a rifle match had hairbreadth escapes. In spite
of enthusiastic official declarations to the effect
that there are two bullets in some of the car-
tridges, and that this strange misadventure causes
the trouble, the general impression among experts
is that cordite corrosion sets up inside the barrels,
reducing the rifling to such an extent that the
bullet strips, leaving the hard nickel case behind.
The next shot causes the damage. Anyhow Aus-
tralia knows that it is facing its possible enemies
with a very excitable gun, and that it may yet
have to defend its hearth and home with the butt
end.
WATER ON A DUCK'S BACK.
HIS NIBS (fiercely, to acquaintance who has re-
fused him the loan of five shillings): “You’re
a bloomin’ tight-fisted, lop-eared, blear-eyed
bounder, that's what you are!”
THE OTHER BLOKE : “ The idea of talking to me
like that; can't you see I’m in a bad humor? ”
The petty vanity that impels second-rate
public persons to label everything within sight by
their names is in evidence in Melbourne again,
where Saclise, the stop-gap Minister of Education,
is endeavoring to have the old Friendly Societies’
Gardens, recently resumed by the State as a State
schools reserve, called “ Sachse Park.” Just
alongside this area is that portion of the Yarra
which appears on Government maps as “ Taverner
Reach.” People never call it “ Taverner Reach,”
and whenever they learn the proper designation of
the strip of moisture they express surprise.
Some of the suburban councils round Melbourne
carry this ridiculous practice to the length of
changing the names of their streets every elec-
tion, so that people who live in Maginnis-street
know that their friends won’t be able to find them
if Smith defeats Maginnis, because the place will
become Smith-street ; while Bridget-lane, off
Maginnis-street (named, after Mrs. Maginnis), will
become Sarah Jane-street after Mrs. Smith, or
Polly Ann Alley after the little Smithess of the
family.
Poor old Cole, of Melbourne Book Arcade,
is again buzzing greatly, like a huge blue fly in a
bottle, about the necessity of admitting plenty of
niggers to Australia. His latest publication sets
forth that “ everywhere throughout the world the
black people occupy the hot countries.” Which—
when one considers that the Persian is nearly
white, and so is his descendant, the Parsee of
Bombay, while a coal-black race of aborigines
used to plod among the snow and bitter winter
slush of Tasmania—is hard to swallow. Then
Cole goes on to say that “brown, olive, or red
people occupy the warm countries ” —though
anyone might doubt the fact when he meets the
brown Indian on snow-shoes on the road to Klon-
dyke, and the dark red Thibetan with ten
pairs of trousers on in the bitter cold
14,000 feet above sea-level. Cole quotes a
lot of unlucky evidences that the white
man becomes black in the tropics—the most
unlucky being the alleged black Portuguese
of Ceylon. Evidently he didn’t drop to the fact
that these particular Portuguese were always
black. The early Portuguese colonist of Ceylon
was a devout person, and every one of him strove
to capture at least one little black nigger and save
his soul by dragging him into the church by the
wool. When he was dragged in he was mostly
baptised by the name of his benefactor, conse-
fluently the oldest and most heroic names in
Portuguese history now hang out in the native
quarter of Colombo over shops that look like hen-
coops struck by lightning, and inside there is a
nigger who always was a nigger, like his fathers
before him.
. Cole also quotes the alleged black Jews of
Malabar, who, like the alleged black Jew r s of
Cochin China, were probably only nigger converts,
but about the almost white Jews of the blazing
Sahara he doesn't appear to say anything. But,
after all, even if all his nigger instances were
proved up to the hilt, they don’t seem to make
any difference. If the nigger became black and
woolly through living in Africa, let him remain
black and woolly in Africa. We don’t object. If
we are going to become black and woolly through
living in Sydney and Brisbane—well, it will be an
interesting process, and only a fool would spoil such
an experiment by introducing ready-made niggers
who, by mixing with the white population, would
make if impossible a few centuries hence to decide
whether it vyas the climate that made us black
and woolly or not. A man who firmly believes
that the world has a chance to witness the
phenomenon of a white race turning black without
mixing with black races—turning black simply on
its own and who would deliberately spoil the
show, as Cole apparently wants to do, must be a
descendant of the person who burned the library
of Alexandria.

Suss : A particularly foolish piece of
grovel is to be seen any day in the streets of
Melbourne. In the middle of the heaviest traffic
in Flinders or Collins or Swanston streets,
a gaudily caparisoned man on a prancing
horse suddenly appears and blows shrilly
on a whistle. Instantly all the bobbies
within hearing turn into lunatic sema-
phores, and stop all the traffic. That
part of the world stands still for a
w minutes because the Governor or
the Governor-General is coming. When
the vice-regal chariot has passed, the
world is permitted to revolve again. This
is done by virtue of an order from the Chief
Commissioner of Police, who directs all
constables to give the sole right of the road
to the vice-regal equipages. On the other
hand, the Fire Brigade has to take pot
luck. -there is no right of way for it.
I ire-engines, hose-carts, and all the other
paraphernalia of fire-fighting are driven at
break-neck speed through the streets and no
nobby stops the traffic. It is only the
hideous screams and shouts of the drivers
and firemen that prevent serious accident.
.Some day a fire-engine will go bolting
a trara - car full of people, and then
all Melbourne will get up and ask “ Why ?”
1 n the meantime even the fire-engines have
to stop to let the Govern >r go past.

the raw travel on a Melbourne workman’s tram
I hese run from the city at intervals, are half-
price, and the workman is conspicuous by his
absence. There is no room for him. The lady
who has been shopping, the said lady’s parcels,
the highly-dressed, lowly-paid city youth and the
fat gentleman and his beetling waistcoat take up
the available space. Then when the conductor
collects the fares ! The outward calm but inward
l°y Wlt “ which the city youth grabs his three
ha pence change, the lofty nonchalance with
which lat pockets the coppers, the fierce gleam
u j ? y , l / 1 r t ie shopping lady’s eye as she hands up
her half fare, and the suppressed groan of anguish
or the man who, taken by surprise, has handed up
a thieepenny ticket, and so loses all pecuniarv
advantage; form a pleasing study. Personally I
am never taken by surprise by a workman’s tram.
1 wait for one.

The French mademoiselle who goes into
foreign lands to teach the uncouth foreign kid to
s f ‘‘ OU1 ’’ ,s a pathetic little figure in the midst
of the .Russian revolution. According to the
cable, no less, than 300 of her are at Riga, asking
for protection or the means of getting away, and
in great alarm, meanwhile, lest her chiffons should
be bombarded. Revolution is a great coarse
animal with large, muddy, trampling feet, and
poor little mademoiselle is being bumped this wav
and that in the rush.
SOUGHT AFTER.
“ Well, Well! So your old manager's gone away since I was here last. Where's he gone to?"
“ There's no clue yet, sir."
Justice Real, a jury, large quantities of
barrister, about half-a- ton of solicitor, and enough
witnesses to make ten casks of tallow if boiled
down, lately perspired for 14 days at Brisbane,
debating whether Francis Joseph Gavegan was or
was not wrongfully dismissed from the Federal
public service. Then four puzzled, weary laymen
went out of Court with a list of 25 questions to
answer. After about seven hours they came back
with some blanks, some questions answered
“No,” and many answered “Yes.” To the
damage query they said, briefly, “£500.” Inci-
dentally they said that Gavegan was sent to
Bauhinia Downs as a punishment; that it was an
unfit place to which to send a married man ; that
the defendant wilfully disobeyed orders; was
absent without leave ; and was guilty of wilful
neglect of duty; but that at the time of his
enforced resignation he was willing to discharge
his duties. Plaintiff’s barrister then moved for
judgment, but someone else suggested that, for
the purpose of any appeal, it should be deemed
that the jury’s findings were delivered on the Day
of Judgment. The tired Court sniggered at this,
and someone suggested that what was meant was
“ the day on which the Judge delivers judgment.”
Then the Bar agreed that the judgment applica-
tion should be argued after the vacation. But
there is so much appeal ahead that it might be
better for both parties to agree that that joke
about the Day of Judgment was a very good joke,
and then for the Federal authority to take Francis
Joseph out for a drink and ask him if he will
accept £lOO and call it square.
The Dreadful Respectability of Melbourne
has shrieked greatly at the prospect of seaside
camps during the belated summer, and “Pater-
familias ” and other well-worn letter writers have
been “viewing with alarm” a repetition of last
year’s holiday-making, when Melbourne first took
its fine beaches seriously. The prevalence of
camps has been denounced as un-British, the in-
sidious approach of the “Continental Sabbath”
was heralded, and frequent references were made
to “our wives and daughters.” One particularly
shocked person wrote to say that on one occasion
last summer he saw men and women actually
bathing together on a Sunday, and implied that
such things will drive capital out of the country.
While there is some truth in the fact that many
of last year’s camps were not all they should have
been, the implication that mixed bathing should
be prohibited is just another phase of the old
doctrine which led the early- Kerristian to pull
down all the baths erected by the clean Pagan.
The gospel of Dirt has already forced a large
proportion of the people living about Melbourne
to forego baths they would delight in. The ass
that is called Law prohibits open-air bathing within
certain hours, no matter how far one may
be from public inspection. It is difficult
for the people of a place like Sydney
to credit that Melbourne possesses such a
perverted sense of decency that it summons and
fines persons who venture into the Bay or the
river in full-size neck-to-knee bathing costumes
during the hours of light. A man who falls into
the water off St. Kilda pier in his frock-coat and
belltopper has to scramble out and conceal himself
before a policeman comes, unless he wants to be
locked up. The law was evidently framed to
apply to nude bathers, but it is directed against all,
so that no cool, pleasant wash takes place in the
open water, except at a distance of at last 10 miles
from the city, and even then it isn’t quite a legal
proceeding.

The King of the Belgians—hoary old
profligate!—is said to have lately added a
morganatic wife to his long list of lady intimates,
his new “missis” being the daughter of a care-
taker at one of the royal palaces. A baby brought
this left-handed alliance into print. King
Leopold, a widower since 1902, is the scandalous
person to whom Queen Alexandra—as Princess of
Wales—ref used admission inttf' the circle of family
grievers what time her mother-in-law, Victoria,
lay dying. The moment he sent word from
Brussels that he was coming along to pay
his last respects to his august relative the
ordinarily unassertive Alexandra nipped his
intention in the bud. Royalty allows a wide lati-
tude for morality in reigning sovereigns, but
Leopold of the Belgians has exceeded the limits,
W. T. Stead denounced him years ago as the bo- 3
recipient of Maiden Tribute from New Babylon,
and until quite lately his habits were so notorious
that when he visited a German watering place
with his youngest daughter the passers-oy, not
knowing the lady by sight, audibly referred to her
as “ another of Leopold’s ‘ tarts. ’ ” At the age of
70 His Majesty is possibly a model husband to his
morganatic Maudie. Everything—even virtue—
comes to him who waits.

Those unfortunate public servants who
have to make searches in the cellars of the Vic.
Titles Office, where are kept original Acts of
Parliament, powers of attorney, bills of sale,
records of inquests, records of land transactions’
registrations, etc., are just now beseeching Bent
to clean up. They say the dust hasn’t been blown
or flicked off the documents for 28 years.
* * * *
“ H.G.” : The blanky shirt was never
waved so vociferously in Victoria as at present, and
the dailies are filled with matter calculated to
conduce to religious amity. To the person who
takes his religion frivolously, it seems “extry”
that two sections of a creed supposed to be an
example of brotherly love—sections which don’t
vary one iota in essentials and only microscopically
in externals—should have any quarrel at all.
This eternal squabbling seems confined to the
Anglo-Saxon Christian. Throughout Germany,
Austria and Switzerland, where the Reformation
fights were hottest, it is quite common for the
Catholics and Protestants in the country towns to
occupy the same church—building, managing it
conjointly, and holding their services at different
hoyrs. Perhaps Australia will have the same
sense some day.
THE CHEAP SAMARITAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the season of the year
When Xmas gifts in print appear.
This is the chance for you and me
To advertise our eharitee,
And feed our brothers in distress
With small donations through the press,
Whereby we level in the sense
Of personal beneficence.
Spare naught in secret to the poor
Who beg assistance at your door,
But boldly notify the town
That Biggs is giving half-a-crown,
Or sending clothes (an ancient suit)
To help a “ home ” or “ institute” :
A fact which finds no fame unless
It strikes the public through the press.
O, mighty Press ! without thy aid
Good deeds would be but ill displayed
Which now are thrust before men’s eyes,
And draw attention from the skies,
VVhere Peter jots the figures down,
And credits Biggs with half-a-crown.
vie.
F.

‘ ‘ Homo ” : On scanning the published
prize lists of many seats of learning, it has struck
me as remarkable that the names of all those who
secured prizes for regular attendance were con-
spicuous by their absence otherwise, so I conclude
that the scholar who regularly answers the roll
call is a woodenhead as a rule. And, as I write
this, a picture on the wall facing me furnishes
corroborative testimony. It was won by the
dullard of the family for regular attendance at
school. He was never known to win anything
else in his early days, but he now has the reputa-
tion of being dead lucky at “two up” and at
shaking for drinks. If Poor Richard has been
fully reported, he evidently forgot something. I
don’t remember anything among his sayings that
meets the case. P.S.—I wish it to be distinctly
understood that I never secured any prize for
regular attendance at school.

The Bulletin has recently been shown a
sort of circular about a new book. It professes
to be signed by one “E. W. Bathurst,” of Bondi,
Sydney, and reads :
As I am preparing for publication a transcript of an
edition in relation to “Men I Have Met” whilst con-
nected amongst them, will be several of those who were
most prominently united with me, in reference to my
pioneering several mining researches (most of whose
conjunctive categories will be oppugned from personal
affinity With them), hence being the belief that from the
promulgation of cogent impressions there will be some
very interesting scientific and other reading matter for
all who will recall the distinctive points thereinto speci-
fied ; because most people must agree that some civic
ideals of aspiration are now being thought ; truly worthy
of record, especially when they are characterised by
open-minded principles whereby others by such means
,can live and learn.
It should be an interesting book when it comes out.
“Snell”: In
Little Bourke-
street, at the
back of Mel-
bourne Royal,
the other morn-
ing, I came up-
on a small knot
of indolent
Chinese and de-
cayed Euro-
peans surround-
ing a mean-
looking Mongol
who was squat-
ting in the gut-
ter, and using
the kerbstone
as a chopping-
block for cutting
up a couple of
very large fish.
Near his right
hand stood an
unclean basket
with a paper
that had been
previously in
use spread in
it; on the foot-
path was a
piece of pine
about half the
size of the lid
of a candle-
box ; and on
this the Chow
was deftly
slicing the fisn
into large
steaks with a
big chopper.
He was an
artist at the
work, and
scaled and gut-
ted each steak
as he whipped
it off. The
finished slab
was tossed into the basket.
I imagined that the fish was for his own con-
sumption, and passed on with an easy conscience,
but two hours later discovered the same heathen
delivering a plate of the same fish out of the
same basket over a front gate to a woman in
Middle Park.
* * * *
A social function in Melbourne a day or
two before Christmas is what is known as the
Christmas market, at the City Council’s emporium
in Elizabeth-street. The ordinary markets start
about 4.30 a.m., and by seven or eight all the
suppliers have gone home and the wholesale
buyers likewise; but on this one day of the year
(Saturday last year) the dealing commences about
2.30 or 3 a.m., and with the usual crowd of shop-
keeping buyers is interspersed a motley throng of
casual purchasers and sightseers Lizzies,
Flossies, boarding-house keepers, Chows,
spielers, larrikins, hawkers, dudes, and assorted
sundries. The market is too small for the
business, which sprawls all over the adjacent
streets, while sellers of flowers, fruit and
vegetables are mixed up higgledy-piggledy. Feel-
ing the poultry, splitting the peas and tasting the
cherries, one sees an eye-glassed damsel in holland
gown and pith helmet, with her escort in broad
cloth «nd Panama, cheek by jowl with a giddy
little i >ve bird in the remains of last night’s paint,
a billowy, lacy, bedraggled, second-hand dressing-
gown and a picture hat. The love-bird is invari-
ably accompanied by a serious-minded, elderly
female. Flossie is'merely out for fun, and for the
time being is the pink of propriety. A notable cir-
cumstance is the entire absence of small children—
save for an occasional market-gardener’s boy,
asleep on his father’s peas or cabbages. But there
is a superabundance of short-skirted damsels of
from 14 to 18—Heaven knows what they want
there ; they buy nothing save ice-cream, and the
colder the morning the more ice-cream they con-
sume. And an amazing conundrum is where all
the drunks come from. One can get liquor almost
anywhere in Melbourne up to 1 a.m., but even if
a man is drunk at 1 a.m., he should be at least
threatened With sobriety by four o’clock. Yet the
inebriates are more plentiful than Bent’s surplus.
* * * *
There is no saying- what a man will do
when he is really sorry. The other day a country
youth, in an aggravated condition of beer and
remorse, accosted a Melbourne constable and com-
plained of having been waylaid and robbed. He
was escorted to the watch-house and locked up as
a plain drunk. Also his pockets were turned out,
and a lot of pay-in slips and cheques on a country
branch bank were discovered. Next morning,
after paying a small tine,, he was about to
depart, when the arresting constable' wanted to
know the how and the why ,6f so many
cheques, and all about the alleged rbbbery and so
forth. Then the infant broke down and confessed
that he, being clerk in the Nathalia branch of the
“’Asia,” had annexed some £2OO that didn’t
belong to him ; that he had resigned voluntarily a
few days before, and had departed, with his em-
ployer’s best wishes, for parts unknown ; but he
now thought he had better be locked up out of harm’s
way. The constable thought so, too. Probably
the youth is sorry now. The moral is that no
king should abdicate on an empty stomach, and
that no young man should give himself up to the
police before breakfast. When anyone feels him-
self repenting in the early morning he should go
and have a souare meal. If he still repents, the
affair is probably genuine. Otherwise it is only a
hollow void.
* * # *
Christmas is the time when the inexperi-
enced person rushes down to the sea in yachts, and
the fellow who can’t swim takes his family on to the
slippery rocks for some deep-sea fishing ; it is also
the time when the fool handles the gun that gets
itself loaded in miraculous ways, and when the
simple stranger climbs on to the roof of the vicious
horse that he never met in his life before. In
Victoria two whole families were wiped out by
drowning while seeking Christmas joys, and the
list of individual deaths by horse, fire, bike, gun,
and flood during the festal week ran into columns.
Why not represent old Pa Christmas as a skeleton
with a coffin under his arm ?

“Oby”: Fifty years ago Lake George
(N.S.W.), where J. 0. Watson wants to have the
Australian capital, was called by many an inland
sea, and big efforts were made to make it live up
to the title. Political wheels carried fish over to
the waters, and there was talk of turning the
place into a resort for Sydney’s well-to-do in the
hot months. Lake George is a natural curiosity,
inasmuch as no one has been able to satisfactorily
explain where its water goes to. The photo-
graph exhibited in Sydney Works Office, the
other dav, shows the lake as something like
one of the half - filled -in waterholes in the
brickmaking area of Marrickville. But the
water returns to Lake George (that is, other
water gets there) as rapidly as it leaves—-the
trouble is that it doesn’t return often enough. I
was once wrecked on that tempestuous sea,
yet, 24 hours later, it was a pond—a little one,
looking like the haunt of the Tasmanian geese in
Sydney Centennial Park. You can see the water
pouring into Lake George from the surrounding
watersheds, but there’s no visible outlet.
* • * •
At Richmond (Vic.), the other day, the
local Bench fined a man 10s. for beating a woman
into a state of insensibility, he having punched
her until she couldn’t stand and then kicked her
all over the body. The Bench “considered the
season ’’—the season of peace on earth, goodwill
to men (not women)—and did not like to be hard
on the accused. Another way to consider the
season would be to try, by vigorous administration
of the law, to make it a time of year when a
woman would have less chance of being kicked to
death than at other times—to consider the season
for the kicked as well as for the kicker. But the
wooden-headed Bench never thinks of that.
