Deeply interesting item from an ’Ome
paper, the 16th November last
Prince Louis of Battenberg, who has been suffering
much discomfort from toothache, yesterday paid a visit
to an American dentist, and remained there an hour while
the tooth was being stopped.
He condescended to remain there instead of
leaving his face behind him and calling for it later
on.
Even Victoria’s soft-hearted Judge Eagle- (
sou can be hard when occasion arises. He almost
broke down over the huge railway defaulter,
Walsh, and expressed sorrow that he did not
possess the power to turn that frock-coated
criminal loose upon the public he had robbed ;
but when another railway man was up before the
same Judge, accused of having purloined half-a-
bottle of whisky left lying loose about the sheds
for days, his Honor administered 12 months’ hard
labor without turning a hair. The huge embezzler
got 15 months, with an express in‘unction to the
gaol authorities that he was to be kept apart from
the real bad men who steal bread, and boots, and
whisky. The man who stole the half-crown’s
worth of liquor was just shunted into gaol to herd
with anybody.

“F.C.” writes from Melbourne :
I put in Boxing Day inspecting Mordialloc and the
vicinity. It is just seven years since I was there, and I
shan’t go for another seven. In the old days the “ push ”
favored the place on holidays, and things were willing
and joyous. Now all is drab respectability. The dance
pavilion, where 'Arry or Bill was wont to catch Lizzie
round the waist and screw her through the mazy
throng, was absolutely deserted. Serious - minded
families went down by van or train, lunched,
washed the youngsters’ feet and returned full of subdued
joy at a decent hour. But just beyond Mordialloc is a
patch of foreshore where all is joy and revelry. This is
Camptown—where the campers camp, in tents, for a week
or a month, or perhaps two. Father and the grown-up sons
are in Melbourne all the week, so Camptown is mostly
peopled by women and children, and the few who
are there have gaudy times. In a mile and a half
of a narrow strip of ti-tree scrub between the Bay and the
railway are some hundreds of good-sized tents where the
families sleep and feed. The rest of the time they sit on
the sand, or bathe, or just meander round. They wear
any old things—Panamas, sun-hats tied down with veils,
semi-discarded blouses and skirts, and often no stockings.
And they bathe, mixed and otherwise. One sweet
picture was a well-developed damsel of sixteen
or so, in flesh-colored silk combinations. She
could not swim, nor did she go in further than
knee deep, but she had got herself wet somehow
and she looked clingsome and pretty. Another sketch
consisted of two grown-up girls with some smaller rela-
tives, and a couple of men friends. The ladies were
plump, with—or—legs that would have got them into the
front, row of any ballet in the world, and I should say
they had annexed their brothers’ singlets and cut
about a foot off the top. They preserved an
appearance of modesty by getting a small sister to bring
macintoshes wherewith to cover their charms while pass-
ing from the water to their tent in the ti-tree. But I
want to know how Camp town passes its evenings.
There are such limitless opportunities and spaces
for strolling round two and two, and viewing the
moon, and the Bay, and so forth. If I had
the luck to possess the affections of either of those-
two plump girls, we would—on a hot night—go for
another bath early in the evening, and I would see the
moonbeams glistening on her plump, round legs, and,
later on, we would sit in the ti-tree, and I would propose.
In camp life you feel as if you could propose to almost
any plump, wet girl—or to any dozen of them. They are
a happy-looking, careless, brown, friendly people, these
campers, and I love them.
Consider the callous cruelty of the bring-
ing home of the Christmas poultry ! It is the
custom of the thrifty householder to buy the
bird a week or two ahead of the time he is
to batter it down with plum pudding on Christ-
mas day. He takes that bird- home from
the dingy and dirty poultry shop, puts it in his
airy backyard, and feeds it as its mother would.
The bird rejoices. Its faith in human nature is
restored. It learns to sing little anthems of
praise to its new master .; to watch for his step as
he brings the plenteous and fattening food •; and
to chortle with joy at the sound of his beloved
voice. Then at il p.m. on Christmas Eve he pays
it a final visit, and as it smiles up at him, he cuts
off ita smile and its head with a blunt axe.
Australia forgot to import some of the
gaud-mayoral appendages of the dear old Ma
Country. At Newcastle-on-Tyne the Mayor lias
the right, on certain occasions, to kiss the prettiest
girl present—after paying her a sovereign. At
various other places the corporation keeps a mace,
and the Mayor frequently kisses it. Also, the
retiring Mayor of Bournemouth is obliged to kiss
his successor. Australia has mostly kept clear of
this sort of thing, though an Adelaide Mayor did
nrint a burning kiss on the hand of the Duke of
York.

THE BLACKBIRD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The blackbird sang and sang,
As he would never tire.
Then thro’ my heart there rang
This cry, “ I will aspire ! ”
I’ll sing and sing until
My song shall perfect be,
And all the world be still
In listening ecstasy.
I’ll sing of mirth and joy,
Of peace and swords in sheath,
Of truth without alloy,
Of Love and Life and Death.
Of courage in distress,
Of holy sympathy,
Of self-forgetfulness,
Of Christ upon the Tree.
Of bee and butterfly
(Oh ! sweetly will I sing),
Of May and of July,
Of swallows on the wing.
I’ll sing of flowers, and books
Whose wisdom glads the eyes,
Of girlhood’s radiant looks,
Of Spring and starry skies.
Of Summer and the rose,
Of Autumn and the mist,
Of Winter and her snows,
And skies of amethyst.
Of lover and of friend,
Of mountain, plain and deep,
And, singing to the end,
I’ll sing a song of sleep.
vie.
DOROTHY FRANCES McCRAE.

ROBEY
The WILD CAT COLUMN
The profits made by Australasian banks during
the past year (or as near to the past year as their last
published balance-sheets will let us get) show a slight
shrinkage from the figures of the previous 12 months. In
1903-4 the effect of the high rates charged for accommoda-
tion during the one or two bad seasons was still felt. The
banks at that time took heavy risks ; as it happened, two
excellent seasons followed, and their losses were not
large. In the past year their risks were much smaller,
their charges lower, and their profits therefore also
smaller ; but the business has been far sounder. The
profits have in the latter part of the year been helped by
a reduction in deposit rates ; but this has not yet had
much time to show itself. Taking for the first table the
results on a full year’s business—the 12 months covered
by the last published balance-sheets—these things show
up °

But the list shows the Bank of N.Z., Commercial of Aus-
tralia, and Q.N. in probably too favorable a light. Every
bank has some skeletons in its safe, and it spends part of
its earnings getting rid of them; what it spends it
doesn t mention, but the amount comes out of profits
before profits are disclosed. The B.N.Z. and the Com-
mercial of Australia have simply passed their old skeletons
on to separate co-ncerns, and thus their profits, as shown
in the table, are not charged with an expense which
practically every other bank has to bear. The OK’s
position is much the same. Further, some banks (the
Australasia and Union in particular), in addition to
making the ordinary provision for bad debts, &e., are
specially writing dowm premises and other accounts ; and
it they carry this beyond a certain point, it practically
means building up an internal or hidden reserve.

The Bank of New Zealand has £1,000,000 of Government
guaranteed 4 per cent, stock, repayable in 1914 ; this is
reckoned as capital, and the dividend includes £51,978
paid upon it. On capital apart from this stock the profit
(less interest on the stock) was at the rate of £2B Is per
cent. The high place of the Q.N. Bank in the list is
largely due to the fact that it has £1,377,563 of Govern-
ment deposits on which only £2 12s. 6d. per cent, interest
is paid and £942,874 old private deposits carrying no in-
terest. The capital of the Australian Mortgage and De-
posit Bank was written down from £909,979 to £193 935
during the year, and £BlO7 of reserves were written off
The worst record of the year, so far as the
worst record is visible, was made by the London Bank of
Australia, which paid away more dividends than it
earned, having to go to its miserable little reserves for
the balance. All the others put by something ; how
much or how little this list shows :

Some remarkable movements have occurred during the
year. Thus Commercial of Australia ordinary shares have
sprung- from 3s. 9d. to 9s. 3d., or to very nearly par;
while the 4 p.c. pref. shares of £lO (3 p.c. cumulative) are
quoted at £6 4s. Only 3 p.c. is being paid, and the
amount put to reserve last year was not half enough to
pay the extra 1 p.c. ; so the market is evidently looking
a very long way ahead when it values ordinaries at 9s. 3d.
Returned members of the Stinking Fish party say that
Australia has a shocking name in England ; yet note that
in the past three years Union Bank shares, mostly held
in England where the head office is, have moved up from
£37 to £5l, and Bank of Australasia shares from £B3 to £95.

* * * *
A.M.P. chairman Littlejohn, defending the
disingenuous ballot-paper issued by the A.M.P. Board
“As only a small minority of members can attend a
meeting, the board has afforded to those who cannot
attend an opportunity to express their opinion, for which
purpose the deputy-chairman and I are willing to act as
proxies and to vote as we are requested. The proxy form
warns voters to indicate their desires by intimating that
if no such desire is expressed the form will be used at
the discretion of the proxy. Can anything be more
transparently candid and straightforward than this’”
Mr. Littlejohn seems to think that a life office can be run
on the same lines as G. H. Reid runs a political contro-
versy. In the interests of candor, what would have been
the matter wi th telling voters that the free proxies they
gave to the directors would be used in favor of the ex-
tension to England, instead of concealing that vital point ?
Chairman Littlejohn says that the objections to
the proposed extension of business to Great Britain
and B’Africa are so numerous that it is impossible for
the directors to answer them. It is a frank confession,
anyway.

Business seems to have been a good deal more profitable
lately. The advances, etc., at the end of the half-year
werb only £532,573 as against £547,992 12 months ago;
yet the gross profits were £15,057 as against £14,195 in
the corresponding half of ’O4. Apparently bad debts
have been fewer, or less provision has been made for
them. The present cash balances are £162,753, against
£91,792 at Nov. 30, 1904 ; and the liquid assets represent
34 p.c. of the debts to the public—for this bank, an
unusually heavy proportion. Presuming that the old
average rate of about 28 p.c. was safe (and that depends
very largely upon the sort of people the depositors are),
tne present figures show that, like most other banks, the
National of Tasmania could do with some more business.
And the sort of business it wants is good commercial
paper; and the best way to get that is to promote
Australian industries.

The Freehold Assets Realisation Co. (Mel-
bourne) was formed to wind up the affairs of the Freehold
Investment and Banking Co. It began at the end of
1895 with liabilities to depositors and others (apart from
shareholders) of £1,357,437 ; it had paper assets of
£1,289,163 ; and, including capital as a liability, it had a
paper deficit of £326,849. “A” deposit receipts, not
bearing interest, were issued against liabilities to the
public ; “ B” scrip, issued against unpaid interest, is a
contingent liability only. Ten years have now been
devoted to the burial of the old boom concern, and this
shows the progress, so far as it can be made out;—

There is, however, not as much certainty about some of

Depositors have got no interest, and have, in round
figures, lost 15s. in the £of their principal. Comparing
the earlier results with the later, deposits have, however,
increased considerably in value. In the first two years of
the liquidation, they were bought back for 3s. 7d. in the
£ ; in the last two years, the average price has been
6s. 4d. But the man who got 3s. 7d. ten years ago may
have been earning interest on the amount since then, and
the man who gets 6s. 4d. now has got no interest in the
ten years. The other side of the matter is shown next

Assets here include balances owing by purchasers, &c., now
amounting to £43,937 (new co.) and £11,813 (old co.). The
properties held in Oct., 1904, had a book value of
£551,030; they now stand at £432,217, showing a reduc-
tion of £118,813 ; and the ascertained loss was £88,494, so
that £118,813 (book value) of property realised £30,319,
or about 4s. 2d. in the £. The cash, balances due, &c. (if
good), would pay “A ” deposits 3s. lOd. in the £. The
properties, if they could ho realised at 4s. 2d. in the £ of
book value, would pay ' A ” deposits a further 3s. sd. in
the £ together 7s. 3d. Meantime the properties are
returning something like £l5OO in rent over and above
all the co.’s expenses ; and that, of course, will add to the
amount available, though the margin between expenses
and receipts will gradually lessen. This amount may,
perhaps, be fairly set against losses on account of the
balances due. Looking at it in that way, on the basis of
recent transactions there is 7s. 3d. in the £ for depositors.
A spurt in land values would increase the available
amount ; but, taking things as they are, an immediate
6s. 4d. in the £ looks a very good price indeed for the
deposits.

For practical purposes, this list is mighty little good
The co. apparently every year credits itself with just
enough of the corpus commission on trust estates to pay
dividend ; and what that proportion is it is impossible to
tell. If there were a large surplus at any time it might pos-
sibly be used to write down securities. The profit and loss
account provides the means by which this might be done, for
theone solitary item on the revenue side is—“ Profit includ-
ing interest, commission, rent, &c., after providing for con-
tingencies.” But if there has been any such writing-
down it is remarkably well hidden ; for of the assets the
only item not included at cost (less in one or two cases
some admitted writings-off) is “ Money lent on mortgage
of freehold property. £5190.” Five years ago this item
stood at £21,965. Possibly contingencies have been pro-
vided for here. Again, it is equally possible that profits
have been divided up to the hilt. Every year, nearly
the directors remark that they “cannot too strongly
urge upon shareholders the necessity of continuing and
increasing their efforts to bring business to the co.” But
how can directors expect the public to hand over its
affairs, when it is too dead to look after its own interests
to a co. whose published accounts are apparently little
better than a make-believe ? Once more, there is rio class
of public co. s in Australia whose published accounts are
more unsatisfactory than those of trustee co.’s ; and, from
their very nature, there are no concerns which ought to
be franker with the public. If they want to strengthen
the demand for a public trustee office, the co.’s (with very
few are'going the right way to do it.

The plant is in the balance sheet at £60,740, and nothing
has been charged to profit and loss for wear and tear of
this large asset. The sum written off is for alterations
made to the buildings. If only 2£ per cent, were charged
for depreciation of plant and buildings, the net profit
shown above would be reduced to £1309 or lust
about 1J per cent, on capital (£83,458). Considering
that practically every other Maoriland mill is in the
same condition as the Mosgiel, there is obvious need
for a close inquiry into the cause of the trouble. It cer-
tainly' isn’t the quality of the goods—unless it be that
their quality is too good. They are made of wool and
wool can t compete with shoddy for cheapness •’ and
it will be found almost for certain that it is shoddy mas
querading as all-wool which is knocking out the Maori-
land woollen business. Should inquiry prove this to be
so, then tiiree courses are open (1) To compel im-
porters of shoddy to brand their goods as such ; (2) to put
on such an import duty as to make it unprofitable to
bring 111 the fraud ; (3) to do nothing, and let the local
industry slide to ruin, and the co.’s to liquidation, and
the workmen to the inevitable sack. The first is easily
feasible ; the second is hardly practicable, and anyway it
isn’t altogether fair to Protection to use it for this pur-
pose : the third is unthinkable. And yet it is the unthink-
able third alternative that is being practised.

So the co. has been buying extensively as well as selling ;
in spite of its sales it holds in properties £158,270 (book
value) against £152,725 at the close of the previous y 7 ear ;
and its liabilities to the public have gone up from £81,984
to £92 664 Presumably the co. expects the demand
for subdivided lands to increase, or at least to be nia"n
tamed; if it isn’t maintained, the year's transactions
won’t turn out as satisfactory as they look at
Certainly the gross earnings show £4 Bs. per cent on the
total assets, and that doesn’t look as though the estates
intended for subdivision were unproductive ; but on the
other hand, the earnings include profits on subdivision
sales, so the figures don’t much help one to tell what the
assets would earn supposing there were no demand for
properties and no profits coming in from sales And if
that happened, the £92,664 of public liabilities, on most
of which interest would have to be paid, would pinch the
co. The fact is, the business is highly speculative • and
the co.’s figures for the moment are not much of a guide
as to what its future figures will be.
* * * *
Coal co.’s were the one group of stocks that
didn’t budge last year. With one or two exceptions they
closed at a lower price than they opened. The most
notable exception was Vale of Clwydd (Western district
N.S.W.), hawked two or three years ago at under 10s ’
and now at 265., a rise of Bs. in the year. For that the
Lithgow Ooal Association and the railway contracts are
to be thanked most. Coal prices have been low, and
prospects of the export trade are not bright. N.S.W. has
hitherto done most of its coal business with Protectionist
countries, and the Protectionist country gets into the
habit of doing things for itself—including the digging of
its own fuel. The coal-miner has for years past been, on
the whole, a good Protectionist ; he lias recognised that
his best hope lies in increasing the home consumption of
coal ; he knows he can rely upon getting the home mar-
ket, and that that is the only market he can be sure
of. ButtheN.S.W. man whose money is in coal mines
hasn’t been a conspicuous Protectionist; and the fact
that his stocks have sagged during a year in which every
other group of stocks has soared may'do him good.
*** ' *
None of . the bad bargains made by the Vic-
torian Government in pursuance of its closer settlement
scheme points more urgently to the need of a compulsory
purchase and land-tax clause combined, than the negotia’-
tions for the Werribee Park Estate. This consists of
24,000 acres. It is within twenty miles of the Melbourne
Post Office, and yet it has been in the hands of one man for
the last half-century, and has blocked settlement foras long.
It was bought, or cadged, from the State for a nominal
price per acre. It required very little clearing, and tens
of thousands of pounds have been made off it. Yet now
that the State wants it back for closer settlement pur-
poses it is asked to pay £3oo,ooo—£l2los. per acre. And
this land has been paying only a few shillings a year land-
tax ; for tax purposes it was valued at a mere fraction of
what it is valued.at for repurchase. A graduated land-
tax of the right size would have forced the owner to out
it up years ago, and the State would have been saved
over a quarter of a million sovereigns, and been collect-
ing revenue for years from a thousand extra taxpayers.
* * *
The belated annual life assurance report of the
Cape Colony (S’African) Government Actuary for 1903 was
presented to Parliament in the 1905 session. Most
striking feature, so far as concerns Australia, is the re-
markable increase in the business of the National Mutual
(head office, Melbourne). At the end of 1902 this society
had outstanding Cape Colony policies assuring £809,650 ;
at the end of 1903 the total was £1,460,358. By far the
largest of the S’African offices is the South' African
Mutual ; but its year’s increase was only £613,041 as
against the National Mutual’s £650,708. No other office
added more than £135,739 to its previous total. But the
A.M.P. policy-holders, who are being invited to consider
what foreign business the National Mutual is getting,
ought to consider also the Colonial Mutual, another
Australian office which has for many years been
pursuing business in Africa, and is now making no pro-
gress there ; its policies in 1900 assured £1,936,782, and in
1903 they had only been increased to £1,995,847. It is
yet to be seen whether the National Mutual will not tail
off in the same way. Anyway, S’Africans are beginning
to point out that the “foreign offices” have very little
money invested in the country ; and the Government
Actuary urges that they should be compelled to maintain
the full reserve value of all S’African policies in S’African
securities. The American assurance scandals are bound
to strengthen the demand for this change ; and, as Aus-
tralian offices are bracketed with the American, the pro-
posed new law will no doubt apply to them also. Probably
the Australian office won’t feel quite as comfortable when
it has to make very big investments as far away as
S’Africa.

“Lead, £l7 17s. 6d.’’ Cabled quotation on
December 28; but next day it was 3s. 9d. lower. Only'
once before since Australia lias been producing lead has
this quotation been reached ; according to the Broken
Hill Prop.’s statistics, £l7 17s. 6d. was touched during
the metal boom at the end of 1900. Then lead fell so fast
that people who were underneath couldn’t get out of the
way ; and before 1901 was very old the price was £ll.
Beckon up the difference in Broken Hill profits between
even £l3 and £l7 ; to the B.H. Prop, it means £130,000
a half-year, to the South and Sulphide Corp. about
£48,000 each, to the North £24,000, and to Block 10
£31,000. Further, if the lead market breaks,
it is pretty certain something will come off
the top of the silver market too. One thing
which might cause one to expect that it won’t be a very
bad collapse in lead this time is that the upward rush has
been very rapid, so that producers haven’t had time to
enormously increase their output, while users of the
metal haven’t had time to look round for a substitute.
By way of a coincidence, note that the highest
price paid for lead in modern times (reliable figures go
back 150 years) was in 1806—just a hundred years ago.
The average then was £35 15s. In the last 50 years the
highest was in 1862, £2O 17s. 6d. ; the lowest' in 1894
£9 11s. 6d.
* * * *
A. D. Carmichael, for eight years past experi-
mental chemist to Broken Hill Prop., and now tripping
in Europe, has been telling a London interviewer, tq
the production of zinc concentrates, that “it is confi-
dently anticipated the Proprietary’s profit from each ton
of tadings treated during the current half-year will be
not less than 17s. 6d. per ton, even from stuff of the
lowest grade.” Carmichael should know as well as any-
body ; for the process which the B.H. Prop, is using, and
over which there has been the law-fight with the Potter
Syndicate, was the outcome of long experiments made
m Carmichael’s laboratory. But the figure cakes some
swallowing, it is so big. The neighboring Block 14
using Potter’s process, only made about 3s. 9d per ton
last half-year ; and the highest price paid by the Zinc Cor-
poration for tailings is 6s. On paper you can easily work
. it out that the profit should be £1 per ton ; but profits on
paper don’t pay divs. in cash.
* * * »
Broken Hill Prop, is having more trouble than
it cares to mention with its plant for production of acid
to be used in zinc extraction ; and that, for one thing
upsets the paper estimates. Wages were at the start
7s. 6d. per shift of eight hours. Men couldn’t stand
the heat and the fumes, so they tried them with six-hour
shifts. Still it was too much, and now the men are beino-
paid 7s. 6d. for four-hour shifts ; and there are so few
rushing the job on even those terms that the co. will let
one man do two shifts in 24 hours if he likes. And as
there are other troubles still, the process of manufacture
will, it seems, have to be somehow modified.

The Scottish Australian Co. has at last let its
Cadia (N.S.W.) freeholds on a tribute lease for three
years; at the end of that time, if things turn out all
right, a co. will he formed to go on with the work
Cadia is the oldest copper-mining district in Australia • it
was to smelt ore there that the late Lewis Lloyd was
imported from Wales. Again and again attempt's have
been made to get at the property held by the Scottish
Australian Co. ; but that absentee concern, which also
owns the Lambton and Burwood collieries, wouldn’t deal
and only deals now on the basis of “a substantial
royalty. ” Yet some people are quite sure that nothino- i n
the shape of labor covenants is needed to make people
work mining properties that they may get hold of.

The Cathcart Proprietary Gold Mine, Ararat,
was the first mine in Victoria to adopt
power for pumping, hoisting and puddling. This plant
commenced work' hi September, 1904, and has been in
constant and successful operation ever since. It consists
of two return tube boilers, 160 horse-power engine, 110
kilowatt dynamo, switchboard, an electric hoist capable
ol hftfng 800 in 24 hours, an electric three-throw
numn raising 25,000 gallons of water per hour and a 35
horse power* motor driving the puddlers. The dynamo
also provides electricity for 40 lights by means of which
surface and underground workings are lighted.
Experience obtained on this mine during one year’s
service proves that: (1) electricity makes an actual saving
in fuel Amounting to about £350 per annum ; (2 the
dynamo and motors cost practicMly nothing to mmntam
(3) the cost of lubrication is reduced to a mmimum . W
electric motors require much less attention tnan
steam engines; and finally (5) electricity is much
more flexible than steam power, Pemrtting “te"'
sion or change as mining conditions frequently requir .
The contractors for this installation were the Austral Otis
no-ineerine* Company Limited, corner of Hanna ana
Kavanagh-streets, South Melbourne, Sole Agents for Ganz
and Company, Budapest, Hungary. 1

fables which thev call the Card Concentrating Table.

inlunction restraining N. Guthridge Limited from m-
frinirina’ the Wilfley patent. This is to give notice
that the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited will
enfore its rights against all persons buying or selling or
otherwise dialing in or using
ino- machines. Edward J. Rigby, Attorney for tne vvunej

Higgins’ Find, near Widgiemooltha (W.A.), is
turning out well, and there will be some good crushings
as soon as water is secured. At present water has to be
carted 11 miles, but a Government party has struck a flow
in a bore-hole four miles away. Higgins has one shaft
down over 100 ft. and is sinking as fast as possible tp prove
the reef, and also in the hope of striking water. Except
for a short blank, the reef has averaged 18 to 24m., woi
over -2oz. This district, although only a few miles off the
Coolgardie-N orseman road, appears to have been missed
in the early days. There doesn’t seem much to support
the suggestion that it is identical with M Cann s Find of
that time. _

Great Ophir, Davyhurst (W.A.), will probably
come on the market before long. Property is traversed
by a big lode—not a quartz reef like the other mines of
the district, and this has been proved down to 180 ft. the
average grade is lOdwt. for a width of 10ft. This lode
faults frequently both in strike and dip, but it it> easilJ
picked up again by those who understand its nature.
Mine is being equipped with a large From roll plant, and
should be producing shortly. As no amalgamating tables
are to be used, it looks very much as if the coarse gold
will be lost, and the treatment will prove a failure 00.
is in 300,000 shares of ss. each of which l-O 000 are fully

Griffiths G.M., Coolgardie (W.A.), which was
owned by a Newcastle (N.S.W.) syndicate, and was the

Tindall’s, but still is reckoned payable. Property has

Here and there co-operative parties of miners
are lining their pockets in Westralia. There are hundreds
of shows too small for big companies, but good enough to
give handsome returns to four, six, or eight men. Almost
every distant field has a State battery, and reefs that
would have been spurned a few years ago aregivmg
more than wages. Instead of hanging on month after
month, scarcely making tucker, and wearily waiting for
J. Bull to come and buy their pot-holes, miners
to-day are self-reliant and resourceful. They know to a
pennyweight or so what the stone is worth, and almost
anything near an ounce is considered good enough.
Altogether, thanks to the State battery system, the pros-
pects of miners who work their own shows have never
been brighter. Here are a few mid-December yields.
From the Emu (Menzies), 77 tons 2260 z„ making a total
of nearly ISOOoz. from 400 tons since lease was taken up
two years ago; Harbor Light (Pmgin), 50 tons, 570 z. ,
Last Chance (Kanowna), 14 tons, 90oz. ; kad}Glad}S
(Mulline), 158 tons, 2510 z. ; Shamrock (Kunanaling) 84
tons 101 oz. ; Savage Captain (Burtville), 124 tons, 310 oz. ,
Bond’s Find (Burtville), 107 tons, 289°z.; The Secret
(Menzies), 28 tons, 115 oz. ; Mackay and Richards (Men-
zies), 35 tons, 103*oz. Even at Norseman, where the
stone is mostly low grade, the State batter} got 3isoz.
from 470 tons fbutthe Boogardie battery, Mount Magnet,
tells a tale of misery every month, some ot the crushings
panning- out less than a quarter-ounce.

Therefore 1905 only just about topped 1901, but its profits

The reason is that the Moon line has been on the wane.
Clarencehas disappeared from the div. list, Great Northern
has only flashed recently, while Virginia ha s huled to

Moon diva fell from 445. in the first half-year of 1905 to

The year’s returns from Bendigo again show
George Lansell as the field’s biggest money maker. He
holds controlling interests in over 20 mines, and most of
them paid dividends. From his own six private mines,
he obtained 10,7300 z. Despite the fact that his conserva-
tive notions have helped to keep Bendigo in the slough
of unscientific management, Lansell has, nevei theless,
been one of the pluckiest and-most pertinacious developers
on the field. He firmly believes that the gold came up,
and lias no qualms at all about starting out to sink a
shaft through 1000 ft. or 1500 ft. of barren ground. So he
earns his rewards, and his success has a vast deal to do
with the ease with which many Bendigo co.’s get m
their calls.
The Union G.M., at Diamond Creek (within
easy distance of Melbourne), threatens to be capable of a.
div. within a few weeks, instead of a further call. Since,
the" present co. aroused the old property from its long-
sleep some six or seven hundred tons of stone have been,
crushed for an average of about loz. to the ton. The-
manager reports that things are looking healthy in the,
main shaft. At the fifth level he found a reef (Ift.) show-
ing fair gold, and the unwatering of the mine is expected
to yield a satisfactory solution of themystery of its flood-
the dim long ago. But an unsatisfactory solution,
is always possible in such oases.

The Gladstone (Tas.) directors are no chickens.
Their balance-sheet saw the light the night the Stock
Exchanges closed, or shares might have finished differ--
entlv Debts tot up £3269, made up thus .Bank, £4lO ~
{or plant, £2261; sundry creditors, £598. The item that,
affraots is plant, £7821, to which has to be added the
£2261 due on it. Therefore a dredge which admittedly is
not satisfactory has cut away with ten thousand. Since
the days of the Campbell’s Creek dredge, when a lot had,
to he unlearned and about three times as much learned,
there has- been such a costly object lesson in the
dredging business. Since the half-year co. has wiped
probably £3OO odd of its debts, bringing them down to-
about £3OOO. Supposing returns keep up at three tons-
every 10 days, it will take well on till June for the co to
clear itself 5 Yet directors talk about being out of debt
in February. They apparently look for higher returns,
as they mention “ some important though slight altera-,
tions to be made which should greatly improve results.
Seeing that the dredge is only working up to 60 per cent,
of its capacity, there is plenty of room for improvement.
Here again half the trouble is due to a preventive cause
the presence of surface timber, which could and ought
to have been cleared away. Generally the report conveys
to the mind an impression of chaos, and the board ought
to he made to face the music over it.

t “E.J.8.”: If the destruction of the
’possum goes on at the present rate the Govern-
ment will soon have to consider the advisability
of a close season to save the animal from extinc-
tion. The increased demand in Russia for furs
lias sent the price of the skins up to a figure never
before realised, and the ’possum is consequently
having a bad time. _ At Roma (Queensland) 9s. a
dozen lias been paid, and men striking a good
patch in that locality can make £1 a day. A
waggon came into Roma recently with a load of
13,200 skins. It won’t require very many loads
like that to clean a district bare. Cyanide poison-
ing is the usual method of destruction.

“6x8”: A blackfeUer once gave us—
Mayne and myself—a dorg. He said his name
was Monte Christo; but we called him
Monte for short. He used to plant super-
fluous ; provisions, when his “stummick”
couldn’t hold any more furniture.
While camping for dinner once,
between Louth and Winbar
station (N.S. W.), Mayne
tried an experiment on
Monte, who had just
buried a piece of meat
for future reference.
Unobservedly Mayne
dug it up, and again
offered it to the
dorg. He at once
scratched a hole
and re-interred
it. Produced
again, hel ooked
just a trifle du-
bious, but buried
it somewhere
else in good faith.
The third time he
sniffed at it suspi-
ciously, while a faint
look of recognition illu-
minated his face. The
fourth time he was almost sure
he had seen that junk of meat
before, but still gave it the benefit
of the doubt. The fifth and sixth
times lie turned it over and over,
and at last buried it in disgust, l'be seventh
time he picked it up without any hesitation, and,
going to the fire, deliberately cremated it.

“Silas Snell”: This story might be en-
titled “The .Jew’s Revenge,” and the scene is a
Victorian mining township in the vicinity of
Clunes. The hero was a stout young Hebrew
partly named Loo ; the rest of his name is lost to
history. He was travelling for a Ballarat firm of
softgoodsmen, and driving his trap up to the
trough in front of the Traveller’s Arms he col-
lided with another trap standing near, and ground
much paint off the wheels. The result was that a
hairy young farmer blundered out of the bar,
called Loo nasty names, and hit him on the jaw.
Loo went down, and stayed down, with glazed,
half-open eyes, and chin pointing to the zenith.
You never in your life saw anything so like a dead
xlebrew. Ihe farmer man gazed at the prostrate
torm in horror for a minute, then he fell on his
knees beside it, and called upon it in anguished
tones to speak to him—only speak to him—one
little word—say, oh, say it was not dead. Loo
aid not speak, and the farmer yelled for help and
besought everybody to bring doctors—to bring all
the doctors available. The only doctor was
brought, and meanwhile the agonised farmer con-
tmued to call upon the collapsed Yid, pleading
with him, m Heaven’s name, to arise and breathe
again. Ihe doctor came and examined Loo, and
k * s^ ve * a * things for him, and charged a guinea
which the farmer paid. He recommended that
Loo be put to bed and that ice be applied to his
head, and the publican charged a guinea for that,
and presently, after meeting other small ex-
penses, the farmer went away, only moderately
relieved in mind. When he had gone, Loo at
once got up and put on his clothes. “ How much
did it cost him ? ” he asked the landlord. “ Oh,
about three quid in all,” said the publican.
Oood enough,” said Loo; “after thish he’ll
have too much shense to punch a Jew.”
“ Wilbea ” : The New Hebridean burial
customs (B. 12/10/’O5) aren’t a circumstance to the
ways of sundry New Guinea tribes. If a man
doesn’t die on the date fixed by his sorrowing
relatives, the unpunctuality of Nature is assisted
by a long spear m the short ribs. This procedure
works better than the New Hebridean method
as the late lamented is prevented from rising
from his shallow grave and putting the mourners
who have passed his will for probate, to extra
troubie. Among other tribes, the deceased is put
to more useful purposes. p

“Jimmie Pannikin” : To-day in Broken
Hill the thermometer registered 102 deg. in the
shade at 10 a.m. At 3 p.m. I floated down
Argent-street in a flood of perspiration, and
found a wild-eyed Out-Backer, in from Past-
Beyond-Milparinka, leaning up against a post
near the Court House. He was full of gin and
geniality, and read me these lines, which he
swore were not his own at all:
What s that thick viscous stream flowing away
from under your boots?” I asked. “Well I’m
hanged !” said he, “if it isn’t me patent rubber
heels what’s melted right off me boots.”
» • * *
“Uckle” : Decorating verandah-posts with
gum-saplings at Xmas time is a dirty and silly
practice. Early on Xmas Eve nearly every
verandah-post in the Australian country town has
a bush tied to it. By Xmas night the leaves are
withered. By Boxing night they have been
blown all over the town—in the tanks, gutters,
spoutings, houses, streets, everywhere ! The smell
of dead gum-leaves haunts that town until about
Easter. In one small township a storekeeper
determined to discontinue the practice. Father
Xmas has a big stock of toys at that store. The
people boycotted him. They reckoned the non-
appearance of gum-leaves on his verandah was
sacrilege or something.
* • * *
“Snell” : There is an idea that the old
difficulty connected with the lodger is confined to
towns. My informant to the contrary is a com-
mercial traveller, but otherwise reliable. Accord-
ing to his own showing, he was driving from
Whosethis to Whereabout on business about a
month ago, when he came across a tremendous
disturbance agitating a small, bleached, wooden
house in a paddock. A small man, also bleached,
was sitting on a gate-post by the track, looking at
the agitated house, which seemed to vibrate under
the stress of the tumult within. Then a broken-
backed chair hurtled out of the door. The
drummer pulled up, and looked inquiringly at the
man on the fence. “ It’s Mary ’n’ Peter,” said
the man, who was sad, but willing to oblige
“ _Who’s Mary ? ” “ Mary’s the wife.” “ Whose
wife? . Peter’s? ” “Lor’, no—my wife. Peter’s
only livin’ at our place—payin’ 15s. a week.”
“What is the row about?” “The row’s about
me mostly. That’s why I can’t interfere very
well. You see, Peter kicked me out, ’n’ the
missus took my part, ’n’ she sez I’ve got ez much
right there as he has, a’most. So I’m leavin’ ’em
to settle it atween ’em.”

“Harrowby”: Be the old-world nomen-
clature of Newcastle coal mines (B. 30/11/’O5).
Nearly all these mines wear the names of U.K.
collieries, in some cases double and even treble-
banked. F’rinstance, Greta, East Greta, and
Heddon Greta; Wallsend, Young Wallsend, and
West Wallsend, Also nearly all Newcastle
suburbs are named from U.K. coal centres, such
as Hamilton, Stockton, Wallsend, Cardiff, &c.
And on the South Maitland coal-beds we have
Stanford Merthyr, Pelaw Main, Richmond Yale,
and Uebburn (the best-appointed colliery in Aus-
tralia). Travelling west to the Cessnock fields,
one stumbles en route on a dilapidated tunnel
with the distinctly novel appellation of “Sore
Eye ’ (on account of an eye-trouble which affected
the men working there). At Cessnock the Cale-
donian Company’s tunnel is Aberdare, and the
Seaham Co.’s is Abermain.

“H.J.L.” : Re “Yigilans” (B. 26/10/’O5).
The same conditions of horror are rife in all out-
lying coastal camps from the Gulf to Cairns— due
to the insatiable and uncontrollable animalism
of the Asiatic. The horrors I could speak
of are only fitted for discreet private investiga-
tion. !he alien has damned the aborigine. As
he is, it were better for the community that the
aborigine became extinct. Protection is a farce
m regard to gins. The cunning Asiatic will beat
Protectors every time. Having fished and
anchored amongst the Asiatics I know them • and
if the whites of Australia knew them as I do’thev
would rise unitedly and do as they once did in
Normanton—chase every Asiatic from our shores
Sensualism with low-caste aliens is a fetish'
Chows lead the van with unspeakable devilry. '

And for your hand did sue ;
I told him, like a Manly lad,
That I had Picton you.
I thought at first that he would Rous
Upon me with his tongue—
Perhaps eject me from the house,
Or say you were too Young.
But my request he did not Bourke—
He mildly said : “ Don’t fret, man ;
If Liz agrees, your suit will work,
And you may have her Yetman ;
Meantime, anent this subject, sir,
With my good wife I’ll speak,
1' or much, of course, depends on her—
Call round Temora week."
Thus far a Prospect seems Tooloom
Of gladness in my life—
My Lucknow will be quite a boom
If you’ll but Beami wife.
A Sunny Corner in your breast
I hope you’ll find for me—
An Eden where my soul may rest
No matter Werribee.
O, Weddin bells, with charms divine,
Ring in the happy day—
The day on which the Bondi sign
As Lincoln us for aye.
Both late and Airly, black with mire,
I’m Grafton for your sake—
In fact, my love, I Nevertire
While gold for you I make.
I labor in the Narromine,
In dungeons dark below,
And while the sun on you does shine
I shout for “ Candelo ” 1
Let’s have the Orange blossoms soon,
And then, fair maid, we shall
Seek Beecroft for our honeymoon—
You’ll be a Merrigal.
I’ve Adelong desire to gain
Your love, which if not won,
There may be found my empty brain
Beside a Barrengun 1
Or, Raleigh, love, if you refuse,
On earth I cannot dwell;
Deepwater is the grave I’ll choose,
And then I’m Inverell 1
My love, my life, my queen. Myall,
Don’t keep me on The Spit,
Lest some such Folly Point my fall
And from this world I flit.
I Ophir you a loyal heart,
O, Keepit from such fate,
For life were Nhill from you apart—
O, save me ere too late 1
O 1 Sydney Cove, if 1 were gone
Away from Ma and Pa,
And no one left to bake a Scone,
Deep grief would Berrima 1
And should that Appin, O how sad ;
My joy would then be o'er;
To Berry her would drive me mad—
You’d never see Lismore !
Speak not so sadly, Darling, don’t,
Else my Good Hope you’ll kill.
Say you’ll be mine, for if you won’t
I think that Emmaville 1
O Harden not your loving heart—
Say but one word, sweet lass ;
Wilt thou be mine—my “ better part?”
She softly whispered, “ Yass !”
K.S.W.
P. JEFF, WALLACE.
I love the sultry summer ;
Ah ! yes, indeed I love
The days when the thermometer
The hundred soars above ;
When everybody fans and swears
About the blessed heat
That scorches till it nearly melts
The boots from off the feet.
NEUTRAL GROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a bloke is dead an’ buried, in some place ’e 'as to
dwell,
An’ they say it may be Heaven, or again it may be ’Ell.
But I’d like another option-I’d like to fossick round,
An after death, with bated breath, to find some nootral
ground.
Me age is close on eighty, an’ me health it 'as declined,
An I m emit with sore anxiety that preys upon me mind:
though I thinks I am forgiven, yet I’m fearful o’ my fate,
An I dread to meet St. Peter, standin’ sentry by the gate.
I ’ave been a mighty boozer since the age o’ twenty-one,
n I see my sins rise tenfold now me race is nearly run •
So I ve doubts about me safety, an’ I raise me ’umble plea
lor just a bit o’nootral ground—that’s good enough for
me.
The shinin’ streets o’ Paradise I gladly will forego,
If I circumvent catastrophe an’ dodge eternal woe :
111 not clamor arter riches-arter corn an’ wine an’ oil
Provided that my ghost can roost in peace on nootral
soil.

“ Yarrum ” : “My experience
of cockies,” said the groom, “was
brief; but it was a clean knock-out.
I'd made up my mind that graft
was the best thing for a man, and I
got it with a cocky near Cobargo.
The wage was (is. a week and victuals.
First day, toiled at digging post-
holes and straining wire for a fence.
Long before dark my shoulders ached
terribly, and I seemed to be a couple
of men, for my back was quite gone.
After supper was loading my pipe,
when the boss remarked, ‘ You
smoke.’ Told him I’d been chewing
during the day, as I didn’t get time
to light up. He said he was glad I
smoked, as smokers were mostly con-
tented men. ‘ Anyhow,’he went on,
• it’s only wasting time sitting about
smoking. Just come away down to
the barn, and you can give me a
hand with huskin’ for a few minutes
and smoke all the time.’ We husked
until well on in the morning, when
we went back to the house, and I
turned in—am not quite sure that he
did. He told me I might ‘ sleep in
a bit,’ and I quite expected to have
breakfast in bed. It seemed to me
that I’d just fallen off, when I was
awakened by somebody on the pre-
mises calling, * She’s a-comin’! She’s
a-comin’! ’ I couldn’t make up my
mind whether it was the wife I had
to flee from or whether it was the
river that was rising. On spec. I
reached for my boots and cabbage-
tree, and rushed to the door. The
cocky was in the passage calling for
all he was worth, ‘ She’s a-comin’ !
She’s a-comin’! ’ ‘ Who’s a-comin’ ? ’
I asked. ‘Why, daylight , yer lazy
vagabond ! Ain’t it time you were
gettin’ up, or are yer goin’ to sleep
all day?’ I didn’t go to work again
for him. He handed me eighteen-
pence, which he reckoned he ‘didn’t
orter.’ ”

“D.H.R.”: The other day I
was talking to a large and hairy indi-
vidual who had travelled in company
with Matilda from North Queens-
land. He told me that somewhere
south of the Fitzroy River he one
night came upon a heap of wood,
with signs of rope, iron, and other
rubbish, and he didn’t know what to
make of it. During the flood of 1864
a schooner, making its way up the
Fitzroy, got over the banks, and sail-
ing out over the country, was, by the
sudden falling of the flood, left high
and dry some four or five miles in-
land. Two stockmen saw it one day
as they were riding round the coun-
try, before it had fallen into dis-
repair, and guessed that _it was
everything but a ship. The blacks in the
neighborhood used to be greatly scared when
meandering through the land to suddenly see this
mighty apparition looming up in front of them.
Would this still be there, and was it the “ heap of
wood ” observed by the hairy personage and his
companion, Matilda?
“Nunomin,” from the New Hebrides :
Re par. 28/9/’O5. The introduced cats haven’t killed
the rats in the New Hebrides nor the birds. But not for
the reason given. Tommy Tanna and Charley Malekula
are very partial to the feline as food, so it has had no
chance to do any good. In my five years’ residence in
the group I have seen dozens of cats speared, toma-
hawked, and eaten. By the way, cats are not the only
things eaten. At the time of writing a man, killed four
davs ago, is being carefully stored for a future feast.

“Black”: At Cronulla, recently, when
fishing for black bream, I hooked and landed,
after much trouble, an lljlb. Port Jackson shark
(Cestacion Galeatus), which I lugged home by the
tail. The creature was handsomely marked with
Y-shaped dashes of brown on a grey ground, and
had a spine in front of each of the dorsal fins.
Thinking to try my hand at skinning and stuffing,
I left the fish all night on the verandah, and in
the morning, to my astonishment, I found it still
breathing. I hustled it off to the coast again,
gave it a “breather” in a pool for half-an-hour,
then put it back into the ocean, and away it went,
apparently none the worse for having been nearly
12 hours in a drought-stricken country.
“Nym.”: I’ve read a lot about “Old
Man” and other dead-sure cures for peaeh aphis
and other pests that come in the night to stay.
Give me the old cock-sparrow and his tribe as the
surest, cleanest, and most wideawake aphis exter-
minator going. To see the units of the cheeky
tribe running their bills over the boughs and
round the rose and peach buds is a sight that is
good to see. Last year my roses and peaches
were pictures, and cost nothing save a good word
in season for the sparrow tribe.
* * * ■»
The Waranga Basin (soon to be known as
Lake Waranga) has been covered with water,
with a rising depth since the Goulburn is in flood ;
and water-fowl are present in thousands. 1 here
is a proposal to have this little Victorian inland
sea reserved for all time as a haven for native
waterfowl, and the idea is an excellent one. The
cove with the gun, when found on its banks, should
be exiled to the M‘Donnell Ranges, where he
might have great sport trying to shoot wild cats.
, * * *
“ Eiddal ” (Vic.) : Things are in a bad way
round the Cockadoodleville fringe. Several of the
wealthiest stock-owners and graziers, who con-
sider themselves a rung or two above their cocky
neighbors, are paying able-bodied grafters putting
in time between the shearings 6s. 6d. per week,
poor tucker (your spuds picked for you, and skim-
milk to wash them down, and told to your face
you’re wasteful if you leave the spuds’ jackets
lying round), and sleeping accommodation that
only requires one sniff from some sane inspector of
nuisances to raise the devil. The petty mean-
nesses of the wealthy round the Cockadoodleville
quarter defy description. A young English chap
came along the road one day down on his luck.
Long Ghost, the wealthiest cocky on the road,
engaged him to plough, putting him on then and
there(at dinner spell). He kept him workinghungry
till tea time (7.30. p.m.). The lad workedfive weeks,
ploughing in first-class style. When the crop
was in he was handed a cheque for 16/6, and told
to skip. God speed the right Labor party.
* * * *
“ C.X.G.” : We had run out of dynamite
at the claim, so I rode into the station store to get
a supply. Was riding a tame neddy, but had a
rather wild young pack-horse. When loading up
I put the dynamite in one of the pack bags. Un-
fortunately I omitted to hobble the colt before
packing him. He broke away just as I was
getting an extra inch or two on the surcingle,
jumped four or five wire fences en route to the
yards, and over the post-and-rail fence, right into
the middle of a mob of the station team-mules
in the horse paddock. I borrowed a stock-whip,
and went after him on my old neddy. Chased
him round a bit, trying to cut him out with the
whip, when one of the mules kicked him fair in
the pack bag. There was great light and a fear-
ful roar. I remember seeing about 20 mules
starting to do the mile in three seconds, while the
air was full of young horse. Th< m a great quan-
tity of blank fell on me. I woke up in the men’s
hut with m'v arm in splints, an- d was told that
about a quarter of my horse was on the roof.
“ Yal,” from Launceston :
Everything south of Launceston is painfully suggestive
of the Cold Country. First the fact is apparent in the
wholesale grovel to anyone with a handle to his name.
For instance, the Mercuey’s weekly, the Tasmanian Mail,
for want of something more instructive, published a full
platepicture entitled “ The Lady Edeliue in her boudoir,
depicting a lady sitting in a room. The", again, every
shopkeeper advertises that he is under the patronage of
vice-royalty, if he has only supplied a reel of cotton.
This makes me tired. .
There is the same style prevailing in architecture as
there was 100 years ago, the buildings then built by con-
victs being modelled on the style prevailing at that date
in Hingland. The style has remained unaltered ever
since. The country is in the same condition, being owned
bv large landholders and practically undeveloped. lhe
tenants and employees hold the landlord in reverence,
and regard him as a sort of demi-god. This makes me
16 All the l youths with any grit in them realise that they
belong to a corpse island, and go over to the mainland at
about the age of 15 or 16. Those who prefer to remain
are the real slothful, easy-going “ Tassies. Most of the
businesses in Hobart bear signs “ Established somewhere
early in last century,” and they look it. Their aspect of
ajre makes me weary. ,
Along the N. W. coast the people are essentially farmers,
and their god is “ Spuds.” The universal cry is, Good
morning, Bill; how is spuds?” And when spuds is
low ” there is weeping - and wailing - in Israel—Spud-lsrael.
Since Federation there has been an influx of bagmen from
the mainland, and they are invariably greeted with
“Hallo, how are you?—when ye goin away ? This sort
of thing palls on you. There is a good future in store for
Tassy if the people wake up, but it is veritably the land
of to-morrow.
* * * *
The Immigration Act is already making
itself felt in the interior. Where Afghan camel-
drivers could be obtained four years ago at a wage
of 50s to 80s. a month and rations (Hindus much
less) it is a hard matter to get men now at £6 and
£7 a month. The Afghan worker who remains
has raised his tariff. This is slowly but surely
forcing the industry into white hands. Hitherto
the white man stood off the camel business, prin-
cipally because the imported Hooshta kept the
wages too low for him, and he could get better-
paid employment at more congenial work, blow
that the wages are rising, however, he is bottling
up his distaste of the camel, and, in most cases,
makes a much better fist at the business than
the colored man. He is far kinder to the
beast than the other fellow for a start, and kind-
ness goes a long way with animals, even with so
leathery and wooden-headed an animal as the
camel, _ ,
»♦ . *
“ Homo ” : One thing I strenuously insist
on is that the rooster can give Mr. Baracchi
points at forecasting. Even when forenoon has
been wet or showery, if the cocks of the neighbor-
hood strut round on manure heaps or other
eminences about 12 o clock, and crow persistently
and defiantly, it’s safe to go out without your
umbrella in the afternoon ; but if they stand
round discussing things, with a look of weird
expectancy on their countenances, prepare for
more dirty weather.

A reminiscence of the 44 bad. old days,
when men were legally kidnapped very much
after the fashion of the gentle Moroccan brigand
of to-day—in the streets, to serve in the British
navy : Number 1 of the London Morning Advjcr-
tiskr, published 10th Feb., 1794, contains the
following: “A very warm press of seamen took
place on Tuesday night, and was followed up with
increased vigor last night; several prime and
ordinary seamen were taken.” Sounds like an
account of a fishing excursion.
44 Vigilans,” from Daly River (N.T.) :
This is the best soil tor agriculture in this lonely land,
vet there is no agriculture, and very little stock-raising
here. What a magnificent sanatorium it would make for
those suffering from pulmonary complaints ! The climate
is ideal. People who have left Melbourne and Sydney
physical wrecks, given only a year or two to live, have
come up to this country, and are still alive, and likely to
remain so. Here they do all sorts of work, mining
included.
“E.J.8.”: It is generally thought that
rabbits can’t climb. Along the N.S.W.-Queens-
land border, trees and bushes can be seen with the
bark eaten off to a height of 6ft. from the
ground And a rabbit that has sufficient climbing
ability to accomplish that, won’t experience much
difficulty in negotiating the wire-net fencing.
Rabbits in a paddock trap will run up the wire-
netting with ease, being stopped only by the
netting spread at the top. The fence along the
South Queensland border has proved useless in
preventing the rabbit invasion of Queensland, and
if the pest cannot be successfully fought m the
sheep country, what chance will there be against
it in the central and northern cattle country ?

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Hugh Watt, British ex-M.P., has been
sentenced to five years gaol for his clumsy attempt
to hire a man to murder his divorced wife. Jabez
Balfour, ex-M.P., got 14 years. Some of these
British M.’sP. seem to fall into pretty deep holes.
William Wilson, of Sydney, was beckoned
across the street to have a beer about a week ago,
and while hastening to have a beer he was
knocked down and killed. But for the prospect
of that beer William might be still alive. It is a
horrible warning.
Again with the rising of the Federal
Parliament there is talk as to the High Commis-
sionership. George Turner now says that Deakin
should take it. Many members of his own party
urge that J. C. Watson would be the most suitable
man, but he is unwilling to become a candidate.
In addition to an Anglican Archbishop and
a R.C. Archbishop, Melbourne has a bishop, in
the person of the gentleman with the hard face
and the choice vocabulary—Howie’s deputy,
Wilbur Glenn Voliva. At the recent inquest on
a Zionitess who was faith-healed till she died, the
faithful Dowieites who gave evidence spoke quite

Willy Kelly, of the Pram, intends taking
“a long sea trip to recover from the exhausting
effects of his arduous work during the last
session,” says the Age. This is obviously wrong.
It is the House of Reps., the Hansakd staff, and
the public who should be taking a long sea trip
to recover from the exhausting effects of Kelly.
Somebody has suggested that Melbourne
should erect a marble statue to “ Alfred Felton,
Philanthropist,” in acknowledgment of his be-
quests to the local charities and Art Gallery. But
the “rare generosity ” of the late Mr. Felton was
mostly the post-mortem result of his bachelor con-
dition. He was unmarried; he had a large for-
tune to dispose of, and he left the bulk of it to be
alnon o public institutions—after his death.
Had he been a live philanthropist he would not
have saved so much money.
Max Hirsch writes a Xmas note to say
(referring to recent verse in The Bulletin) that
he is not leaving Australia for good, but will
return in a few months. Also, he claims that he
has not “abandoned the Single Tax.” As to
which later fact he may or may not know best.
Certainly Maximilian has seemed much more
anxious lately to destroy Australian manufactures
than to shift Australian land monopoly.

“ 0.K.” : A paragraph in a recent Bulle •
tin stated that the new Baronet O’Loghlen, suc-
cessor to poor old Bryan, is only 19 years of
age. The youngest of O’Loghlen’s five sons is 19,
whilst the eldest, who inherits the barren
baronetcy, is about 38. Talking of the barren-
ness of the title, now in the third generation, it is
remembered to the credit of the late Bryan that
he twice denied himself a judgeship in his days of
political importance. He had the chance to fix
himself comfortably for life, but his scruples were
stronger than the temptation. His contemporary,
Kerfsrd, had no such scruples.

Died the other day, aged 76, ex-Victorian
Pohce Superintendent W. B. Montford, who, in
the early 70 s, helped to capture bushranger Power.
Montford, then a sergeant, was stationed in the
Benalla district. Superintendents Nicholson and
Hare got news as to Power’s whereabouts, and
went with Montford, who was a good bushman, to
the King River Ranges. On the top of a high
ridge they came upon a mia-mia, from which they
saw protruding a man’s legs. Montford pulled
energetically at the legs. They proved to be the
ower ends of Power. The three then arrested the
balance of the bushranger. He was sentenced at
Beech worth(Assizes to 20 years gaol for highway
i ery. font ford got liis “super’’-ship soon
after. He retired from the service about 13 years
ago. J

Dr. Quick, of the Tariff Commission, is a
source of worry to more than one member of that
information-seeking body of peripatetics. His
persistent hunt after details exhausts some
of his co-workers, who are satisfied to take
statements for facts. He doesn’t take them
tor facts, even on the authority of a tall
hat and patent boots. On one occasion he
sent for a copy of Coghlan, and burst up an
ingeniously-woven fairy tale of imports and
exports. Quick was always a cautious, pains-
taking verificationist. It is said he once refused
to set down his age on a census document until he
had looked up his birth certificate and had done a
. profound calculation.
William Severin Salting, of London, mer-
chant, died worth nearly a million sterling of
which £311,343 was in New South Wales, conse-
quently Carruthers collects the amount of £31134
Ihe Saltings of N.S. Wales date from 1835, when
S. K. Salting was a merchant in Jameson-street.
1 hen lie was joined by Horace Flower, and the
hrm became Flower, Salting and Co., having
stores—still standing—in Hunter-street. John
H. Challis, .a great benefactor in after years to
Sydney University, was the co. To this firm
Sydney is mainly indebted for the establishment
of the Union Bank, Philip Salting and S. K.
Salting being two of the first directors. The firm
gave up business in Sydney in the early ’sixties,
but continued in Victoria under the tide of
Flower, McDonald and Co.
Bill Lyne and Falstaff Forrest are, in the
run of events, sleepless political competitors.
They keep apart as much as possible, each
valuing the other precisely at his worth, but
inexorable necessity couples them to draw the
Federal cart up a pinch from time to
time. Lyne makes no secret of what he
thinks of John, and Big John guffaws when
Bill is called a statesman. In one thing
they are alike—vanity over their personal
appearance. For men long past first youth they
retain inordinate pride in what they consider
their physical graces. Both lived strenuously in
early manhood, and are fine specimens of Aus-
tralian nativedom, and their ponderous and cheery
proportions give pleasure to beholders. But there
are a few other able and good-looking men on the
continent, and there’s no reason why Bill and
John should carry on as if it were wholly a question
of Bill and John.
“ H. Glubb ” writes:—
Deceased White Knight Fitz Gibbon was an example of
the brainy man with the loyal bee in his bonnet. I once
heard the old man refer to the late Queen Victoria as
“the greatest, noblest and best woman the world has
ever seem Now, how is it that a man of common sense
like old Fitz. came to pervert his judgment so as to exalt
in such a way a very decent but obviously mediocre old
lady, with no claims to greatness of any sort ? He was
quite serious and believed every word he was saying.
There was no spell-bind’ng about the phrase. It was a
calm deliverance giver, in private conversation. I can
understand the flowery orator firing off this sort of gup
at public meetings or the ignorant cap-touching family
servant holding these views. But to hear such a man as
Fitz. shove them forward absolutely beat me.
Fitz Gibbon waged a bitter war with the Melbourne
press, and though he cried a truce with the Argus during
his latter years, he died, as far as David Svrne was con-
cerned, with the hatchet in his hand. The “ extry ”
feature about the old man’s death itself, which occurred
at one o’clock in the morning, was that the Argus got a
scoop of the information and published a column-long
obituary next morning, while the Age was a day late. It
was freely rumored in press circles that old Fitz. left
instructions that the news was to be kept from David
Syme in order that his journal might be “ left.”
On one occasion old Fitz. was so annoyed at the Age
that he delivered a violent attack upon it at a Board of
Works meeting, and, singling out the Age reporter, fixed
him with an eagle eye, and read a lecture upon the
iniquity of a man of talent prostituting his abilities for
gold.
The Age man didn’t reply. A somewhat similar thing
happened once in Queensland, but in that case the
reporter did reply. The mayor, at a council meeting,
reproached the local reporter in almost the same words
for selling his abilities for “gold” to a debased rag—the
rag, that is, which didn’t approve of the mayor. The
reporter spoke not, but he solemnly turned every pocket
inside out, placed on the table a box of matches, a pipe, a
plug of tobacco, and ninepence, and went on taking notes.
The mayor fell into his chair with his unfinished denun-
c.at:on in his hand.

the King. Henniker s title must be a baronetcy
at least, or else nothing. J

“T. D.” : What comparative oblivion
covers the name of late Queen Victoria. I cannot
recall hearing that departed lady mentioned more
than once or twice in the two years I have been
perambulating Britain, and, as for the papers,
Julius Caesar has been much longer dead, but he is
far more frequently mentioned. Yet, in her day,
Victoria was supposed to take a hand in the
arrangement of things, and to have a very decided
mind of her own, just as Ned in these times is
supposed to be a great arbitrator when Teddy
Roosevelt doesn’t get there first, and is also under-
stood to promote no end of good feeling between
the empire and various people of more or less
importance and some diversity of color. Is the
Edward worship just as big a sham as the
Victoria worship used to be, and will the pore
Yorldet loom larger than his pater when his turn
comes? As for Victoria, I’ve wandered England
considerably, and I can’t recall a single monument
to her, except a dreadfully chiselled statue in
Kensington Gardens which was hacked by a
royal princess and looks just that sort of art.
V.J.D.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Dead—Art thou dead ? And yet I wonder not —
The marvel is that for so long a space
You lingered here within this little plot
Of little men to spend your sovran grace.
The stars are richer, and the widowed earth
Is glad in sorrow to have added well
Another giant to the world of worth
That went before with black-browed Azrael.
The birds will sing unrivall’d once again,
Except when memories, denying death,
Awaken that half Celtic, Southern strain
Which poured its music in thy ev’ry breath.
Farewell 1 There is not one among us here
Enough the man to solemnly intone
Thy dirge across the cypress-covered bier—
Unless it were.. .thy shadow’s self alone.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.

The Medical Journal,
OCTOBER, 1904, SAYS:
“ The uric acid problem affords an ever-increasing interest, not
only on account of the many attempts at solution and various
theories set forth, but because of the wide field of investigation
which is opened up with the fascinating prospect of bringing to light
metabolic facts which have ever been shrouded in obscurity. e
do know, however, whence uric acid is derived, but the metabolism
produced thereby has yet to be determined. Regardless of diet, rich
and poor suffer alike from the accumulation of uric acid, which some-
times occurs without any apparent failure in health ; but more often
are the kidneys and bladder affected, and the digestive organs im-
paired ; hence excessive uric acid is frequently responsible for heart-
burn, flatulence, and liver trouble. Litlna has been tried and found
useless, for when taken by the mouth it chemically combines wi
the phosphates in its progress through the stomach and there forms
insoluble compounds. The best remedy we know possessing a solvent
action and eliminative properties is Kutnows Powder, which is
largely used by the Medical Profession pid has always given the
highest satisfaction wherever it has been tried.

NERVOUS HEADACHES.
How is nervous Headache brought on ?
Principally by the retention of the excessive
uric acid, which is a terrible scourge to the
nervous system.
How is this uric acid headache distinguished 1
When it is due to uric acid poisoning, it is
generally one-sided or partial, as over the
brow, and more or less intermittent pain
on pressure of the scalp, and darting or
shooting pains from temple to temple.
Is uric acid dangerous ?
Not immediately so ; it is very insidious, but
ultimately, if not checked, its ravages are
disastrous.
Is nervous Headache a first sign of uric acid
poisoning ?
Yes ! when the uric acid is absorbed by the
blood it is rapidly circulated and deposited
in the nerve centres, notably the spine
and brain.
Is uric acid productive of disease ?
To its baneful influence may be ascribed in-
numerable ailments, amongst which are
Rheumatism, Neuralgia, Insomnia, Melan-
cholia, Gout, Sciatica, Rnfeebled Biain
and Suicidal Tendency.
What is the proper treatment ?
Kutnow’s Powder not only removes the
headache, but concurrently invigorates the
nerve centres and expels the cause of
the trouble.
Is the action of Kutnow’s speedy ?
Yes, it will remove headache within 20 or 30
minutes.
