FOR
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 2, 1906.
My Dear Arini.—
Nineteen hundred and six has not, of course,
advanced more than the tip of its nose, so to
speak, into our day as yet. It is a very cold nose.
The temperature is mid-wintry. Last year piled
OP horrors during the last weeks of its life—
drownings, burnings, crimes ; and all the time the
wandering bands were playing “Come Let us
Adore Him” in city and suburbs. “Christian
fools with varnished faces,” as old Shylock called
the masquers, saw the old year out blowing into the
goose’s windpipe and the penny trumpet, and
making aboriginal noises at the theatre crowds.
The Old Year Parade of Sydney Block has, by
the way, quite died out. Some years ago, at the
dawn of the New Year, you might always see
little groups of notables walking along King-street
and greeting the New Year in the middle of the
city. Now the streets are given up to wild horse-
• play, larrikins of both sexes rehearsing the bar-
barities of “ mafficking.” For the matter of that,
the “ Block habit” seems to have died away alto-
gether in Sydney. Hardly a trace now remains of
the old fashion following which.those with le.sure
and a good dress sauntered along the King-street
Block for a few minutes at noon, Saturdays, or
at four on fine afternoons in the week.
Concerning Nellie Stewart. How studiously
she kept her ears covered ! Awhile ago, I asked
one who had known her for 20 years if she had
ears, and he replied “Yes, and very pretty ones.”
Whenever I met her in private, she always wore
her hair so as to show only a narrow little wedge
of face from eyebrows to chin. Her forehead was
always covered by her hair, and her eyes glowed
from under an awning of carefully disordered red-
gold locks, that also partially concealed the upper
part of her neck below the ears. Possibly it
was the exaggerated hairiness that was the
first cause of the frantic story—half Sydney
implicitly believes it—that the artiste tucked
back her sagging skin out of sight, and had it
secured with a very strong kind of sticking plaster.
Her round, unlined neck and smooth cheeks
proved the success of the dodge, asserted
that storyteller. And one rich and fading
beauty is said to have offered Nellie “anything
she'd like to ask ” for plans and specifications of
the way to be beautiful for ever. Every skin-food
concoctor and face-lotion mixer, everyone who
had a powder to push or a soap to float or a hair-
grower to urge, sent samples to Miss Stewart.
“ I take no risks,” fair Nell would say as she
either threw or gave away the fakes.
Off the stage, especially in the house, Miss
Stewart was an extraordinary dresser. Like all
originals she had a great assimilating power, and
could wear with artistic effect clothes that would
make the average woman look a candidate for
Callan Park. Blouses and skirts that would fight
if even spread on the bed together, triangular
shawls, or capes with deep fringes (of fringy gar-
ments she was very fond), strange laces, neck-
laces, and combs of barbaric beauty, bangles, ear-
rings, and belts—they were all unusual, but’they
looked harmonious on Nellie. A belt she was
wearing during her last days in Sydney was
mostly splendid cameos—big ones that she had
patiently collected and had mounted—or belted.
. Little Nancy, her daughter (by the way, “ little ”
is no longer a correct description), promises to be
a handsome grown-up. She will probably grow
out of her freckles, and all she wants now for real
prettiness is some more eyebrows and eyelashes.
In New York the beauty doctors produce those
for their clients inside a month. At least, they
cry aloud their ability to do so. Nancy’s manners
are already grown-up, and she is calm and tactful.
Awhile ago, when she was about 10 years of age,
she would sometimes, whilst her mother was at
rehearsal, come down to lunch at a certain table
d’ hote by herself. And often her order would be
“ Welsh rabbit and a pony glass of English pale
ale.”
Some pearls of wisdom gleaned from a single
issue of Sydney D.T.’s “ Woman’s Letter ” :
Man has been a g-ood friend to woman in the past and
there is every hope that he will continue so in the future
Marriage is by no means to be despised as a profession
for women. It is old-established, safe, and in most cases
fairly remunerative.
The oil of the scalp should never be disturbed by wash-
ing. It is only when the scalp is actually diseased* that it
is in need of cleansing, and then a suitable tonic should
be thoroughly massaged into it.
The tidy man always goes over the front of his coat and
waistcoat on taking* them off with a little benzine, castile
soap and water, naphtha, or gasoline, rubbing the spots in
a circular direction with a piece of flannel.
. Brisbane, as is usual with it at Christmas-
time, decorated its shop window's with a back-
ground of cotton wadding to represent snow, and
fixed up an overcoated, long-bearded figure ’with
icicles hanging to his nose as a centre-piece. At
night a musical crowd went to and fro and carolled
for all it was worth, “ The Snow is on the
Ground.” as it mopped its joint and several coun-
tenance and tried to ignore the thunderstorm. A
backblocker held his head under a spout for five
minutes to cool his fevered brain, in order that he
might better be able to recite “Beautiful Snow ”
in Queen-street; but his mate interfered and led
him away to have a drink. After which the ther-
mometer left the nineties and moved down two
degrees.
. Justice Barton and family, who have been
living at “Elystan,” Woollahra, are going hill-
ward for the summer.
The Mayoral Allen Taylors, who live some-
where in the fastnesses of Annandale, are a-house-
hunting nearer town, and last week viewed the
interiors of certain Elizabeth Bay mansions.
Mrs. Arthur Dowling is taking time by the
fetlock and is getting up a girls’ dance for the
Powerful ” officers. That’s so as to forestall the
Hobart girls in the honor of kicking off the first
ball.
Sydney society is making it a great evidence of
its own spirituality that it did go to church, to
Christchurch, to a “ high ” service, on Christmas
morn, and that it did kneel on the utiearpeted
floor, and that the altar had fortj' candles burning
upon it. It is in little things like these that
bridge-playing, warship-pursuing Sydney reads it
title clear to luxurious flats in the ethereal blue.
Some senders of Xmas and New Year cards
showed individualism. The tooth-edged glitter-
ing bit of cardboard with a snow-scene and sprigs
of holly and mistletoe, and a sedative verse by
Adelaide Proctor or Julia Jobson, has departed,
ihe majority of the Christmas wishers didn’t
break their wish-bone in the hunt for the new.
They just ordered and sent out a couple of hun-
dred cards with their printed names and a waratah
in gold relief, or with the Southern Cross and a
native beastie on a twig, or loping along an inch
and a-half of desert. Some who live in pic-
turesque houses sent out picture post-cards
of the same, and others who have picturesque
small children “carded” them. Saw a long narrow
panel portrait that would have made a fascinating
picture-card. A small boy, Justin Markell, such
a jolly little chap in a belted blouse, stands behind
a row of seven blinking puppies'. Each infant dog
has a pupality of its own, and their puzzled eyes
seem to say to the child “So that’s the world, is
• t ?—well, you should see our kennel.”
VVent to see the “Eight English Primroses” at
the Tivoli. Had been told that as the time for their
turn approaches not a vacant seat is to be seen in
the circle, and the standing room at the back of
the circle is filled up by “ lean-overs ”•—all men
and all moderately full of drink, and quite full of
expectations. The Primroses enter by sliding
down mountain scenery. After some preliminary
singing and dancing, they stand on the backs
of their shoulders on a strip of green carpet,
heads towards the house, and wave their tan feet
up in the atmosphere. As the yellow skirts fall
back hundreds of leaf-green frillies are disclosed.
The tan-hosed legs are the flowers’ stalks. It is a
pretty enough exhibition of pretty supports, but
on the whole rather tiresome. You could find
scores of Australian girls in any chorus to sing
better and dance better than these imported
starlets.
That pretty little Tasmanienne, Nora Parker,
had a very brief career in the pantomime at the
Royal. She is related to some Sydney Some-
bodies, and at first production of the panto, these
were in the audience, and nearly dropped dead at
seeing the ingenue in tights. She made no second
appearance, and is now back in Appleland.
This from a primly Conservative English paper :
A MERIOANS and Bridge Players.—A Young Girl of
birth and position BEGS for £5O to pay off debts
incurred secretly before November 24 ; will promise
not to play again ; in great distress.—Address, X.Y.Z.,
01882, Morning Post Office, Strand, W.O.
If genuine, she must have lost the money to per-
sons who were much the same at heart as the
animals that hurried over the precipice at Gadara
a long time ago. Eor naturally she, before appeal-
ing to public charity, would have asked the
winners to forego their winnings. In Sydney
very few young unmarried women play bridge for
money. Hostesses don’t encourage the young
spinster to their card-tables, not because of any
special care for the spinster’s pocket or welfare,
but, I am told, because “the irresponsible
Australienne wouldn’t feel bound by debts of
honor.” Which, if true, makes the Australian
girl very different from the Australian lad in that
regard ; he is most scrupulous about his “debts of
honor ” and will too often rob the bank or the
till to pay the bookmaker.
A girl wearing pyrographed shoes (and an ankle-
length skirt) did the Block industriously for the
last week. She had evidently got a pair of cream
velvet shoes and hot-pokered them herself, for the
decorations were initials and flannel flowers. Very
well done too.
At Medlow Hydro was a gay Christmas dinner.
One hundred and eighty persons attended the
feast. When the plum-pudding was brought in
all the electrics were shut off, and in the deep
darkness the brandy round the pudding was fired,
and the flame of its burning shot up eight or
nine feet, like the burning of Moscow on a
diminished scale.
A modest wedding was that of Sydney Parltes
a son (by the second wife) of the late Henry
Parkes, and Miss Marion Edith, eldest daughter
of Daniel Morrissey, of Glebe Point. The mar-
riage was celebrated on Nov. 28 of last year.
The widow Parkes—the third wife—in whose
charge the children of the second marriage were
left, is understood to be enjoying a holiday in
England.
The motor car Prestons a,re back and staying at
the Wentworth Hotel previous to going into their
Kirribilli Point place. The G. B. Simpsons and
the Matthew Henry Stephens also tread the
Commonwealth once more. Amongst Australians
doing the West Coast Sounds in the Union Co.
steamer Waikare are the Edward Knoxes, five in
number, two Thorne girls, and the G. Swinburnes
of Melbourne. The Philip Mortons are going to
Colombo for some months, and the Jack Tooheys,
if they can let “Buckhurst,” will go away for
three or six months.
Among the presents of a bride of last month
was an anklet of gold and enamel beads with her
monogram on' the clasp. Sender (a friend in
India) advised the bride that it was for wear
under a very transparent stocking. The anklet
is the last link of the chain which the primitive
husband fixed to the primitive wife when he drove
her before him to their cave at a sharp run, and
flicked her with his whip if she showed signs of
flagging on her honeymoon. It seems* ut of date
these times.
Miss Amy Castles, now that all trace of doubt
as to her stellarship is removed, has a duty to
herself, her art, and the public, and that is to get
thinner. When she was here she was just about
right in the matter of bulk, but of late
she has plumped out too much, She will, so her
friends promise, now go in for training down.
The two Castles, Amy and Dolly, are described
by those in whose houses they’ve stayed as
‘miserably small eaters,” and they never touch
a stimulant. And both have that nervous organi-
sation that afflicts them with nausea and sickness
previous to facing their audiences. The fat that
is put on under such conditions should be easily
dissipated.
Isn’t it time exploiters of “the woman of 40”
let up on the fiction, or raised the age of charm to,
say, 50 years ? We are rather satiated with essays
and paragraphs on the physical and mental
charms of the woman of 40. If a woman
of 30 is good to behold and charming to
associate with, she will doubtless retain con-
siderable looks and charms when she is 10
years older. But the woman of 40 who re-
tains a youthful complexion, lustrous hair and
a girlish figure, and for whose society men neglect
sweet twenty, is such a rarity that she doesn’t
warrant all the pumped-up enthusiasm about her.
if or it is pumped up by the beauty-doctors and
physical cultunsts, aided in a degree by the swell
dressmakers and corseticrcs. Also it helps enor-
mously the sale of ladies’ papers. A woman of 40
loolvs it, except in unique cases. If she lias been
m the habit of taking spirits or wine she generally
more than looks it. Nothing hurries up a middle-
aged complexion and figure faster than the use
of the liquors that, under the new Act, you can’t
—really can’t—get after 11 p.m.
Ihe lost property office of Sydney Tramways
has been kind to 6421 homeless articles found
in the cars during the year just closed.
Ut these lost parasols head the list—over 1000
of them were “left behind.” Gloves numbered
oln, and purses totalled 490. Sydney is a very
picmcky place, so 66 hampers, 29 billycans, and
three kettles were amongst the forgotten. The
travelling public is the finder, mostly always, and
it doesn’t feel pained at handing over an empty
purse, a billycan, or a prayer book to the tram-
guard. Jewellery it promptly annexes. Opera
glasses and ladies’ watches it just loves, and find-
ing them means keeping. A few weeks ago the
engineer on a Mosman boat found a portfolio con-
taining eighteen hundred pounds in notes on a seat
in the boat. ||s owner was discovered. A friend
tells me that she was staying in a frilly Melb.
hotel some few years ago when a fellow guest
roused the hotel after midnight with loud out-
cries about the loss of a priceless diamond ring. It
had slipped from the ledge of the bath down the
waste pipe. The electrical engineer of the premises
began at one in the morning to pull up skirting
boards and cut pipes. The lady loser sat and
watched him like a famished cat eyeing a fat mouse.
After two or three hours work the ring was
found in the “elbow” of a pipe and the
owner became reckless with joy and offered the
engineer half-a-crown Probably he said nothing.
I have looked through a dictionary of 1876 pages
and found nothing that an engineer would be
likely to want to say under such conditions.
Probably he felt a desire to bite the lady hard.
Mrs. Fitzwilliam Curtis is to be married shortly
to a well-known London man—name to be dis-
closed later on. She will be remembered in Syd-
ney as Miss Genevieve Fattorini, and she
divorced her Captain some little time ago.
Mrs. Curtis is of an alluring type—large,
dark, sleepy-looking, and when she dressed
ln W:th big bunches of violets, and
made Cleopatra eyes round an assemblage,
the men present felt as if a big surf was breaking
over them and began to dream out loud. The
1 attorinis are said to he great great grand-
daughters of the great Napoleon, and certainly
have some of his facial characteristics.
Miss Grace Joel, a Maoriland artist, who has
been working for years in Paris and London, is in
Sydney, and intends to hold an exhibition of her
pictures about March. She is mainly a figure
painter, and has exhibited at the Salon and
Academy.
A male essayist on the modern woman writer’s
novel:
Iu these books man exists to be run after or to be run
away from ; to be attracted, rejected, married, deceived,
divorced. In the world, of course, he serves all sorts of
other purposes, but in the ordinary woman’s novel he
lives for these alone.
Not long- ago I read a really able novel bv a
really able woman—there was nothing* ordinary about
either the writer or her book—in which, from
cover to cover, the hero never addressed one sen-
tence to any man but his valet. The whole of his mental
and physical activities were exhausted in making- love
and being- made love to. We were told, I seem to
remember, that he was a journalist, and an amateur of
pictures ; but he never had time to write anything but
billet* Uoux,and he never visited a picture gallery except
to keep an assignation with a woman.
State Excellency and daughter Alice are staying
at Cranbrook for this week. I hey landed quietly
at Rose Bay, both looking far from well.
Puce faces, coats and skirts, and shabby white
frocks at New Year’s Day races. Swarms of
country people, but very few Sydneysiders pre-
sent. The visitors asked if we were not ashamed
of our climate, which was winter under false
pretences.
The admission of a resident woman-doctor to
Prince Alfred Hospital is almost historical in its
importance. Also, it’s somewhat of a backhander
to the Sydney Hospital, which refused to
admit a resident medical woman last year.
Miss Aspinall, the accepted of the P.A.H.,
is described by those who know her, including
h e-fellow and she-fellow students, as “a dear little
thing, quite pretty, almost fluffy.” The Master
of the Scotch College has the honor of being her
Pa. There are eight or nine male residents at
the P.A.H., but her friends say Miss Aspinall
intends to do her share of the work. Unlike the
women residents at the Melbourne hospitals. The
men did their work “when it was of a horrid
description.”
Dr. Agnes Henderson, for many years practising
in India, is in Sydney getting ready for a Maori-
land tour.
If we are to lose motor-car Kelly, we will at
least keep sight of his auto’bile. His brother has
bought the whizzing instrument for his fair Ethel
and her babe.
Lamb (he manages for Houlder Bros.,
the steamship people) was married the other day
to a North Sydney pretty. Bridegroom is of a
Hour Irving pictorial type, and used to be a
renowned oarsman and fine all-round athlete.
They are going to build themselves a home up the
North Shore line.
Australians at home—that is, in the Common-
wealth-will soon learn something about Bruges.
Because one may live there for about tuppence a
week, sundry Australian girls who have not for-
tunes and who have married poor naval men
intend to reside in Bruges while hubby’s vessel is
away cruising. At present it’s mostly known
through Longfellow’s poem mentioning the belfry
old and brown, the bronze twelve apostles, and
Hans Sachs, the cobbler bard.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Ex-Minister Perry makes no denial of the
published statement that he made a Tattersall
win of about £4OOO over the success of Crick’s
horse, Ossian. As Education Minister under
Lyne and See, Perry was a strict disciplinarian,
and at the Boer-scrimmage time he made teachers
aware that “disloyalty” to the pro-Chow raid
was an unpardonable offence. “Tattersall"’ is
against the law of the land directly, and not
merely argumentatively, and here comes the great
disciplinarian’s name at the top of the list of law-
breakers.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 2, 1906.
My Dear Narrie,—
The summer girl is paying a lot of attention to
her back hair. She wears a gossamer over the
rear of the head, even if she leaves her face
exposed to the sun-kisses and the fierce breath or
the baked wind. Another summer fad is the tan
which is carried round generally. It is usually
of a dainty kind, opening pansy face or rose petals
to the view. In use it suggests a flower in a
breeze. Some of the choicest are not much
roomier than a pen-handle, and all are as decora-
tive as a French poster. Even the schoolgirl
hereabouts flies a gossamer sail and carries her
own breeze-whisker. But then the local school-
chick is a daisy. She has a waist, defined curves,
and a precocious swing of the figure, and her
open-work hose are most knowing—and informa-
tive.
A most unlucky softgoodsman’s mistake has led
to a serious hitch in what has been outwardly one
of our best-regulated families. The wrong Cup
accom t passed into the hands of Madam. The
highly proper Missus went to settle a bill that
wallowed in under-frillies and fluff. Instead of
her three commonplace Cup garbs and sets of
plain good underwear (Jaegers next the skin) she
found chiffon creations, billowy cobweb laces and
silken raiment of a most luxurious and suspicious
order. The same old story. The proteg# and the
wife of the suburban Dives had patronised the
same shop and the bills had got mixed. Now
there’s the devil as well as the draper to pay.
There has been no peace or good-will in Dives’
house this goose seaso .
One of the principal nummers of the Musgrove
Co. nearly landed one of the wealthiest Bull and
Bush brethren before setting sail for Uncle Sam’s
country. The cattle-king paid opulent attentions
to the little beauty, and had the co. only been
able to tarry a month or two longer the clank of
the matrimonial chains might have been heard.
As it was, one of the most promising stage catches
came to nought, and the ranch-man remains to
wallow in his bachelor wealth, and probably to
thank Providence for his rescue—as the bachelor
is wont to do.
Ex-Judge Casey prepares for another globe-
trot. This one, from the point of view of the
epicure-on-tour, is to beat all former programmes.
It is a most elaborate bill-of-fare, with gorgeous
shows from the start to the finish. Even the pil-
grimage to Lourdes (though there is nothing
famous to eat there) is listed as one of the items.
And the Judge is 76.
The Northcote pair paid a year-end visit to a
State school out Brunswick way. The Dame
heard the kids elocutfs and saw them in a variety
of scholastic turns, finally looking on at a grill and
cottage-pie being burned in the school oven. So
satisfied at its being done to a turn was her Ex.
that she ordered the next breakfast steak and the
supper stewed onions from the miniature cooks.
They tell great and gay things of Cairo. Miss
Tressie Madden, daughter of an ex-Mayor of
South Melbourne and small City magnate, has
returned from a season or two there, and relates
gaudy tales of life in the land of the Sphinx. Her
medico brother is in practice at Cairo, and making
pots of money among the sickly English residents.
Mosquitos seem almost the only evil of the visitor.
These attack the newcomer in great force. If you
can resist the attack or beat the enemy down, then
you have the time of your life. Paris seems to
be a small circumstance to high life in the hot
Egyptian city.
The story goes that a wealthy dame who owns
nerves has her daily programme, as well as her
prescription, made out by her doctor. She only
goes away or returns by his leave. Lately she
secured permission to visit a neighboring capital.
She was having a good time there when the
medico ordered her back, and she meekly packed
her bag and returned to the tyrant. He has
refused to sanction a trip to the Cold Country.
The dame pines to go, but dare not disobey.
The faith put in a bossy doctor by the sex passes
all intelligent credulity.
Miss Y. De Loitte’s matrimonial engagement
with Howard Vernon causes the dark little stage-
twinkler to be an object of interest and curiosity
to the local girleen. The other night, at “ Pina-
fore,” all the glasses were levelled alternately on
Miss Yinia and her elderly Howard, and every-
thing else in the show was overlooked. The De
Loitte air was free from any self-consciousness or
shyness, which led the cynics of the house to
declare, “Then the girl’s married.” And they
believed it. As if the engaged girl of these times
is supposed to be blushful !
Several motor mishaps occurred this goose
season. One jaunty little German who has a
habit of chanting “ Car-rar-a-boom-de-ay ” as he
steps aboard with his party, had a worse accident
than usual this trip. His party and his car came
back in pieces and took days to arrive even that
way, and repairs are going on steadily even now.
It’s rather foolish, by the way, to go motoring
with a Benedict. There’s no hope of permanent
consolation in that case. The bachelor machine-
owner provides the safer course. Then, should a
girl-guest be damaged in a break-down, there’s
always the hand of a repentant motorist at her
service and a sympathiser to murmur that he
always did prefer a girl with one leg or a scarred
skull, as the case may be.
Miss Margaret Thomas returns to London in
j u ]y—being satisfied with success here and hunger-
ing for a wider field. It’s a common complaint.
A self-depreciation pill to be taken three times a
day is the only cure for the there-is-a-green-hill-
far-away sickness.
Madame Anita Sutherland leturns to London
presently. In view of that, the ’Varsity Conser-
vatorium girls gathered at the Voice Instructor’s
rooms last week and after a moving speech (the
farewell speech always moves) made a presenta-
tion of a silver rose bowl to the dame. The Con-
servatorium wasn’t altogether a happy family,
but the girleens stuck like glue to the big, joyous
Madame.
The Chief Justice Madden flock is preparing
for a flit. There is a present effort to arrange for
the whole family to go gadding with papa. The
youngest hope is now out of pinnies.
Miss Violet Morrison (of the musical-medico
clan) has set off for Singapore on a matrimonial
mission. Her fiance— Mr. Clive Gaunt—is,
stationed there and couldn’t call out here for his
Mrs. Archbishop Clarke lately imported a book
of songs supposed to be suitable for singing by
the Girls’ Friendly Society. Several gushers,
male and female, voted them “simply charm-
ing, and so appropriate.” One of them (to be
sung to the once well-known tune of “We are
a happy family, we are, we are, we are ”)
ended thus: “ We are such happy servant girls, we
are, we are, we are.” Mrs. Archbishop tried
vainly to introduce this poetical effusion at St.
Mark’s Church of England, Fitzroy, but the fac-
tory “ladies” flatly refused to call themselves
happy servants. “ Indeed,” said one young lady
dressed in an imitation voile, enormous hair pads
and a large string of Roman pearls, “I’d like to
catch meself singin’ that.” And the beautiful
ditty is still unsung.
The widows are making a wild dash from this
dusty centre. Mrs. Tobin has set off with her
chick, Mrs. W. Blake with hers. (The latter
shipped by the Victoria.) Mrs. Ross-Soden has
made for Colombo with her brace of sons. The
Cliveden Dowager, Mrs. Wesley-Hall, and a few
less gilded grievers hustle away shortly.
A colossal brother of ex-Senator Harney passed
through here last week. He is on a wedding-trip
(his second venture). The marriage came off in
the Apple Isle without any spit •ding of confetti
or scattering of dissolute shoes. There’s perhaps
not a weightier wag than Mr. Francis Harney in
Australia. He has the light heart and ready
tongue of the Hibernian, and carries his 26 stone
of material round the earth as if it were feather-
down and didn’t bulk any. The lawyer Harneys
are known in the Sandy State as the firm of the
Blarney Brothers, and where the big bulky pala-
ver of Francis fails the subtlety of the slim Ned
succeeds. There’s a seat on the Bench waiting for
the lean Harney, as soon as he likes to leave off
gadding and take law seriously.
One of the best correspondents among the Melb.
footlighters who have gone to Europe is Miss
Alice Crawford. She keeps dozens of her old pals
posted up in her doings, and photos, come along
every now and then showing the Bendigonienne
growing more and more like unto a stately Juno.
Recent letters state that Alice is now drawing a
salary of £25 a week. Her recent performance at
Sandringham and the royal approval thereat,
have gladdened Bendigo considerably.
A recent scribbler from the Big Smoke relates
that an Australian warbler whose solo once sent
’Rooland into massive applause, is actually
starving, and the failures that have met every
effort of hers to convince London that she has a
voice and a great soul attached have affected her
mind. She is a ghastly illustration of an Aus-
tralian who believed in herself and in the Big Fog.
It is said that the Australian Parliament House
waiters have “struck” on attending the poli-
ticians’ garden-parties at the Spring-street estab-
lishment unless paid specially for their services.
They smothered their feelings so long as it was
the large potentates of politics who gave the
parties, but when the small fry started doing
likewise, and it began to appear that they would
have to dance attendance on everybody’s party,
right down to Joseph Cook’s garden bun-struggle,
the worm revolved on its axis.
The pathetic sight of a short, red, fashionable
bachelor of 15 stone or more, in a trim grey tweed
suit, towing about a tearful and draggled family
of five dirty little boys and girls, with the stains
of picnicking all over them, afforded gratification
to the campers-out down near Carrum on Boxing
Day. The florid and reputable bachelor
man was seeking the parents or guardians
of the weeping family, which clutch of
miserables he had discovered howling in the
scrub and declaring that they were lost. The
bachelor, whose figure is the delight of scores
of affectionate girls in Collins-street, took the
waifs and strays out of the kindness of his heart,
and set about finding fathers and mothers for
them. It seemed a simple thing at first, but as
the long day wore on, and his search grew more
hopeless, and his desolate family more hungry and
more full of howling, and the derision of the
campers-out swelled to a great clamor, he
realised that he had tackled the job of his
life. Being unable to foist his deplorable
charges on to any authority, he made a desperate
resolution to deliver them to the police in North
Melbourne, the eldest of the lost souls having a
vague idea that his parents resided there or else
at Coburg. A maddened bachelor passed through
Flinders-street station at half-past eight that
night, with flags of distress flying and his string
of wailing infants hanging on behind and clamor-
ing for “ ma.” People noticed that the grey suit
had the tracks of jammed hands and baby boots
all over it. The trouble came to an end soon
after, for a large woman, claiming to be a friend
of the family, threatened the philanthropist with
her umbrelia on Port Melbourne platform, and
took the children from him. After he had satisfied
a policeman and three independent citizens that
he had not meant to kidnap the family for his own
use he was allowed to depart without a caution,
and with scarcely a stain on his character.
Victoria put up a record of Christmas sensa-
tions. Five persons—including a father and three
children —were swept by a blind roller off a rock
at Sorrento, and drowned. A girl swimming at
Queenscliff was caught in the backwash and
drowned. On a Melbourne suburban railway, an
unknown man was found dead. An old pen-
sioner of 84 years was knocked down and killed at
a crossing. The deadly pea-rifle accounted for a
15-year-old boy. In Bourke-street, in the heart
of Melbourne, an armed burglar shot a grocer.
A constable was half murdered by roughs in South
Melbourne. A buggy upset in a Carlton street,
and injured four men. An old man’s body was
hooked out of the Yarra, and a dead infant was
found in a Carlton right-of-way. A small boy’s
clothing was discovered on the banks of the Salt-
water —the boy being presumably in the river—
and a skeleton was dug up on the beach at
Frankston.
Melbourne Homoeopathic Hospital took its
sensations in large doses on Christmas day. A
nurse venturing to the door encountered a hairy
person on the doormat; he looked like a Reform
Leaguer from Kyabram. She shrieked for help
and the entire male staff turned out to repel the
invader, who was a very tall baboon escaped from
an adjacent circus. The baboon had just been
secured and returned to durance vile when an
elderly person turned up and announced that he
was a vegetable. It was at first thought that he
was a member of some Upper House and really a
vegetable, but when he insisted on being planted
he was passed out in charge of a policeman, and
the Homoeopathic Hospital returned to its
globules with a sigh of relief.

Great preparations are on hand in a woolly
Western family for a domestic event where such
happenings have not been particularly rare as
times go. A Gamp is on the spot, and has been
accompanying the dame on her voyage to this
city, and she also guarded the fair one from
shock on her trip to Macedon last week. This,
however, isn’t as remarkable as the case of the
medico man who was retained for weeks by a big
mining magnate for a similar event. The magnate
and the medico hardly lost sight of each other
once till the final act. It proved the best “ad.”
that particular doctor ever got.
The Domestic Pet craze has arrived at a humor-
ously mad stage in the Cold Country. A column
or two of ads. in every journal reveals the state of
things in all its absurdity. Siberian puppies,
“very affectionate, like small Polar bears”, are
up for auction. The likeness possibly consists in
their hugging propensities. This would suit sen-
timental she-fanciers. Pekin bantams are pro-
claimed by a trader to be “charming pets.”
Another advertiser gushes out, “two sweet Persian
kittens like little balls of silken fluff.” Price, so
and so.
The sad minority of males at all the seaside
hotels is still a source of soft complainings. The
party of twelve girls and two men starting off oil
a jaunt to the bracken and fern glen isn’t satisfy-
ing. Tt lacks the true picnic spirit. It is obvious
that the men will be spoilt and probable that the
girls’ tempers will also be spoilt. In some in-
stances the father of a comely flock of girls has to
requisition all his old veteran cronies to come
along and help to man the picnic outing. Small
boys in knickerbockers, and old rakes with one
leg at the picnic and the other in the grave, are
the only availables at most of the resorts. Now
and again a good-natured dame lets her husband
take the girls for an outing. Being married
she is inclined to be generous at the sight of so
much waste unattached girl. The Miss who
attracts a rare youth all to herself wags her tail
and is generally overbearing in her behavior
before the other stranded dears. It isn’t that
there are actually so very few males in Victoria,
but the present state of things is the result of
painful experiences of former years. The eligible
male was then so rushed by that most persistent
of all campaigners—the kind indigenous to the
summer sands—that his holiday was spoilt. Now
the proposing sex steals off in large batches and
camps out of feminine range.
The adopted daughter is the latest craze. It is
said to be a very charming fashion by which a nice
old man can enjoy young female companionship
on strictly proper lines. Of course, the family—
that is the non-adopted family, the family that
“ growed ” —sometimes objects. If the idea catches
on generally type-writer girls will be the worst
sufferers. The adopted daughter will take the
place of the lady typist

One theatrical manager believes in a star

cheap suburban corner.

TRYPHENA.
Hugh Nelson, ex-Premier of Queensland,
died at Toowoomba on New Year’s morning, at the
age of 70. He spent a quarter of a century in
Queensland politics, and except for his rather care-
ful management of the finances, left no mark on
them worth speaking of. Even as a financier he
was only good as compared with some of his awful
contemporaries. He once laid down the rule that
when there was any definite sign of the year’s
income exceeding the expenditure taxation should
be reduced, and that any accidental surplus that
happened in spite of this should go in reduction
of the huge accumulated deficit. Under these
circumstances—especially as Nelson had a pretty
cordial belief in borrowing—the deficit didn’t
reduce very fast. Yet Nelson was a good financier
as financiers went in Queensland those drunken
days.
The Saturday night jar is the Sydney pub-
lican’s first idea to meet the difficulty of the rigid
new Sunday-selling law. It is ingenious, but
hardly meets the occasion, for great numbers will
empty the jar before Sunday’s sun rises, and will
consequently be in a worse plight than of old
when the day of drought comes. An improve-
ment on the idea would be for the publican to sell
a jar fastened with a patent lock, of which he
would hold the key. The jar, or case, or whatever
it is, could be delivered to the customer on
Saturday night, and the customer could
call for the key on Sunday morning.
This would enable Bung to keep his financial
drinking crowd together. Of course a question
would arise as to whether handing out the .key
wasn’t really Sunday trading after all, and this
question would in due time come before the
courts, but it would take many a day to settle it.
And by that time the jar of whisky with the time-
lock would have arrived.

Centenarians. Professor Metchnikoff has just
drawn up a formula to enable life to be prolonged beyond
the usual term. His idea is to reduce the work of the
digestive organs by using food of very small bulk but of
superior nourishing power. Now by rejecting the bones
of animals, the husks of wheat and the rougher parts of
most foods, civilisation has been gradually selecting the
parts easily cooked and easy to be eaten. At the same
time, however, that which is necessary and invaluable
has been removed from the food. Hence it is that child-
ren are more puny, that the teeth become soon decayed,
and that adults are not so capable of endurance as were
their ancestors who lived on a coarse diet. The golden
mean can be reached by using the ordinary food and
adding to it “Cerebos” Salt, which makes all the food
much more nourishing because it contains the vital phos-
phates of wheat. This solves the problem at any rate in
part, since phosphates are peculiarly necessary to bone,
blood, muscles, nerves and brain. The use of this salt in
the kitchen and at table, therefore, increases the strength
of the body and consequently its power of resisting
disease ; and thus constitutes an important factor in
prolonging life.*

Austin Chamberlain, on the effects of
Freetrade in England
. , nlen of. 60 years ago were told to fight foreign
tariffs by free imports, and the nation had done so for
these 60 years. Had we reduced a single tariff ? Had we
succeeded in converting a single foreign nation by our
example ? Had our rivals been ruined, as it was said they
would be, by their adherence to Protection ? In the iron
and steel trades we had been reduced to third place.

Considering how recently it is that Japan
demanded and obtained freer admission of Japan-
ese students to Australia, it is rather startling to
read that Japan has lately put such restrictions
on Chinese students at Jap universities that prac-
tically the whole lot have had to leave. The Jap
is a broad-minded person in a way ; that is, he
wants other people to be broad to him, but he
isn t broad to other people.

Germany has just put a duty of Is. Bd. a
case on imported apples. But there is no corres-
ponding duty of about 40 per cent, imposed by
Australia on German manufactures ; which is why
the Australian fruit-grower has to skirmish all
over the earth to find markets for his produce,
instead of supplying the local artisans who should
be making the millions of pounds’ worth of goods
which Australia now buys from the foreigner.

N.S.W. has had, up to now, 32 Ministries
and 16 Premiers. Cowper, Robertson, and
Parkes were each Premier five times; Martin and
Dibbs three times. There have been five new
Premiers in the last eleven years; the last of the
old recurrent Premiers—the men who kept turn-
ing up periodically pretty well all their lives—
passed out when Dibbs resigned. He was the
latest of the professional Premiers. Parkes was
the toughest holder of the office (11 years, nine
months, 16 days in all). Waddell’s reign as Pre-
mier was the shortest (two months 14 days).
Donaldson enjoyed the job for two months 19
days, and Foster for four months 11 days.
Reid, as an ignoramus or a liar, takes the
cake every time. To the daily press (issue
27/12/’O5) he said :
The Anti-Trust Bill was one of the funniest things in

the session, and Parliaine. was not to be prorogued
without it, but the voice of lur. Watson was heard, and
the Premier would not risk a division, and the Bill was
rejected on the voices.
And the second reading of that Bill was carried
on the voices. Reid shows himself either a very
reckless liar, or such a dodger of his political duty
that he doesn’t even trouble to read the votes and
proceedings of Parliament.
The scheme of J. C. Watson, M.H.R ~ to
have the Federal capital site shifted from Dalgety
to Lake George strikes one very bad difficulty.
Lake George is mostly dry or nearly so—even now,
though this isn’t a drought year, it is only a
grassy depression. The lake ” has been known
to be dry for 14 years at a stretch.
It is proposed that it should be filled by
diverting some of the waters of the M.urrum-
bidgee. But Carruthers is going to use the
Murrumbidgee to fill his great Barren Jack
reservoir, so apparently Lake George will just have
to parch, and it would be ridiculous to put the
federal city beside a dry hole close to jßungendore
when it might be alongside an absolutely unfailing
river at Dalgety.

Beach, for instance, there is a large area leased in

The Bulletin receives the programme of
a new Citizens’ Progressive party founded at
Bundaberg (Q.). There are many good progres-
sive ideas in the policy of the Citizens’ Progressive
party, and The Bulletin had just finished admir-
ing them when it ran into this :
(4) Renewal of State borrowing for above purposes.
One of the “above purposes” is assisted immi-
gration. Queensland has already spent more loan
money on assisted immigration than all the other
States put together, and one would think it was
time it expended a little of its own money on the
good work instead of resorting to the three-ball
institution. The loan business has left the State
in about the deepest mudhole on earth. Surely
it doesn’t want to scratch the hole any deeper.
The Liquor Act which came in force in
N.S.W. on January lis interesting in spots. It
provides, among other things, that the bar siren
must be at least 21. (She generally is, but she
doesn t look it.) It says that no person under 14
shall be sent for drink, and that no person under
18 shall be served with drink. This is confusing,
but it seems to mean that anyone sending a child
of 13 for beer can be fined as well as refused the
beer, while anyone sending a child of 14i will
only suffer from inability to get the “rum.
A sorrowful point is that not only must the door
close at 11. but the supply of liquor must cease at
11 ; therefore the person who gets in at one
minute to 11 had better get served by a swift girl,
and snatch his drink with both' hands, else he
may go out dry after all.
Now that the westward movement of popu-
lation is filling up the Pacific Coast of Canada
and the United States, why should not Australia
seek for immigrants there—when it has the land
ready and the industries ready? These people
are nearer to us than the people of Europe, and
they are mostly of a better class; also, they are
men who have already acquired the colonising
idea. Besides, there is some instinct which
always ma,kes great human movements “ go west.”
Even the interest on our public debt goes west
worse luck ! Australia has tried to drag people
eastward from Europe with only moderate success.
Perhaps the really great inrush of population is
still to come—from across the Pacific.
There is something pathetic about the
S.M. Herald’s dread lest somebody should do
something. Last week it issued a solemn warning
to the Commonwealth against taking over the
Northern Territory because S. Australia has tried
the place and found it a mill-stone and a white
elephant. That, no doubt, is true enough, but
the Commonwealth can stand a white elephant
better than a single small State can ; and the
Commonwealth can put up the money to give the
territory railway communication, whereupon it
may cease to be a white elephant. Anyhow, it is
our own elephant—white or otherwise—and we
must keep it. Every country has to keep the
elephants which a discriminating Providence
leaves on its doorstep ; therefore the Common-
wealth should take over the Territory with all its
assets and liabilities, and the unfinished railway
leading to it from Adelaide, and wade in to make
the best of it.
William Nathaniel Pratt, manager of the
Juinbunna (Vic.) coal mine, broke loose before
.Commission which is enquiring into the con-
ditions of work in the Gippsland mines, and de-
livered himself of some vigorous remarks on the
T??stion of compulsory arbitration. Part of what
William Nathaniel said :
I consider compulsory arbitration a wolf in sheep’s
clothing. It is a contradiction and an absurdity. . . It
has been a failure where tried. It is an unjust law, com-
pelling* a man to work against his will, and an employer
to keep his works open against his will.
Also, it is possibly unjust and a wolf in cows’ gar-
ments to compel a man to pay his debts against
his will. By the way, when a man doesn’t work
against his will, but simply sits down and rests, the
law often sends him to gaol for having “no
visible.” Would Bill Nathaniel Pratt abolish the
vagrancy law as being also a wolf in bulls’ clothes,
or something like that ?
THE IMAGE WITH THE FEET OF OLAY.
“Joe” Chamberlain stated in a recent speech
that “the idol of free imports was shaken to its
base.”
In 1894 Canada produced 25,685 tons of
steel and rolled iron. That wasn’t much, but it
was more than Australia produces even now.
Then Canada built up a huge tariff-wall to protect
its little industry, and in 1904 it produced 382,822
tons. _ That, and other things like it, fully
explains why Canada gets so many immigrants
while Australia gets so few.
When Thos. Bent chooses to say things
about Fat, he generally manages to say something
that hurts. When, however, he says he’s going
to do something against Fat, Fat always wags a
threatening forefinger at him and Thos. subsides.
This is what Thos. said at Warracknabeal (Vic.)
the other day:—
I know something of the big swells of this State. Years
ago they got the land in big areas at a cheap rate. Then
they built big houses. Then the girls wanted to live in
Melbourne, and after a while on the Continent, and the
big swell" iollowed them, and continually cried “ Stink-
ing Pish ” ..bout the country which had behaved so well
to them. Ik: uv the run of these swells, because I have
been in Parliament 31 years, except when I was given a
rest. The time has arrived when one and all should
stand up for their country.
But, alas, Thos. does not act up to those brave
words.
Another cry of protest:—
The Australian Parliament has closed its session
ingloriously. It began with a useful achievement by
casting Iteid into the place of forgotten things, and,
having done that, it appeared too exhausted to do any-
thing further. The affable and ineffectual Mr. Deakin
came into power, and spent several months acting in a
polite and useless manner. While Reid went away, and
began to develop a lucrative practice in the Sydney law
courts, Reid’s little coterie of obstructionists held
up the business of Parliament until Deakin’s
Labor supporters forced him to take steps to put
an end to this farce. It seemed possible that
thereafter the affable Mr. Deakin might ask Parlia-
ment to do some work. But he remained ineffectual.
He passed the Trades Marks Bill, containing some
measure of useful legislation. An Electoral Bill about
which practically no difference of opinion existed; a
measure to placate the sensibilities of Japan, and a slight
alteration of the law regarding contract immigrants, were
sent through Parliament. The affable Mr. Deakin was
not the man to spur a lazy Parliament to work. The 110
men whom Australia pays to make its laws were not in a
law-making frame of mind, and to oblige their tired feeling
Deakin began to drop things overboard. He dropped a
promise (just or unjust) to give compensation to Colonels
Price and Baylv. He dropped the Bill that aimed at
giving some aid to the production of Australian iron.
He dropped the Bill that set out to protect Australian
manufacturers from greedy foreign trusts. He dropped
the Federal Capital Bill. By the time the end of the
session arrived, Deakin had succeeded in dropping almost
everything but his affability.

“ Two Seas ” : A melancholy feature of
the back country is the number of bush workers
travelling through miles of “improvement leases”
looking in vain for work. At present the “great
pastoral industry” doesn’t, on the average pro
vide permanent work for one man per 5000 acres
I would suggest to Joseph that, instead of bleating
defiance at the Australian Government like a little
woolly lamb on wheels, he should legislate that
all pastoral leases henceforth granted carry labor
conditions. A mining lease must employ one
man (usually) to every five acres. Why shouldn’t
a pastoral lease carry a similar obligation—if onlv
one man to every 1000 acres ? J
“T.D.,” from London :
• T , he °how commences nowadays to get a footing even
in dear old England ; the yellow sailor-man is deserting
his ships whenever he gets an opportunity,, and settling
down in the big commercial to /ns. It is calculated that
there are now over 1000 Chinar en in Liverpool, and several
hundreds more in Birkenhead, whilst much of the laundry
business in Manchester has fallen into Chow hands. In
Cardiff and the smaller seaports the Chinese are securing
a footing, and the first people to offer up protests are
those who so strongly favor the yellow peril in South
Africa, and deprecate the anti-alien legislation of Aus-
tral'a;, The Harmsworth Mail, however, Mails the Chow
with delight, and says that the police give him an excel-
lent character. That far-seeing journal seems to approve
exceedingly of the fact that in the typical Chinese
lodging-houses of Liverpool and Manchester, “opium-
smoking is the chief pleasure,” and states approvingly
rru A* l Sin is proud to marry English women.'”
The Chow is not firmly established yet, but when he is
tairly settled, and a few of him have gone back to the
Orient with comparative wealth and pointed out the
advantages of England as a field for Private Enterprise of
the yellow variety, and Chow Private Enterprise comes
along in good numbers, the British worker will find that
his Australian brother wasn’t so far out after all. But by
that time the Chow Empire will probably be bound in
alliance with the British ditto, and the man who desires
to cast out the yellow citizen will be denounced as a
traitor and a pro-Caucasian and a person who wishes to
do his country much harm.

The figures submitted herein are not the product of

bouth Wales man will claim for his State the primacy
amongst her equals. Primacy, that is, in importance and

Out of four Federal Premiers to date N.S.W. has
supplied three. It has had more than its fair pro-
portion of Ministerial billets. Of the three High
Court judges two are N.S.W. born and the other
was educated in N.S.W. The sole P.S. Oommis-
sioner and many other important billet-holders
are from N.S.W. And if the State’s large propor-
tion—nearly one-third—of Federal Representa-
tives have not the influence they should have, the
fault lies with the people of N.S.W. for sending
such men there.
Another bogey is being worked up to fill
the place of the frayed-out Six Hatters and the
submerged Petrianas and the cracked Potters,
this time it is a Syrian, Malouf, a prohibited
immigrant, who got into Australia Dec., 1903, on
exemption for four months, to help his mother to
wind-up a business. Finding he was in a good
place, Malouf resolved to try and stay on, and on
y?i]. ous P re^ ex tB managed to remain until Dec.,
I.JOo. On Jan. 17, 1905, the American Consul,
applying for about the fourth exemption
extension, pledged his word that Malouf,
it allowed to stay until Dec. 31, 1905
would then surely go. In spite of that pledge]
Malouf raised a clamorous cry last week that he
should be allowed to remain permanently. And
the fact that he was met by a properly firm refusal
is being twisted by the Sydney papers into
another grave wrong against Joseph Carruthers.
Tiip papers represented Malouf as a man who
had been led to come here and start a new busi-
ness not wind up an old one—under an elaborate
series of false pretences, and lied magnificently
over him. But the plain brutal fact is that Aus-
tralia does not want Malouf or any other Syrians
and Malouf must get.

A costly block of land, alongside the Mel-
bourne Supreme Court, which a Victorian Govern-
ment bought some years ago for a new City Court,
is just now being used as a grazing paddock for
one cow. The animal is just a common poley
cow, and doesn’t seem to put on any “dog” over
the circumstance, but it is none the less a fact
that every mouthful of grass that cow eats costs
the suffering State about sixpence !
“Two Seas” : Long years ago I was an
apprentice on a ship that was unloading Mauritius
sugar in Bombay harbor. My duty was to see
that the niggers at work in the hold did not broach
the cargo. One day I came softly upon a group
scooping sugar into their capacious maws by the
double handful. The first to note my presence
was a pot-bellied serang, and he at once fell upon
his astonished mates with both fists. “ Whaffor
Kj blully tiefs steal ’em sugar?” he cried.
en, with his own black mug glistening with
sugar, he explained to me that he viewed the be-
havior of his countrymen with disgust.. ’Twas a
long time ago, yet whenever I read that our own
pot-bellied political serang with a glass eye has
been denouncing anything or anybody in the
Australian Parliament, I form a mental picture of
him —not as a responsible statesman, but as an
oily nigger, clad only in a dirty turban and a
waist-cloth, and his stout face encrusted with
the sugar he is denouncing the other party for
having stolen.
“ T.D.” from London :
Have just been having a look through a new “ Settlers’
Guide ” issued by Minister Jack Murray at great expense
to the Victorian Government, and sent to London for
wide distribution through Agent-General Taverner. The
book contains precisely the information that nobody in
England wants, and the one or two facts which might
possibly be of some use are stowed away in such a mass
of verbiage that they would take a week or two to find.
Some magnificent word-painter by the name of Orooke
contributes an article in which he constantly refers to
himself and indulges in general statements to the effect
that “ Victoria may, without fear of serious criticism, be
said to have been endowed by Providence with a most
plentiful stock of valuable arboreal vegetation.” Like-
wise, there is an excellent portrait of John Murray, and
an article on the geological formation of soils, which
contains many words and statements which the “ intend-
ing settler ” won’t understand unless he takes a special
course of instruction for a year or two. Another
document of which there is much floating around just at
present is the “Journal” issued by the Victorian
Agricultural Department, the main purpose of which
seems to be to advertise Victoria in England a,s a place
infested with poultry tick, cattle tick, swine fever,
phylloxera and all the scourges that ever afflicted nature.
The object of sending these weighty books to London is
understood to be that of inducing people to make tracks
by the first boat to Victoria. The statements about
drought and cattle plague, and the portrait of Jack
Murray ought to fetch them.

BREWERY AND OFFICES:
DOWLING ST., REDFERN, SYDNEY.
SPORTING NOTIONS-
N.S.W. police force received its greatest set-
back for many years on Thursday night last, when it
essayed to break into the two-up school in Pitt-street,
Sydney, and took so long over the job that the gamblers
wandered out by the secret exits and came round and
cheered the copmen on to greater efforts against the
steel doors that barred their passage. The Pitt-street
establishment is pretty solid. Even after the police had
burst in the front door they found a steel door a few feet
down the passage, and to get past it they? burst out
through the passage wall into the shop adjoining, and
thence burst in again when they judged they were past
the steel door. Their calculations were quite correct, but
the two-up people had thoughtfully provided steel doors
every few feet, and the windows were protected by
barbed-wire entanglements. But good as is that Pitt-
street emporium it is not quite perfect, and the writer
intends to float a company to construct the absolutely
impregnable gambling den. The building will be con-
structed of freestone lined with a skin of cement and an
inner skin of steel. The passages will all ascend towards
the centre of the building, and when the last steel doors
are blown in by the raiding police the operation will
burst large tanks of water, ana the contents will wash the
police back into the street. The water will be mixed with
large quantities of blue indelible ink, which will leave the
force of a nice bright tint. From the ring room, which
will be in the centre of the building, a couple of
underground passages will lead to adjacenthouses. These
passages will also be fitted with steel grills, and a patent
contrivance will fill them with water if anyone tries to
enter by them. As a last resource the gamblers will re-
tire to the tower on the top of the building, closing the
steel compartment doors behind them and filling the
intervening spaces with water. From the tower they will
be taken off to a place of safety by an airship of the
writer’s own invention. Then men could two-up in quiet
comfort.
» » * *
At the A.J.C. summer meeting all the six
winners were by imported sires—Grafton (2), Positano,
Sir Foote, Haut Brion, and Simmer, and not one of them
started first favorite. At the V.A.T.O. meeting, on the
same Boxing Day, the failure of the favorites was equally
complete. Moral: Don’t take Ikey’s shortest odds at
Christmas meetings.
* * * *
For once, W. P. Crick’s luck was right in.
Ossian had so often failed to do anything, in spite of the
fact that his owner had planked his affluence on him,
that Moses thought he might meet the plunging ex-Lands
Minister with perfect safety ; and then—Oollarit and
Ossian both won. Israel went home and rent its
frock-coat, and hoped that it might find itself in Russia
if it ever played the fool in that manner again. Crick’s
old colleague, Perry, drew Ossian in Tattersall’s sweep,
and immediately laid William Patrick one-third of the
£4OOO, and altogether, with that little sum added to what
he wrung from Ike.v Noses, William Pat. is understood to
have cleared nearly £BOOO. Had Crick backed Collarit
for the Nursery Handicap to anything like the same
extent as he did Ossian for the Summer Cup—well, it
would have been as good as a small Mount Morgan.
Marvel Loch, seemingly, is a flyer up to 12 fur-
longs only, like many another Caulfieid Cup and Metro-
politan winner before her. It was want of stamina,
rather than the heavy burden on her back, that stopped
the mare in the Summer Cup last week. Paris, of old,
was, perhaps, the most brilliant of the mile-and-a-half
specialists that Australia has seen. Paris w’on a couple
of Caulfield Cups, but never won a race of consequence
over a longer course. Hymettus w'as another horse of the
same class—something more than a non-stayer, yet less
than a stayer as compared with a Lord Cardigan or a
Tartan. All the same, the Lochiels are noted for gaining
stamina with age, and Marvel Loch may get among the
first three in nex t Sydney or Melbourne Cup. Where are
the first-class animals (bar Tartan) that can go two miles
without flinching, these times ?
* * * *
The way Antonious, the son of Sir Foote, won
his Nursery Handicap from a field of 17 after a long and
sustained run, marks him as a coming champion, and a
horse to be considered in anything he starts for.
The winning of Tattersall’s Club Cup was an
interesting business. On Saturday night owner Wootton,
strolling into the Club, heard £5OO to £3O offered against
his horse for the big race. Shern, who had just shied at
4 to 1 against Ossian, grew suddenly venturesome when
the owner of Fabric said he would take £lOOO to £3O, and
booked it. Wootton’s offer to take another £lOOO to £4O
was declined with thanks. Out at the course on
Monday the fielders were more venturesome, and
were calling 6 to 1 against Fabric and Elvo, but
only 7 to 2 against Ossian. Cakewalk and Marvel Loch
were going at 4 and 5 to 1 respectively. Cakewalk
started at a bound, and streaked away at a great pace,
with an apparent intention of getting a break on the
field. For the first mile and'a half he led the way, and then
Fabric ran up close, ranged alongside and passed him.
With Fabric came Elvo and Marvel Loch, and the ex-
hausted pacemaker dropped back to fourth place. Ossian
and Naphro then passed the luckless Cakewalk, and he
finished sixth. It was a gallant run, but badly managed,
and the horse met the usual fate of pacemakers. The
time (3min. 30sec.) has been tied twice in the race’s his-
tory, by Nightingale in 1894, and Hoop Iron in 1902. In
1903 Postilion beat it, getting over the distance in 3min.
29Jsec., and the record for the race is Overdale’s 1905 time
of 3min. 28sec.
• * * *
William Patrick Crick is understood to have
made a desperate effort to lose the cash he accumulated
over his double win during the A. J.C. meeting. But the
sons of Shem had no faith in the Law of Chances, and
declined to raise their price from threes to the “4tol
against ” that William P. asked, and no business resulted.
When they saw Ossian struggling home fourth they were
very sorry.

A private, cable tells that Australian bred nags
Long Tom, Great Scot, and Munderah filled the places in
the Viceroy’s Cup at Calcutta on Boxing Daj-. Long Tom
was bought for India after last A. J.C. Autumn meeting
for 1000 guineas, and Great Scot won the two previous
Viceroy’s Cup.
* * * *
Czarovitch, winner of Perth Cup, Wednesday
last, promised big as a two-year-old, and then failed so
badly the two following seasons that his owner gladly
parted with him for 120 guineas. The Westralian heat
and conditions seem to have improved the horse
wonderfully, as they did many others from the eastern
side, particularly Blue Spec. Czarovitch now belongs to
P. A. Connolly, who also owns Blue Spec, which appropri-
ated last year’s Cup for him, and the same owner has
r iked in quite a lot of big things lately. If he is wise he
will sell out now while the profit is substantial, for as
Solomon said of the game, there is no profit in it in the
long run.
* ♦ * *
Tattersall’s Steeplechase at Rand wick on
Monday was a dispiriting business. Five started. Shooting
Star was the first to fall, and was destroyed. Then Royal
Standard came down at the second fence, while May Not
refused the fence at the top of the hill. This series of
misadventures left the winner, Verger, about a furlong
and a half in front of everything, and people had forgotten
all about the race when May Not drifted in like a purely
accidental horse and got third prize.
The Select Committee appointed by the
Westralian Legislative Assembly to inquire into horse-
racing in the Golden State practically advised that the
State should take over the control of unregistered racing,
and the clubs should go on drawing the profits and
having the fun. Which is so very Westralian ! Westralia
has, it seems, only two unregistered clubs, and each of
them supplies 60 days racing per annum—l2o days of
unregistered racing out of 365 ! The registered clubs
have 19 days among the three big clubs. Practically the
committee finds that unregistered racing is a curse, and
instead of recommending its abolition on that account,
the committee proposes that the two curses now in exist-
ence should be given a monopoly by law, and the start-
ing of any opposition unregistered clubs be prohibited. In
return for this actual monopoly the two unregistered
clubs are to submit to State control, and the Governor in
Council is to fix a minimum amount of stakes to be raced
for each day, fix the days per year on which the unregis-
tered clubs may hold race meetings, and, ye gods! fix
the distances over which the races shall be run. The
only astonishing thing about the report is that the com-
mittee didn’t suggest that the proprietors of the unregis-
tered courses should be made members of the Upper
House, have free railway passes granted to them for life,
and have their office expenses defrayed out of loans.
One of many odds the punter has against him !
At Epping, t’other day, Lady Beauchamp went to the
post for Epping Handicap, but the stewards ordered her
back as being too lame to run. Backers of the mare very
properly looked for a refund of their money, only to dis-
cover that there is a wholly unjustifiable rule which
enabled Moses to hang on to it. The air was blue with
oaths for some time afterwards.

Old Marmont is still a shining light among
hurdle-racers when the handicapper doesn’t drop too
heavily on his dwindling “ form.” At Caulfield on Boxing
Day he won another race over the little sticks, giving 541 b.
to the second horse. And the second horse was named
Brilliance. The irony of it 1 At the same meeting the
aged Proceedor carried top weight (9.5) in the Hopetoun
Cup and won handsomely.
* * * *
The Maoriland footballers managed to beat
the district teams of Wales, but by very narrow majori-
ties. Their best exploit, the defeat of Glamorgan by nine
to nil, looks very puny after the 40 to nil wreck they
inflicted on Yorkshire. Newport went down by three
points only, Cardiff was beaten by two, and the last
match in the principality, that against Swansea, was won
by four to three —a field goal to a try. And the lordly
Maorilander has always held the potted goal in awful
contempt. The ex-Maorilander who runs the football
screech of Sydney Telegraph has explained it all away,
and the gist of his columns of screech is that when this
great and glorious team of toeballers triumphs it is
magnificent football—the concentrated essence of pure
Rugby. When it goes down, it is trickery, biassed or
corrupt referee, rough play, and darned roguery gener-
ally. Which is so like the Maorilander. The remaining
match is one against Great Britain.
When it was announced that the Maoriland
footballers would play a match against All France, every
Briton beyond the seas wiped his eyes, and making - a
mental note of the brand he had last- consumed, swore
never to touch it again. But it was not a dream, neither
did it live in the bottle, and an All-France team actually
met the Maorilanders at Paris and did more than England,
Scotland or Ireland could against them. On a heavy
ground the Gauls scored eight points—-a goal and a try-
while the Maorilanders scored 33 points—four goals and
six tries.
* * * *
Champion sculler Jim Stan bury picked up a
£25 handicap single sculls race at Wallaga Lake (N.S.W.)
regatta last week, after conceding an opponent a start of
90 seconds. In the double sculls race, however, the
champion and Nicholls were defeated by Harry and Clem
Bate, after a line race, by barely a boat-length. Stanbury
and Nicholls are old “ cobbers,” having rowed together 17
years ago, after which Nicholls held the South Coast
championship for five years,
' * * * *
Stanbury now gets back heavily on Towns for
the bargain that the ex-champion drove with him when
he asked for a match. When Stanbury won the champion-
ship on the Parramatta a few months back, Towns took 75
per cent, of the steamer money, and now Stanbury declines
to consider a match except on the condition that, win or
lose, he takes 75 per cent, of the steamer money. Stan-
bury admits that the terms are hard, and back’s up the
statement by offering to row anyone but Towns with an
equal division of steamer money. This 75 per cent, idea was
an inspiration of Towns’s, which he justified on the ground
that it cost him a lot of money to obtain the title, he having
to follow wily Jake Gaudaur to Rat Portage in Canada,
and give away everything when he got there. He argues
that as Stanbury will not be put to any such expense, his
terms are rapacious in the extreme. The Stanbury crowd
is said to admit that it is asking for the lion’s share of the
proceeds merely in a spirit of reprisal. In the meantime
the match is off.
* * * *
The easy triumph of the N.S.W. team against
S. Australia and Victoria gives an accurate idea of N.S.W.
cricket. The team contained quite a host of men who
had been sitting on the door-mat awaiting a chance in
Sheffield Shield cricket, and the success of the Southern
trip is rough on the section which has always argued
that if the personnel of the State team were changed,
defeat was certain. Fancy a N.S.W. team without
Trumper and Duff putting up such scores ! N.S.W. is
rich in good young cricketers who only await the oppor-
tunity to illustrate their ability.
* '« * *
When The Bulletin went to press last Tuesday
the N.S.W. cricket team in Victoria had scored 35 without
losing any wickets, and had made a decent start on the
record innings in N.S.W.-Vic. cricket history. When
Mackay and Noble got together the downfall of the
Victorian bowling commenced. Mackay drove the ball
along the grass in a way that gave the fieldsmen no
chance, and Noble fell on it with the ferocity of George
Reid attacking a chocolate cream, slashing and off-
driving in a manner marvellous to watch. It was a sort
of frenzy—a dancing dervish kind of cricket. A few
minutes after 5 o’clock the third century appeared on the
board, and almost immediately afterwards Mackay pulled
one from Saunders round to square-leg, where M'Alister
held it, and one giant was down for 194. Gregory took
his place, and Noble and he saw the day out, when
N.S.W. had scored 370 for the loss of two wickets.
Wednesday found Noble steadier, andatrifle uncomfortable
facing Armstrong, but the second century appeared
opposite his name shortly after the luncheon adjourn-
ment. In spite of frequent changes in the bowling,
shortly after the tea adjournment be was well on his way
towards the third century, when Christian clean bowled
him at the fourth attempt, and he departed in a blaze of
glory with the figures 281 on bis tail. Waddv, Cotter and
Carter accumulated 185 among them, and the day ended
with N.S.W. 796 for eight wickets. Thursday morning
saw the N.S.W. innings end for 805, and the Victorians,
with 438 to make to avoid a defeat on the first innings,
commenced their hopeless task. Warne certainly made a
struggle, putting up 56 before he was bowled bv O’Connor.
Laver made 17 ; Carroll and Carkeek 48 and’ 30 ; but all
the rest were content with single figures or the sign of
the water-fowl. Their tails were obviously right down
The innings ran out very quickly for a miserable 185, and
N.S.W. had won by an innings and 253 runs.
* * * *
As this paper goes to press the Sheffield Shield
match is proceeding between Victoria and South Aus-
tralia on the M.B.C. ground. The South Australians’
first innings had yielded 181, a miserable score. The
Victorians made a shocking start (no less than three of
their men failed to show figures), but making a slight
recovery the stumps were drawn with nine wickets down
for 166. Compared to the N.S.W. display this wretched
showing on both sides is too dreadful for words, and the
less said the better.
*■»*-*
Cotter didn’t lose a great deal of his erratic
trundling in England during the recent tour. In the
match against Victoria he bowled 276 balls for 134 runs
and six wickets—besides adding seven no balls and one
wide to the opponents’ total. Wicket-keeper Carter had
an exceedingly anxious time prancing around to stop the
flying missiles. Carter is a particularly clever wicket-
keeper, but has the appearance of being too small for a
representative player capable of standing the onslaughts
of Cotter. Cotter’s bowling argues the wisdom of
wicket-keeper Kelly’s retirement.
* * * *
The idea has been started of X oily’s benefit match
taking the form of N.S.W. v. Rest of Australia. And as a
large house is the main object, this fixture would attract
more local attention than one against the Australian XI.,
as was first mooted. The career of the XI. is happily in-
terred, and its resurrection would not be pleasant to
anyone.
* * * *
N.S.W. cricketer Mackay, in Association
matches, has scored 1070 runs this season ; while his
inter-State figures are 202, 90, 194—486 runs in three
innings, an average of 162.
* * * »
One thing the Australian cricketers have decided
upon—that future Elevens for England must travel as
bachelors or grass-widowers. No honeymoon players
need apply for places in future teams. But, then, we
have heard that same thing 13 times before. The trouble
is that the cricketers of the future will be young fellows
with their own honeymoons yet to take.
