That industrious cricket recorder, R. H. Camp-
bell, has compiled a “ hard-luck ” list of batsmen, who in
N S.W.-Vic. cricket have topped 90 runs, but failed to
reach 100. Nobody stopped at 99, but A. Christian and
F. A. Iredale each came to grief at 98. Aleck Bannerman,
Jack Worrall, and Frank Iredale all figure twice in the
list.
* * * *
“ F.C.” gives his version : —“The ‘unregistered
cyclists’ ’ race meeting, engineered at Melb. Exhibition
track on Christmas and Boxing nights by Plugger
Bilmartin and Don Walker, was a wearisome and
bedraggled failure. The novelty and the absence of
counter attractions brought some 500 or 600 spectators
on the Monday, but the following night saw an attend-
ance of about half, of whom a liberal proportion were
Ikeys. These paraded their amazing generosity by
bellowing odds of ‘ evens ’ and ‘ 6 to 4 the field ’ —in
fields of ten and fifteen ! As for the class of Ikey who
laid these noble odds, I would sooner risk my coin in a
two-up school or marking Chow lottery tickets. The
riders seemed to be either disqualified ex-Leaguers or
unknown juveniles. Each evening saw some £4O distri-
buted on half a dozen races, of about four heats each ;
but Heaven knows where the coin came from (unless
from the Plugger’s Collingwood pub.), for the takings
wouldn’t pay for the lights. Another £4O was recklessly
splashed (£3O to the first, £lO to the second), in alleged
matches between Walker and Martin, which Walker had
little difficulty in winning. Plugger Bill also pervaded
the track at times in alleged matches against the old first-
flighter, Con Dwyer, on the old high machines, and the
pair toddled round at the breakneck speed of 10 miles an
hour. The only swift circumstance about the draggled
meeting was the way many Ikeys disappeared just before
they were expected to pay out on the last race.”
* * * *
Sydney Summer Nights Syndicate is going to
spice its January meeting with a series of motor-bicycle
races. The first will be between A. E. Austin, of Leich-
hardt, on a 4J h.p. Rex machine, and J. Doherty, of New-
town, on a h.p. Buchet. Both riders are of an inven-
tive turn of mind, and have made improvements to their
engines according to their own designs, and each claims
that the improvements have made his engine absolutely
perfect. A. J. Bergin, the Rozelle rider, has challenged
the winner to a five-mile match for £25 a-side. The first
contest is to be decided by the best of three runs of five
miles each. Doherty tried his improved engine at New-
castle on New Year’s Day, when he conceded Bergin 6sec.
in two miles, and beat him by more than half a lap.
Austin, meanwhile, is lying low.
After all, and contrary to expectation, Lawson
and Mac Fat-land failed to rake in the Austral Wheel race.
T. E. White, of Boulder, won the big- prize. Still, the
Yankees did not have their trip for nothing, the ex-world
champion taking a five-mile and a three-mile scratch
race, and a half-mile handicap. MacFarland won the
First-class Handicap; and, in addition, the two cracks
are understood to have been generously treated in the
matter of appearance money.
* * * ♦
Bill Martin’s experiment of an unregistered
“ cycling carnival,” at Melbourne Exhibition Oval, was
not a success, although the dates, Christmas Night and
Boxing Night, were probably well chosen and the weather
favored the spec. Racing for the most part was dull-
unknown men going for paltry stakes. In the principal
events Don Walker proved a bit better than his old
antagonist, Martin, and an absurd “match” on old-
fashioned bikes between Martin and Con Dwyer gave
Plugger Bill his only win. The spectacle of two men
perched on tall machines was a novelty for the youngsters
present, but if there is any popular demand for sporting
studies in the antique a tricycle race would be more
attractive.
* * * *
Victorian League of Wheelmen promptly dis-
qualified Don Walker for 12 months for taking part in
that “ unregistered ” cycle-meeting at Melbourne. It
was an inglorious ending to Walker’s wonderful Australian
career as a League rider ; and his retirement has taken
the shape of throwing brick 3 at the League out of which
he has made a lot of money. The Victorian League would
have been justified in disqualifying him for life as a
deterrent.
* * * if
The new idea of publishing cycling training
notes, with full information of how Herr Sauerkraut held
the watch while Saussagestein covered the 100yds. in 6sec.,
and Saussagestein afterwards performed the kind office
while Sauerkraut whizzed over the distance in a fraction
less time, is just plain unmitigated rot. Nobody
but a born idiot who wants to try another
way of losing, just to see what it is like,
would dream of using such information as data on which
to base his wagers. It is one thing to equal the best
times riding on a track that is empty, save for the trier
and his pacer, and another to do it after a gruelling run
in a handicap, with the front markers comparatively
fresh, and all bunched together.
* * * *
S. S. Levey, who won the Austral but was
disqualified for forcing Sandberg up the bank, having had
his appeal dismissed, now threatens recourse to the law.
As he subscribed to the League’s Rule 145, which
stipulates that the Appeal Board’s decision shall be final
and binding without recourse to law, it looks as if the
pugnacious young man were throwing good money after
bad. But he challenges the Appeal Board’s constitution
and its conduct of the inquiry, on the ground that his
witnesses were excluded from the inquiry room while the
disqualifying stewards were allowed to cross-examine and
afterwards to give evidence themselves ; and he may pos-
sibly find a leg to stand on. But the £5OO won’t go very
far in “ costs.”
**•**.
Now that the Austral Wheel Race is all over,
and the damage, if any, is done, Chief Secretary Gillott has
arisen in great wrath and demanded of the police heads
why they didn’t suppress all betting at the bike races.
Gillott is president of the M.8.C., and says he can’t very
well prosecute himself—though he omits to say why—
but somebody belonging to the club badly wants
to be talked to. It was chiefly at the instance of
this M.B.C. that the law prohibiting betting at sports-
meetings was passed, and notices drawing attention
to such prohibition were prominently posted, yet the
blessed club actually licensed 55 Ikeys to ply their calling
(or bellowing) at ss. per head per meeting—there were
seven meetings—and gave permits to 30 sweep-promoters
at 2s. 6d. per head per meeting. A club that first pro-
hibits betting and then sets out to collect £l2O out of the
illegal traffic is a poor kind of object. And if Gillott, as
president, isn’t responsible, why isn’t he ? And who is?
• * • *
Marsh, the aboriginal cricketer, was up at
Sydney Central Police Court last week under the Vagrant
Act, for having- no lawful visible means of support. The
beak discharg-ed him with a caution. As a matter of law,
no aborigine can be charged under the Vagrant Act. He
is supposed to live on ’possum, which may he lawful, but
isn’t visible unless you know where to look for it.
* * * *
The Highlanders have this year deserted the
Sydney Cricket Ground for their annual display, and soon
the ground trustees will have to consider the position.
The tariff of membership—£2 2s. per annum and an
entrance fee of £3 Ss. —is high, and its only justification
is an attractive programme of sports. This season
cricket, owing to the poor quality of visiting teams,
promises to be vapidly uninteresting. Cycling is
on its uppers, and an odd football match or
two is really the only attempt at an attrac-
tion. The feeble efforts to fill up gaps by semi-
music-hall-semi-circus sort of desolate entertainment
don’t go really far. There is a popular idea that the
members’ stands are filled with dead-heads ; still the steady
income from members’ subscriptions is as much to the
trustees as an occasional bumper house in the other parts
of the ground. And to keep up this steady income it
behoves the trustees to scratch their bald pates and
devise some attractions.
* * * *
One defaulting Melbourne clerk, who gotthrough
over £lOOO of his firm’s money, declares that he lost the
coin in a two-up school. He says he lost £6O at one stand,
and had seen plungers rake in £3OO for an evening’s earn-
ings. Melbourne employers are now taking extra pre-
cautions to ascertain if their clerks attend “ school.”
* * * „
Professional athletics seem to be on the up-
grade just now in Australia. The recent Kalgoorlie
racing was so successful that a second Kalgoorlie Hun-
dred meeting will commence on January 16th. In
Sydney the governing professional body is carrying on a
war of extermination against stiff runners, and is doing
the business a lot of good. Only last week four were
suspended for three months, and two others passed out
for a “sixer.” Once it ceases to pay to be dishonest, the
professional sprinter will develop all the virtue of a
Galahad. As the League announces its intention of
dealing out heavier sentences in the future, there is
every promise of a fine crop of Galahads, and although
this writer has no more love for a synthetic virtue than
he has for a synthetic perfume, he prefers a synthetic
virtue to no virtue at all.
* * « »
Although there were no sailing races on Sydney
Harbor on Saturday before New Year there was some
beautiful navigation. Writer went up the usually peace-
ful Parramatta in a motor launch that heeled over before
the howling nor’-easter like a torpedoed battleship. Down
the river, with the howling gale behind them, rushed
three sailing boats, the crews, perched up on the side
that was out of the water, baling for their lives. Every
wave went clean into the boats, and the great white-
winged things ran clean through many waves, but they
flew so fast before the howling gale that they could not
go over, for even a sailing boat can only go one way at a
time, and as they rushed past and disappeared in the
mist and spume they were very beautiful—as beautiful as
they were uncomfortable. It is to be hoped the sailing
boat will never go quite out of fashion ; it makes the
harbor look so picturesque for the people smoking snugly
behind the windows of the motor launch.
■‘Jab.”: Is it possible that appendicitis was
not really responsible for swimmer Kieran’s death?
Late crack N.S.W. lightweight boxer Herb M’Kell com-
plained of abdominal pains, and was “ satisfactoryv ”
operated on for appendicitis, but after “ progressing
favorably ” for several days the trouble appeared as bad
as ever, and the knife was used on the left side and back.
Yet he died, and a post mortem revealed some intestinal
trouble not suspected.
The period of peace on earth and good will to
men was broken up at Sydney Gaiety Athletic: Club by a
disturbance between Walcott and Wilkes. Mr. Walcott
is a gentleman of color, and, although the white man had
the advantage of reach and .youth, plugged steadily away,
taking his punishment like a Trojan, and dealing it out
whenever he could reach his lighter-footed opponent. In
the. seventh round the colored man landed his right
behind the ear and broug'ht his left to the jaw, and down
went Wilkes. At the word eight he was rising, when
the police intimated that they were satisfied, and Walcott
was declared the winner, while Wilkes went home to
commence the New Year with a deteriorated counten-
ance.
* * * *
Now that Kieran has gone, people are beginning
to ask who will take his place in this section of the uni-
verse. At present there is no one about who can cover
all the ground Kieran did, hut Cecil Healey has more
than a possible. So far he has not cultivated himself any
—merely trained for specific events and won them. If he
would settle down to build up his physique there is little
doubt that he would beat anybody who is likely to come
on deck at the present moment.
* * * ' *
N.S.W. Amateur Swimming Association has
inaugurated a new by-law compelling clubs that wish to
forfeit grade fixtures to give 24 hours’ notice to the Asso-
ciation, or pay a fine of ss.—which isn’t nearly enough. A
similar by-law (with a bigger fine) is badly needed in other
branches of sport. After paying admission-charges, to
find that the bottom of the programme has fallen out is-
annoying, and it isn’t always possible to forecast where a
disappointed crowd will stop in order to get revenge.
* * * *
“J.” :When I called on the garage-man and
asked him could he let me have a motor car for next
Sunday, he assured me it was as easy as drinking.
“ You’ll want it for the afternoon, of course ?” “ No, the
morning, want to go for an 80-mile spin out into the
country, where the bees sing and the birds ” “ Hum !
Don’t know if I can manage that. Tell you what I’ll do.
It’ll cost you eight guineas, and you leave your telephone
number and I’ll ring you up and let you know if I can fix
you.” Every garage in Sydney talks like that, and the
reason is that the naicest of Sydney, who have paid
anything between £5OO and £750 for a car, turn an
honest coin by hiring the thing to various garages when
they are not using it themselves. But the majority
of them bar long spins into the country, and fix a
suburban radius, outside of which their vehicles must not
go. No matter how great the rush, some garage can al-
ways get you a car to run to National Park or Parra-
matta or somewhere within shanghai range ; but often
neither love nor money will obtain a snorter for a run to,
say, Moss Vale. When the garage man has to fall back
on that class of car he can never give a direct answer ; he
has to find out if Mrs. de Darlingpoint is going out, or if
the jigger is in the hospital because old Darlingpoint was
so heavily freighted with whisky that the lamp-post got
in the way, or something of that sort.
* * * *
A Presbyterian church in one of the Melbourne
suburbs is getting the name of a mascot. In 1904 one. of
its prominent members drew first prize in Tatts. In
1905, in the very same kirk, while the minister was
preparing a biting sermon on the evils of gambling, report
reached him that one of the elders had drawn another
big prize in the great gamble.

Mr. Seddon opens up a new field of political
possibilities by his decision to “shout” to Maoriland
footballers now in England, out of the Treasury, and pos-
sibly out of loan funds, a holiday trip through America.
A “permanent and reproductive work” of the future
Loan Estimates (for every item of expenditure one day
finds its way to the Loan Estimates in Australasia) may
be the price of the neat little pub. that a grateful country
handed over as a token of esteem to some pugilist of
derring-do.


One of the most successful means of
quenching the Great Australian Thirst is the
Austral MCrating Water Machine. This imple-
ment of cheerful intentions, manufactured by
Eckersley and Sons, of Queen-street, Melbourne,
has the good word of thousands of hotelkeepers
throughout Australasia and Maoriland for its
time and labor saving properties. Moreover, the
blessing of all Temperance Unions descends upon
it annually like a dove.*
A prominent member of the Federal
Ministry expressed, the other day, the unanimous
opinion of machinery experts when he said the
Simplex Oil Engine is the best, of its fighting
weight, in the world. The Vic. Railway people
recently tried conclusions with it, and were wholly
satisfied with the results. And this after they
had tested a number of imported oil engines for
pumping water. The Simplex is manufactured
by E. Ooulson, of A’Beckett-street, Melb. ; its
improvements are of local invention, and it is a
very hard and sudden fact for the. belittlers. of
Australian industries to bump their scepticism
against.*

Evidence at an inquest re a boiler explosion
at a sawmill at Tallarook (Vie.), whereby three
men were killed, draws fresh attention to the need
of regular inspection of boilers and engines, and
of the men who work them. Usually, when
a boiler bursts, the fault is found to be
that its plates were rotten and worn; a man
putting up bush machinery generally buys a
cast-off boiler when its anginal owner has
abandoned it as beyond service. Here, the boiler
appea-s to have had plates sound enough to stand
a pressure of 75 to 801 b., beyond which (said expert
evidence) no experienced man would have dreamed
of using it. But those in charge had the safety-
valve overweighted, and the pressure ran up to
1501 b. Then the boiler burst in every place at
once.
Probably no man ever showed more de-
votion to his hat than did an excursionist near
Ballarat the other day. He was riding in an open
truck (the way Victoria accommodates most of the
people who want to travel in holiday-time), when
a gust of wind blew the hat off. He made a
desperate leap to catch it, and jumped right over
the side of the truck to the permanent way. Ihe
engine was uncoupled to run him back to Ballarat,
where he died of a fractured skull within a few
hours. The hat was picked up by the engine-
driver and taken back to Ballarat too.
Supporters of a Black Australia would do
well to study a recent discovery by Professor
Clarkson, of Columbia (U.S.) University. The
fact he has elicited has long been known to all
breeders of stock, but it has been left to Clarkson
to prove what was merely a theory in regard to
human beings. He has found that, when a white
woman bears a child to a negro, and subsequently
bears other children to a white man, the latter
progeny carry with them some of the negro taint,
and, though their children may be apparently
pure white, there is a chance that their grand-
children may have among them a little woolly-
headed coon as black as if both its parents were
fresh from the cotton fields of Alabama. This is
further reaching and more serious than the imme-
diate throw-back, which led a U.S. Senator, who
married a beautiful octoroon girl with scarcely a
trace of her black origin about her, to become the
father of an apparently full-blooded nigger kid.
“ P.” : There are some good blokes about.
Walking on Christmas Saturday up Pitt-street,
Sydney, and marvelling at the number of Chinese
“ importers ” who hang out their shingles in that
region, I felt a timid touch on my arm. I was
asked by a dilapidated, skinny youth of 17 or so
to “ give us a smoke.” I gave him my cigarette
box, from which he gratefully took two “smokes.”
Then, by request, I gave him my match-box, and
he bashfully took DO per cent, of the contents.
Next, he raised his cigarette-adorned “chiv’
to mine, and said: “O’ny wastin’ matches
ter strike in this wind; give us a kiss.
I kissed him according to local custom
among blokes by igniting his cigarette from mine.
Then he said, “By Ghost! Great feeds about
now.” "Yes,” said I, genially. “ Harmy’s givin’
it out ter day, an’ some other push ’s cornin’ ter
the rescue at ther Town ’All, C’ris’mas Day. By
Ghost!” He threw his “bumper” into the
gutter, and took three more cigarettes from my
box. “Yer ought ter go ter day; it’ll be
a bosker feed. I’ll get yer a ticket, if yer
like. I got two other blokes tickets this
morning’.” He turned, seemingly to hurry off
and procure my ticket, when I stopped him
with the apology that I was pledged to dine with
the Governor-General. “Wot ’ard luck! he
said, regretfully; “yer won’t ’ave a chance of a
feed like this fer another twelve munse. O’ris’mas
o’ny comes once a year, yer know.” Then he
emptied my cigarette box, and went off, with his
shoulders squared under the stimulus -of that
prospective “ booker feed.” I think he was a good
bloke.

“ Alix ” : How are the mighty fallen!
I bought a threepenny Christmas stocking, and
found in it three sweets, a wooden doll, a tin
shovel, and a patriotic song on “ Our Heroes in
South Africa ! ” Presumably that is the only way
they can get rid of the African blood-song in
these degenerate days.

“ Dependence Implies Subordination.”
—
In a recent Westminster Gazette, a leading
English Liberal newspaper, there is an article by
Harold Cox, a former secretary of the Cobden
Club, and a prominent Liberal, on “ The Imperial
Government and Colonial Opinion.” Mr. Harold
Cox’s personal views are not of much importance
to Australia, but when they are published, with-
out any editorial comment, by a leading party
newspaper, they may be fairly taken to represent
something more than the irresponsible opinions of
an individual. This is what Mr. Cox says about
the Australian Immigration Restriction Act:—
A conflict of opinion (between the Imperial Government
and the Commonwealth) did arise a few years ago when
the Commonwealth Parliament passed a law which had
the effect of excluding Japanese subjects and our own
Indian subjects from the huge continent of Aus-
tralia. This law was, and is, utterly at variance
with the liberal traditions of this country. ... The
right of self-government possessed by Australia in no
way debarred us from interfering in this matter. We
have granted to the Australian colonists the right to
manage their own internal affairs. We have not granted
to them the right to manage their relations with outside
countries, and we cannot grant that right, because it
would be useless to them unless we were prepared to
enforce their decisions.... The Australians not only
make no contribution to the general defence of the
Empire, but even their own purely local defence is largely
KpL'S m h K
practically reminded that dependence implies subordm-
ation.
mi • ~ . . ~ ,• , . cnWonto
Thui throws an interesting light; on
It shows, first that there are “swh whi
admitting that we have as a gift the people
is* the
right of the Imperial Government to determine
what are our own exclusively internal affairs. It
shows, second, the impression created in the minds
of English politicians by our miserable policy of
hiring our naval defence from the Admiralty. As
far as the first of these points is concerned, it
doesn’t seem to have occurred to the writer, or the
people who think with him, that any claim on the
part of the Imperial Government to decide what
are matters of exclusively Australian interest
cannot be tolerated by any self-respecting
Australian Government. If we really have
the right to manage our own internal
affairs, it is essential to the exercise of that
right that we should also have some voice in decid-
ing what are our internal affairs. And if there
is one matter above all others, which Australians
regard as of exclusively Australian concern,
it is the character of the population which is
to inhabit this continent. If our connection with
the Empire involves an obligation to admit into
Australia any race which, thanks to the earth-
hunger of rapacious Jingoes, has been included in
the category of British subjects, or which, for
reasons to which we had nothing to say, has been
admitted into political alliance with Great Britain,
the most ardent believer in the Imperial connection
will begin to get uncomfortable. A piebald Empire
is the poorest of reasons for a piebald Australia.
The jeer at Australia’s defence policy, with which
Mr. Cox concludes his references, is ungenerous
and undignified. But it is not, unfortunately,
without justification. As long as Australia re-
mains content with the miserable policy of
dependence on Britain for its naval defence, it is
true, as the writer says, we “ have no right to
complain when practically reminded that depend-
ence implies subordination. ” That comment comes
with very bad grace from Englishmen, whose
official representatives are mainly responsible for
our existing conditions of “dependence.” In 1899,
when a practical scheme of independent naval
defence for Australia was formulated by a com-
mittee of Australian naval officers, it was re-
ceived with indignation and ridicule by the Eng-
lish naval authorities. Australians were bluntly
told by the Times, the unofficial spokesman for
the Admiralty, that the naval defence of Aus-
tralia was not Australia’s business, but essentially
the business of the British Navy. This view was
strongly urged by Lord Selborne and other naval
authorities at the Conference of 1902, and it is the
underlying principle of the unfortunate naval
agreement which was drawn up at that Confer-
ence. Only the other day Admiral Fanshawk
reiterated the old story, and told us that any
attempt at naval defence on our part was an ab-
surdity. However, the bad taste of the comment
doesn’t matter much. What does matter is its
truth. As long as Australians are content with
the “ dependence ” involved in the mean-spirited
system of buying their naval defence from Britain
it is very difficult for them to repudiate the
subordination which their dependence implies.
Australia can never claim complete self-govern-
ment until it is in a position to defend itself from
all real risks.
»
N.S.W. State Parliament once sat into
Sunday morning. In 1886, in the Premiership of
Jennings, the 5 per cent. Customs Duties Bill was
stonewalled by the Foreign-traders from Thursday
until 33 minutes after midnight on Sunday morn-
ing.
Australia, whose exclusion laws are rep-
resented as something beyond the average in
savagery, is one of the few countries m the world
which allow commercial travellers free entry.
Canada and the S’ African colonies both charge a
heavy import duty on the bagman, even if he is a
British bag-gentleman.

TTHE chief feature of the Beale Australian Pianos is the Beale-Vader Patent Iron Wrest-Plank, which forms a part of the usual cast-iron frame. We cast the frames for our pianos in our own foundry
1 thus obtain complete control of the material, worlooJ
ship and design. The Beale-Vader Patent Iron Wrest-Plank takes the place of the wooden plank used in all imported pianos,

with no less than two hundred and sixteen i-mch holes, into which are hammered steel pms and to these are attached the strings or wires. The total strain on these pins is Twenty Tons and the ninoh oi
the timber is all that there is to hold them and keep the strings at true pitch. They do not hold at concert pitch for any length of time, and tuning tends to rub down the fibres of the wood so that it losai
its pmch and the pins become loose. Hence our invention of an iron wrest-plank. We shall be pleased to send you a booklet, giving full information on the subject, on receipt of your name and

“ Mulgarian”: Let me ask in all reverence
-Is thecferical section of the Cold-Water party
acting in the best interests of the Church by its
strenuous efforts to restrict the liquor trade > I
£ not Afc first si s ht thls T ould
heterodox even to the verge of blasphemy, but the
standpoint from which I regard the question is
less a moral than a fiscal one, there being, in my
opinion, a co-relation between the offertory bag
and the hotel-till. In other words the value of
offerings is regulated by the price of drinks. I
support whereof : .
m On the W.A. goldfields drinks were a shilling. Then
no one put less than that sum in the plate ; but the in-
stallation of sixpenny beers was a blow to the foundations
of the local tabernacle, from which it has never recovered.
(2) When the little town wherein I live was first
founded, we had to part 2s. for our nips. A visiting
evangelist who arrived then got a half-crown gate, and
went away insensible from surprise. . ,
Cl) Sydney was the pioneer of the threepenny drink
movement. The Wail of the Thrum was heard in the
land earlier there than elsewhere in Australia.
14) In England it is possible to obtain even a ha porth
of liquor ; and the falling of the halfpenny in the plate
drowns the voice of true religion.
An “ offering ”is not an entity. Like the Chinese
tael, 'it is simply a hypothetical amount, but it
varies, not with the rate of exchange, but with the
price of beer. Should it be possible in the future
to obtain a “’arf o’ 4-’arf ” in Australia as in
England, the diaconal dirge will be sung, not on
the recurrence, but oh the absence, of the despised
thri’penny. In a conventicle I (very) occasionally
attend, there are some apparently-prosperous
persons to whom the saucer is never proffered ;
they prefer to compound by quarterly payments.
These individuals never frequent bars but get in
their drink wholesale and keep it in the cellar.
They pay their liquor bills quarterly, so they also
pay quarterly to the church. Thus does the co-
relation of the Drink and the Plate again assert
itself.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Some humorous misdeeds are charged to the
account of a sanctimonious Melbourne trader, who lately
cleared out and is understood to be now lying low in San
Francisco. The absconder in question started his down-
ward career by taking over the business of a general
importer and taking in a partner with the insufficient
capital of £9OO. The guileless partner seemingly dropped
out of the firm when his working capital was exhausted,
and then the wily Christian gentleman began operations
on his own. Before trying his hand at forgery and
other old-fashioned indiscretions, he played some
new and amusing tricks on a money-lender—of all
people ! For instance, he ordered a box of camphor
from another importer, giving instructions for the goods
to be delivered late in the day. When the goods came
to hand per carrier, the good man professed to be so
full of business that he could hardly find time to sign
for them, and early next morning he rang up the car-
ner, wanting to know why he had brought a box of
camphor round to the office instead of taking it to the
free store. The apologetic carrier rectified the mistake
by calling for the box and carting it to the free
store, where it was accepted as being just off
the ship. Then did the tricky schemer invite
a money-lender to come along with him to the free
store and advance £3O on the security of that case of
newly-imported camphor which was subsequently found
to be full of sawdust, the change having been worked
what time the case was lying at the office. An advance
of £l2O was obtained from the same money-lender on a
case of zithers that were possibly concealed within the
package when the transaction took place, but were cer-
tainly not there when the victim foreclosed on his
‘security.” In pawning his zithers the merchant
referred casually to his high moral standing as
a principal member of the Wesleyan church in his
suburb, and even that didn’t scare his dupe. But
the saddest part of the story relates to a second box of
“camphor” which was pawned for another £3O. The
goods in this instance proved to be bottles of patent
medicine, and the boss of the free store consented to buy
them from the mortgagee for £l7, in consideration of the
circumstances of the case. A wholesale firm of druggists
gave the free-storer a profit of £43 on his deal, and the
moneylender, hearing of this, went forth like St. Peter of
old and wept copiously. Also, he took out a warrant for
the arrest of the missing Wesleyan, but seeing that he
couldn’t get it executed without paying the necessary
police expenses, he has decided to lose his vengeance in
preference to losing some more money.
* * * *
“Robert Brothers”: The Yorkshire Post
waxes awfully indignant because an expert inveighed
against Yorkshire’s shoddy before the Tariff Commission
in Australia, and it pointed the finger of scorn at the
Commission for accepting such evidence. The indigna-
tion, however, finds no expression outside the office of
the Post. Yorkshire weavers do not try to get away
from the imputation, in fact they are rather proud of
their shoddy, which is difficult' to detect from good
tweed. There is no secret about the matter. It is made
and sold as shoddy to suit the pockets of the poor. The
fact that it is sold as good tweed in Australia is the
importer’s lie. Old clothes are put through a devil and
then soaked in acids to destroy the cotton. The product
goes through the mill and comes out looking as good as
tweed. But it is easy to detect by unravelling the staple,
which will be found in short shreds, while good tweed is
always long in the wool. Shoddy suits are sold in Eng-
land at 255., while you cannot get a good tweed suit
under 70s. Yorkshire people in the weaving business
have a,sked me how it is we send wool to England to be
made into tweeds and blankets for our own use. They
think it is a good joke, and generally consider we are
awfully “slow.” “You have the finest wool in the
world in Australia,” said one spinner to me. West of
England tweeds are not very well thought of in York-
shire. “ There was never anything lived so long on a
name,* a weaver told me.
* ■* * #
Australia now pays any prices the Standard Oil
Trust chooses to fix for its kerosene. Besides being used
for illuminating purposes, much oil is now required for
motive power, and more would be used were the price
reasonable. Australia could, by an adjustment of the
tariff, make itself independent of kerosene. For both
illuminating and motive power purposes spirits answer
admirably. The spirits give off no noxious fumes and no
smoke. The clear, colorless flame, giving no light but
much heat, can be rendered light-producing by causing it
to raise to a white heat a mantle such as is now used in
the burning of gas. For motive power, both for fixed
engines and motors, it has the advantage that there is no
deposit of carbon to interfere with the electric ignition
and no trail of suffocating stench lies in the wake of the
car. When spirits are in general use, the motor-car
driver will be able to pick up a fresh supply of fuel at any
town, in place of, as now, having to send his supplies
ahead. Spirits can be distilled from almost anything
that grows. Potatoes and grain of all kinds would be a
constant source of supply. In place of allowing potatoes
tq rot in the ground when prices are low, they could be
converted locally into spirits which would keep for all
time, and thus the prevention of a glutted market would
do much to make potato-growing and the like a certain
and profitable industry in place of the risky speculation
it is at present.

TO-MORROW WE DIE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is some land-, only youngsters now play in it;
It will grow \Vheat—let the kids go to hell.
Crop it as io'rtg as the virtue will stay in it;
WheS,t may go up yet, you never can tell.
Let’s have a dig at it,
Come on, let’s jig at it,
Make our cheques big at it,
Leave no heart in it. To-morrow we die.
There is some ground with a little fine gold iri it,
W orn-out old fossickers make a few bob.
Let’s float a company—when the scrip’s sold ih it
P’rhaps the old battlers will still get a job.
Let’s have our cuts of it,
tick Out the huts of it,
tear out the guts of it;
fteave it and rob it. To-morrow We diie.
there ih the forest the saplings are straight enough,
they wOvild make logs if we left ’erh to grow.
Cut ’em tot (firewood, gap >jj make weight enough,
turns aWay quick ? Well, the people don’t know.
Let’s have a slash at it,
All have a bash at it,
Make a great splash at it,
Hew it and hack it. To-morrow we die.
There are the kiddies, why should they not work a bit ?
We have to work though we’re getting old men.
Keep them well to it, and don’t let them shirk a bit,
Chickens can scratch as well as a hen.
Don’t let ’em sing at it,
Make their hoes ring at it,
Till their brains swing at it,
Bend ’em or break ’em. To-morrow we die.
There is Bull Cohen, he’s got some money left,
Come, let us borrow it whilst still we may.
Who gets the nest will find there’s no honey left;
What will it matter to us turned to clay ?
Let’s have a go for it,
Blather and blow for it,
Give an 1.0. for it.
Get it and spend it. To-morrow we die.

“ Hassan ” : The piecework system has, at
one time and another, come in for a deal of well-
earned abuse. It is curious to find in the byways
of entomology, a brick to heave at it. Some years
ago the British Museum employed a person to
classify and describe certain sections in its col-
lection of insects. Instead of paying him a regu-
lar screw, as they should have done, the authori-
ties gave him a matter of half-a-crown or so for a
classification and a pound for a description.
Ihe result was obvious. He was perhaps poor,
and was certainly not conspicuously honest.
Wherefore he, upon the slightest opportunity,
classified the same insect three or four different
ways, thus receiving enough out of the extra
enumeration of the beast to get an extra drink or
so, and to buy himself a new pair of boots. And
now the entomologists curse him loudly and upset
and correct his classifications wholesale. Which
upsettings may at any time be discovered by the
curious in a frivolous and ephemeral publication
labelled, “ Proceedings of the Lin mean Society.”
Lil : I wasn’t feeling too well the morn-
ing after a joyous outing, but I brightened when a
pamphlet entitled “How to Have a Perfect
Bust ” was poked under the door. To my disgust
it did not tell of a new kind of tipple with no
unpleasant after-effects, but merely advertised
the method of making the front elevation of
woman look more globular than it was intended
to be.

LIFE.
(BY “VIVA.’)
No living thing, from man to a microbe, can exist unless it is continuously dying. It is not merely
necessary for man to absorb nourishment in order to live ; it is equally necessary that, simultaneously,
some portion of his body, bone, blood, or tissue, must waste away and die. It is essential, also, that'll
balance be preserved in the two processes of replenishing the body and the removal of the waste or
dead material which is constantly accumulating within it. An uneven action of either means disease.
A total suspension of either means death.
Of course everyone knows that an adequate supply of suitable food, pure water and fresh air is
requisite to build up the body, but, unfortunately, most people are not as well aware of the complicated
action of the mechanism with which nature has endowed man for dealing with the used-up, dead and
waste material always being produced within us, and which, unless regularly extracted from the blood
and expelled from the body, must cause disease or death.
Upon the skin, lungs, kidneys and liver rests the main responsibility of removing waste matter
from the system. The skin usually requires little attention beyond regular washing with good soap
and water, and it will, in the form of perspiration, rid the body of a quantity of dead matter? A fit of
coughing or a chocking sensation will speedily call our attention to anything which hinders the lungs
in their work of removing, in the form of carbonic acid, an amount of used-up material equivalent to
eight ounces of pure charcoal every day. The kidneys and liver, on the other hand, are delicate organs,
and it is often not until we begin to suffer from some serious complaint that we become aware that
their action is impeded. Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Dis-
orders, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, General Debility, Gravel, Stone, Bladder
I roubles, Depression, Low Temperature, Anaemia, Bright’s Disease, are all caused by disease, or
indifferent action of the kidneys or liver, or both of those organs, permitting the body to retain
urinary and biliary waste poisons which it is their functions to eliminate from the blood and expel in
a natural manner.
It will therefore be seen how important it is that the vigorous health aud activity of the kidneys
and liver should be maintained. A person suffering from one or more of the many complaints men-
tioned should not regard them as diseases, but should adopt the rational course of attacking them at
their true source, by restoring a proper and regular action of the kidneys and liver, when, the cause of
the disorders having been rectified, the waste matter which produces the trouble is removed and
health ensues as a matter of course.
It was the comprehension of this fact which resulted in the discovery of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Certain medical men—being aware of the difficulty of treating the disorders referred to, and knowing
that they were due to inefficient action of the kidneys or the liver, but being unable in most cases to
accurately diagnose just which organ was at fault—set themselves the task of compounding a medicine
which would act specifically upon the kidneys and liver alike. After long research they succeeded
and introduced the medicine to the world under the name of Warner’s Safe Cure. This was about
thirty years ago, and since then many millions of bottles have been sold, and millions of people have
been saved from pain, suffering and death by taking a course of this invaluable specific for all kidney
and liver diseases and disorders arising therefrom. rg

History.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CHAOTIC annals feel my breath,
Creative, warm their clay ;
I read from hieroglyphic Death
The text you heed to-day.
In quarry-holes of Greece and Rome
I hammer crystalled creeds,
And archetypal fossils from
The lava-jetted “leads.”
Mine alchemy, retorting with
The flux of neighboring facts,
From leaden matrices of myth
A silver Age extracts.
In wastes whose wayward glimmers miss
The threshold of the eyes.
My spectroscopic synthesis
Bids realms unknown arise.
The walls of Distance I can rase,
The feuds of race o’erset
With prattle of your baby days
In exile spoken yet.
The score of modern glacier
Upon the oldest rocks
I trace, and rifts that reach you here
From ancient earthquake shocks.
From jumbled empires petrified
In common ruin’s heap,
I bring the crystal’s facet-side
Of each from aeons’ sleep.
I lure with sunlit guess and hint
From crannied time and place,
The negatives through which I print
The Genesis of race.
My Clian breezes playing by
Wrecked arch and monolith,
HColian oracles supply
That ravel tale and myth.
Elusive salts of rumor press,
I average with tact ;
With litmus ordeal I assess
Their bias and their fact.
I rummage plays and chapbooks for
The manners of a time,
And on its darker shadows pore
In chronicles of crime.
In every theologic shard
(My microscopes declare)
Wherever graved, however marred,
The Hand of God is there.
I yoke the Thalian arts to use ;
From climate argue worth ;
From blemish on the sun deduce
A period of dearth ;
From all your Time-barque’s mystic crew
Their poignancies I draw :
Of all their functions glean for you
The biologic law :
How wind-blown raiders silent grow
To rich prey’s shapeliness,
(As parasitic fig-trees slow
The palm or gum obsess);
How here in fastness, there in slums,
Survive the races faint;
How ruder age’s strength becomes
A finer’s felon taint;
How Nemesis on tyrannies
Dews her corrosive rust,
Or waits relentless centuries
Until her scales adjust;
How legends winged with love or hate,
From pods obscure uncurled,
Are lifted by the wind of Fate
And planted o’er the world ;
How selfless acts are madmen’s dreams
When on you come their blows ;
How patriots’ astuteness seems
Obliquity in foes;
How prophets on the tower of Truth
With myriad tongues confound;
How, still, ascetic lonely Youth
Sees visions with Mahound ;
How Europe, brand-new truth to win,
’Neath Eastern temples cast,
Discovers new evangels in
The pupae of her past.
At times I wield the drama’s power,
I plunge you in the past;
You see your own young errors tower
O’er ruined cities vast ;
You find in ancient charnel-rooms
Your baby crimes full-grown ;
You hear, perchance, in older dooms
A presage of your own ;
You see the awful vials pour
That rouse you at your feast;
And, trembling, feel your foreheads for
The signet of the Beast;
Till you, at Death’s reproof aghast,
On Virtue lean, and live —
For, lo ! like rainbow o’er the Past
God’s promise : “ I forgive ! ”
Before I speak I send my seers
To chart minute the scene,
The chroniclers of days and years,
Tempestuous, serene:
They fill me from the masses’ dens,
From caves of coteries,
Museums with the specimens
My science classifies;
They scour the jungle, watch the mote,
The sacred landscapes scan,
All sea-paths map, all passions note,
For monographs on man.
To Truth’s perspective lines I drill
Their crude facts’ disarray ;
Empiric wells of night I fill
With principles of day.
I gauge in voltage absolute
Their great events and small;
Regrade, degrade, reconstitute
World-empire, noble, thrall.
Amanuensis, annalist,
Yea, poet and his theme,
Are but the brook and cradle-mist
Of my Gangetic stream ;
They are the birds who daily rove
To save your daily crumbs ;
I move but when the aeons move,
Come as the Pheenix comes.
The cyclic vision, story, song,
They sing, they tell, they see ;
I am the Homer of their throng—
Am Epic History.
VIC.
BERNARD O'DOWD.
A Gulf Funeral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT is seldom that a full-blooded white
man starts business as an undertaker in the
Gulf country. Chinese, Manilamen, and
Japs rush to earn a few shillings when one of
their countrymen die. The struggle for a body is
not so severe among the whites. The hearse may
be a handcart, or a hastily-rigged carry-all, and
someone may sound a death-horn or kill a pig.
There is a different noise at every funeral.
In the small, nameless Gulf ports, where the
population changes color every year, and where
the children are squat or lean or Hindu-eyed, a
burial becomes a festival. Two things impress
the newcomer from the South—the thirst and the
rapid funerals.
Tommy Tung, a half-caste Chow-Kanaka,
entered the ghoul business with great fervor.
He marked off and fenced a three-acre block
adjoining a mangrove-skirted inlet, and put up
a notice signifying that black and white men
could be buried cheaply on the shortest time-
allowance.
After Tommy had deposited about 30 Kanakas
and Manilamen under the earth, he began, like
his European brother, to wear crape pants and
sheets round his hat. His ideas of a first-class
funeral often led to unexpected results.
The residents of Chow Flat were kept awake at
night by terrible noises in the creek bend. Some-
one suggested that it was Tommy working over-
time. A white official came to the Flat suddenly,
one day, and closed Tommy’s little burying trust.
He pointed out that the cemetery was a mere
swamp that swarmed with alligators. Tommy
had to spend £lO on fireworks to prevent the
Chows and Manilamen from lynching him. The
fireworks created a friendly diversion, but the
“boys” promised to give him a lift next time he
started an alligator farm in the paddock set apart
for their illustrious dead.
* * • *
O’Keefe’s funeral was the biggest thing that
ever happened up North. It lifted itself from the
ruck of ordinary corpse-shivoos and burst into
whiskified splendor.
O’Keefe during life had been a hard man, a
fjearl-sheller and captain, owner of a small fleet of
uggers, and a tyrant in his way. Such men have
large funerals. His daughters worked like
negresses in his cane-field so that his banking
account might keep level with that of Wong Ling,
the Chinese butcher.
Everybody in the little Gj»lf port attended his
funeral —Kanakas, Malay's, aborigines. The
aborigines stole the tobacco that was placed near
O’Keefe’s head.
O’Keefe was an unprepossessing corpse. Death
sometimes casts a halo around the lifeless figures
of men, but the Man with the Scythe simply hit
O’Keefe as he passed, and called him a bad name.
Two gins walked in from the Gilbert River and
offered to make funeral noises over the body if
someone would give them all the tobacco in sight.
Jim Malone rose and said that the purity of wakes
ought to be observed. He threw out the black-
fellows, and shoo’d the gins into the street.
Ram Sinjh, the storekeeper, apologised for not
coming to the wake. He hinted that his presence
at the white man’s corroboree might interfere
with his caste rights. He forwarded a packet of
tea and some fireworks to O’Keefe’s widow. The
Asiatic mind never gets past fireworks in moments
of grief and tribulation.
A slush lamp and two candles burned at
O’Keefe’s head and feet. There was a penny in
his left hand, and Boko, the aborigine, regarded
it wistfully through the window, and bided his
time.
The undertaker and his assistant, a full-blooded
Malay, took O’Keefe’s measurements. The under-
taker measured the whisky in the big jar, and he
appeared satisfied. Other people measured it
regretfully. The whisky up North runs pretty
deep at some funerals.
At ten that night, the company got to business
with an accordeon and a Chinese fiddle. The air
was full of yells and Polynesian laughter. Chinese
Kate, the wife of Won Ling, danced a mad horn-
pipe with Jim Malone, the sheller. Tommy
Tung measured the whisky again, and passed
from the house. He put his head through the
front window a minute later and said he’d be back
with the coffin in two hours.
Won Ling remonstrated with his wife for
dancing so violently. He complained of Malone
having breathed on her. Malone said that if Won
would step outside he would guarantee another
wake in seven minutes. Won Ling looked
alarmed. Then he offered Malone a pound of tea
and some crackers to let things drop.
M‘Gee, a cousin of Malone, hit a Manila boy
because he wore a hat in the corpse’s presence.
The Manila boy pitched under the truckle bed
violently and the deceased assumed a list to star-
board.
At 3 a.m. the undertaker returned with the
coffin. The funeral started at dawn. Won Ling’s
cart led the procession. The Kanakas followed
on foot, while Chinese Kate and O’Keefe’s widow
brought up the rear.
Half-an-hour later the hearse appeared on a
sand hill ploughing its way to the new cemetery
at the back of the lighthouse. A clergyman had
come over from Port Darwin, and he stood near
the grave as the procession approached.
The Kanakas were first in the cemetery. Won
Ling limped up and complained about the scarcity
of roast pork. The spirit of O’Keefe was uneasy,
he said', but a little hot pig would put things right.
The-hearse was in difficulties in the deep sand.
The funeral party went back and pushed it from
behind. The Malay assistant seized the coffin
with both hands and ran with it to the waiting
clergyman. The face of Malone grew dark at sight
of a thin-legged Asiatic assuming the important
office of bearer. Catching the Malay by the waist-
cloth, he swung him, coffin and all, into the newly
dug grave.
The clergyman regarded the pair severely for a
moment, then scratched his ear. “Gentlemen,”
he began earnestly, “ does that coffin hold an
astral body or six feet of sanctified atmosphere?”
Malone lifted the coffin with one hand and
waved it at the distant undertaker.
“Ye, poisonous ruffian,” he said. “Where is
O’Keefe? Tell me, ye soulless blackguard, the
meanin’ av this?” And Malone shook the coffin
to emphasise its loneliness and the absence of
O’Keefe.
The undertaker eyed the empty coffin dumbly.
Then he smote his assistant twice with a large
piece of uncooked tree. “You dam M’lay fool !
What for you not put Misa O’Keefe in de blitty
coffin. You screw up de blitty lid en see notin’.
You work one week more for me en I go into te
blitty asylum !” The undertaker wrenched off
the lid, swung it like a cricket bat, and pursued
his assistant into the mangroves.
The clergyman coughed uneasily. “ I regret
that such heathenish conduct should be displayed
at the grave-side,” he said sternly. “ The deceased
in life was not a paragon of humility or politeness,
but he deserved a better funeral. 1 ’
“ He did,” sighed McGee.
“Be gob.” Malone shook his head mournfully.
A sudden shout turned all heads from the empty
coffin. At that moment the undertaker appeared
on the crest of the sand hill, bearing O’Keefe in
his arms.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
Thus an old battler :—-
It was the first time I was jugged. I was blind para-
lytic drunk, and two Johns picked me up and gently
threw me into a cell. These Johns treat you as a brother
all right. Ten minutes later two other Johns brought in
a mad German and chained him to the wall. My oath,
he did perform until sweet oblivion overtook him, as the
poets would say. A little later some more Johns brought
in another drunk, who fell asleep without a word. In
the morning the third man woke up, and looked round
him in a sort of puzzled way, and asked me what he was
there for. “ Murder,” says I, solemnly. “ And what are
you here for?” says he. “ I’m here as a witness,” says I.
“Yes,” says the German, “and I’m chained here as
another witness.” We got the poor bloke going, and
spun him a yarn full of blood and murder and dead Dagos
until he began feeling at his throat for the rope. Just
then a cobber bailed him out, and he left wondering
why somebody didn’t come and bail the witnesses out.
Anyhow, I had a feed with one-and-a-sprat I found I had
after he left. I think someone must have put it in my
pocket while I was asleep.

The Parlor and the Bar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHERE the hardened drinkers cluster in
the corner of the bar,
Where the Once Had Beens are drink-
ing to the health of Men Who Are,
Wlrnre the man who has the money gets the
of a king,
And the stony-broke is hustled and regarded as a
“thing”—
I can see him sitting nightly ever ready for the
call,
With a pewter pot before him and his back
against the "wall.
I can see him from the parlor—where you pay a
penny more
For the liquor, and you don’t expectorate upon
the floor,
Where a blazing fire is burning when the nights
are bleak and cold,
And a siren sings in chorus to the jingle of the
gold 1
And from dreams of wine-sweet kisses I in fancy
wandered far
As I pondered o’er the difference ’twixt the parlor
and the bar.
Night shall have no dread to-morrow, as you roll
your cigarette.
Dawn will bring no crown of sorrow —you are
flush and out of debt.
“Dark eyes flashing from the sofa, come and clink
your glass with mine.
So! I love you ! and your lips are sweeter than
your ruddy wine.
I will gem these slender fingers that more soft
than snowflakes are.
Hear that laughter ! What’s the matter with the
drunkards in the bar ? ”
These are nights of wanton pleasure. Lift the
chalice high and drink !
Drink and gamble ; think at leisure —if you have
the time to think.
Friends in plenty gather round you ; spend your
money while you may ;
White and tender arms have bound you—soon
she’ll take those arms away.
Drink and revel with the devil ; you will see her
as a star
When across the blinded threshold you have
stumbled to the bar !
Where the hardened drinkers cluster, and the
pewter-pots are deep,
I can see him sitting nightly getting drunk upon
the cheap !
And his ancient hat is battered and his whiskers
wild and grey,
And the old frock-coat upon his back has seen a
better day;
And the way he sometimes glances thro’ the cur-
tained door ajar
Makes me think he knows the difference ’twixt
the parlor and the bar !

C. LOUIS RANDALL.
A Whirlwind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
UNDAY at Brulong. All was quiet. Few
of the residents were out of doors, for the
heat was intense. For once the wind was
resting, and the red dust lay undisturbed on the
plains.
At the navvy camp on the. railway job also an
unusual quiet prevailed. The week’s washing was
drying in the sun, and the toilers, in clean flannels
and moles, were lolling in their tents.
Dinan, the working ganger, was lying on the
bunk in his 6xB in a semi-conscious condition,
trying to piece together the doings of the previous
day and night. Friday had been pay-day, and
the intervening time was but a blurr in Dinan’s
memory.
He remembered that he had had a row with
Graham, and had left his boarding-house for a
calico home midst the other workers. That was
clear enough, but what of his month’s pay ? Had
he knocked it all down, or had he been robbed ?
His pockets were empty, anyhow.
Dinan engaged in much bitter self-reproach.
Then he thought some more.
“I paid Mrs. Graham f’r me board,” he said,
“an’ didn’t Oi sock a fiver in th’ behind pocket
av mo best pants ? Av coorse Oi did, an’ put th’
pants under me piller whin Oi come back.”
He felt under his pillow: The pants were there,
folded up, right enough. He felt much relieved.
“It ain’t as bad as I thought, ennyliow,” he told
himself, and went to sleep again.
Out on the plain beyond the township a tiny
cloud of dust had arisen, and now it began to
move rapidly. Gathering strength and increasing
in speed as it came, it caught into its whirling
embrace all things movable that lay in its path.
Through Brulong’s only street it rushed a twirling
mass of dust and debris reaching now many feet
into the air. Doors were closed in haste, and
windows shut down, whilst children and live stock
scudded for shelter. Then the whirlwind gave up
Brulong as a place of no further interest, and
roared away in the direction of the navvy camp.
Dinan’s awakening was a sudden one. He
thought the earth had arisen and assaulted his
massive person. Ere he could think of enough
swear words to express his feelings the ridge-pole
of the tent smote him violently across his intel-
lect. Then he felt his home was leaving him, and
he held on manfully. He was caught up bodily
holding on to his tent, and whirled through space.
All around him was a thick brown darkness, as of
the infernal regions. His nose, eyes, and mouth
were choked with dust. Dinan tried to pray as
he flew. He thought the last trump had sounded,
and that he was being transported to the place of
torment. Around him he could hear many navvy
anathemas, and felt better for the company.
Presently he again struck mother earth with
violence; and then there was light. The whirl-
wind had dropped him and was careering gaily
away towards the Dai'ling. Dinan sat and gazed
after it, speechless. Then a thought struck him,
and he leaped to his feet and gasped, “Me pants ! ”
High up, at the very apex of the whirlwind,
rode a pair of pants, filled out as if enclosing some
ghostly form.
They were Dinan’s. Even at that distance he
recognised them.
“ I’ll have thim or bust,” he vowed.
Hatless and barefooted, he set out in pursuit of
his trousers. The hot earth blistered his feet.
The fierce sun burnt his unprotected scalp, but he
faltered not. Many articles of clothing were
strewn over the plain, having fallen from the
column of whirling dust, and many more kept
falling; but Dinan’s nether garments still fled on-
wards and turned somersaults in the sky, and left
their angered owner far behind!
In the distance could be seen a belt of dead
timber, which stood close to the Government dam.
Towards this struggled Dinan in the track of the
whirlwind, but ere he reached it the dusty dis-
turbance had vanished.
Throwing himself down in the mud at the edge
of the tank, Dinan drank deeply of the dirty
water. The crows up in the dead trees cawed as
if in mockery, and he gazed aloft and cast ve-
hement curses at the birds. As he looked up his
bloodshot eyes discovered something caught high
on the limb of a big box-tree. ’Twas the wreck of
his Sunday trousers. In desperation, he began
throwing sticks and lumps of clay up at the cloth-
ing. All to no purpose ; it was out of range.
Then, in a state bordering on insanity, Dinan
addressed the wide world. His language started
several more whirlwinds going, scared the crows
into flight, knocked a big goanna off a tree into
the water, but still the pants hung tight.
The rumble of wheels checked the flow of lurid
language. Graham of the boarding house, with
one of his boys, had arrived at the dam for water
with two casks in the dray.
“What th’ deuce are y’ doin’ here?” said
Graham. “ Y’ll have t’ give up the beer.”
“ Beer mefut! ” shouted Dinan. “ It’s not that;
’tis th’ whir-rly wind, man.”
“What ! Y’ don’t mean t’ tell me y’ was carried
away be th’ whirlwind. That licks ”
“ No, ye ox, not me, me pants. Luk at ’em oop
th’ tree. Oi’ve chased thim two moiles an’ bad
luck to it, Oi’ll have t’ be after failin’ th’ tree to git
thim.”
“ Y’ gettin’ mighty mean, Dinan,” said Graham.
“ Why, tli’ things ain’t worth th’ trouble of runnin’
after.”
“Me good man, all mo wor-rldly wealth is in
the pocket av thim pants.” Dinan almost wept.
“ Why don’t y’ climb up ? ” said Graham.
“ Is ut a abor-riginal black fellow ye be after
talcin’ me f’r ? C’ud ye cloimb ut, think ye ? ”
“ It’s easy.”
“ Get me thim pants an* o’ill give ye haf a quid.
There y’ are,” said Dinan.
Graham descended from the dray armed with
a tomahawk. Waving off Graham, junior, who
wished to undertake the job, he started the ascent.
Though the tree was high and very hard, Graham
arrived at last. By holding on to a limb above
and walking out on the lower one to the extreme
end he managed to reach and detach the
trousers, which he took between his teeth. Then
he began the return journey. But his luck was
out. With a crack the lower limb broke off,
leaving Graham suspended by his arms in mid-air.
In vain did he try to work his way along by his
hands. He could not manage it.
“Stand fr’m under,” he shouted as loud as he
could, with his mouth full of pants. “I’ll have to
drop.”
“Howld on a minit,” yelled Dinan. “Wait
till Oi giv’ th’ wor-rd.” Graham hung on pa-
tiently.
Running to the horse’s head, Dinan backed th e
cart under the tree. Then seizing the tarpaulin
f ro m off the casks, he secured a corner to each
side of the back of the vehicle. Taking one
corner, he gave the other to young Graham, and
they pulled it tight under the suspended boarding-
house-keeper.
“Now thin, let go,” yelled Dinan. “Whoa;
stand stiddy, ye divil.”
Graham fell .with extreme velocity. As he
struck the tarpaulin the old mare bolted. Dinan
was pulled off his feet, and landed heavily on
top of Graham, with whom he instinctively got
into holts, and they were dragged some distance
across country before they fell off the tarpaulin.
Why didn’t ye luk afore ye jumped, yeyong
idiot?” gasped Dinan. “ Oi’ve a momd to ——
“Lemme up, y’ lunatic, or I’ll murder y,
hissed Graham,
Give me me pants, thin.
Graham wrenched himself free and threw the
cause of all the trouble at the owner,
Oman searched the pockets. His red face we it
wb Jte and he trembled. , ,
Graham, he said huskily, yr a dirthy

ARTHUR WRIGHT.
Professor W. E. Addis, of Oxford, Eng-
land, has pretty well boxed the compass in tiie
matter of religions. He was the son of aPresby-
terian minister at Edinburgh. He became a
Roman Catholic priest. Next he was assistant
to Dr. Strong in the latitudinarian Australian
Church, Melbourne. On returning to the Cold
Country in ’92, he joined'the Unitarians, and the
other day he. entered the wide door of the Church
of England. He is still a comparatively young
man, and may expect to hear his threepenny re-
bound in the collection-plate of many more sects
before he dies.
